Partial Usurpation of a Sold Commodity: Fungibles vs. Non-Fungibles
Ruling on buyer's right to return when part of sold item is usurped after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fungible (mithli) and non-fungible (qimi) goods in the context of partial usurpation and state the buyer's right to return the remainder.

Core Idea
Partial usurpation of a fungible gives no right to return; for a non-fungible, it does because partiality is a defect.

When part of a sold item is taken by usurpation after delivery, the buyer may wonder if they can return the remaining part. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes based on the nature of the good: for measurable or weighable goods (fungibles), no choice; for unique items like cloth, slave, or house, the buyer has the option to return. This lesson explains the reasoning and the key factors that determine the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of goods
The text mentions 'measurable or weighable' (كيلي أو وزني) for fungibles and 'cloth' (ثوب) representing non-fungibles. Recognize that the key distinction is between mithli (fungible) and qimi (non-fungible).
Fungibles can be replaced by similar items; non-fungibles have unique value.
2
Understand the ruling for fungibles
For fungibles, partial usurpation does not give the buyer a choice to return the remainder because partnership (شركة) in such goods is not considered a defect; partiality does not harm.
The reason: 'الاستحقاق لا يمنع تمام الصفقة' (the usurpation does not prevent completion of the transaction).
3
Understand the ruling for non-fungibles
For a cloth or any qimi (slave, house), partial usurpation is a defect because it harms the buyer, so he may return the remainder.
The text explicitly says 'بخلاف المثلي' (unlike fungibles).
4
Recognize the timing factor
The ruling above applies after possession (بعد القبض). If the usurpation occurs before possession, the buyer has the choice to return or keep the remainder regardless of the good type, because the transaction is not fully complete.
The last sentence: 'فإن كان الاستحقاق قبل القبض، فله أن يرد ما بقي أو أمسكه'.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
Given a scenario, first classify the good as mithli or qimi, then determine if the usurpation occurred before or after possession, and state the buyer's rights accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يخير: أي إذا استحق بعض الكيلي أو الوزني لم يخير المشتري في رد ما بقي؛ لأن الشركة فيه لا تعد عيباً؛ لأنه لا يضره التبعيض، والاستحقاق لا يمنع تمام الصفقة؛ لأن تمامها برضا العاقد لا المالك المستحق. (عيني، فتح) ولو ثوباً خير أي لو كان المبيع ثوباً فاستحق بعضه خير المشتري في رد ما بقي؛ لأن التبعيض في الثوب عيب فإنه يضره، بخلاف المثلي، والاستحقاق ليس بعيب حادث عند المشتري لوجوده وقت البيع، وإنما تأخر ظهوره، وأراد بالثوب القيمي، فعم العبد والدار، هذا إذا كان بعد القبض، فإن كان الاستحقاق قبل القبض، فله أن يرد ما بقي أو أمسكه؛ لتفرق الصفقة على المشتري قبل التمام. (عيني، فتح)
He is not given a choice: i.e., if part of a measurable or weighable item is usurped, the buyer is not given the choice to return the remainder, because partnership in it is not considered a defect, for partiality does not harm him, and usurpation does not prevent completion of the transaction, because its completion is by consent of the contractor, not the rightful owner. ('Ayni, Fath) 'And if it is a cloth, he is given choice': i.e., if the sold item is a cloth and part of it is usurped, the buyer is given the choice to return the remainder, because partiality in a cloth is a defect since it harms him, unlike fungibles. And the usurpation is not a defect arising at the buyer's side because it existed at the time of sale, but its appearance was delayed. By 'cloth' he means a non-fungible (qīmī), which includes a slave and a house. This applies after possession. If the usurpation occurs before possession, he may return what remains or keep it, due to splitting of the transaction on the buyer before completion. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between fungible goods (mamlūk bi al-mithl) and specific goods (qīmī) when part is usurped after sale and delivery. For measurable or weighable (fungible) goods, if part is usurped, the buyer cannot return the remainder because the partial loss is not considered a defect that harms him, and the transaction is complete by mutual consent regardless of the usurper. However, for a cloth (or any qīmī item like a slave or house), partial usurpation is a defect because it directly harms the value, so the buyer has the option to return the remainder. The distinction is based on the nature of the goods: fungibles are replaceable by similar, so partial loss is compensated by a similar amount; non-fungibles have unique value, and partial loss reduces utility. If the usurpation occurs before possession, the buyer has the option to return what remains because the transaction has not been completed.

Key Terms
مثلي (mithlī): Fungible goods that can be replaced by similar items.
قيمي (qīmī): Non-fungible goods with unique value, like cloth, slave, house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a specific hand-written book (qīmī) and after delivery, a section of it is usurped. What is the ruling?
A) The buyer cannot return the remainder
B) The buyer may return the remainder
C) The buyer must accept a price reduction
D) The buyer has no options

Key Takeaway
For fungible goods, partial usurpation gives no right to return; for non-fungibles, it does because partiality is a defect.

Actions Implying Acceptance of a Known Defect
Ruling when buyer's actions indicate acceptance of a known defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions after knowledge of a defect imply acceptance and identify the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
Using an item after knowing its defect forfeits the right to return it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common situation in sales: when a buyer discovers a defect but then uses the item anyway. The Hanafi school holds that such use indicates acceptance and waives the right to return. However, there is an important exception for actions that are truly necessary, like riding an animal to water it when no alternative exists. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
If a buyer discovers a defect and then performs any act of ownership (wearing, riding, leasing, etc.), that act constitutes acceptance (riḍā) and forfeits the right to return.
The rationale is that such actions indicate the buyer's desire to keep the item.
2
Identify the listed actions
The text enumerates: wearing a cloth, riding an animal, leasing, pawning, writing a mukatabah, offering for sale, dwelling, and general use. These are all examples of using the item as an owner would.
The common element is that the action is inconsistent with the intent to return.
3
Recognize the exception
Riding for the specific purpose of watering the animal is not acceptance if it is necessary (e.g., the animal is stubborn, or the buyer cannot walk). Also, riding to return the item is not acceptance.
The exception covers actions that are unavoidable or directly related to returning.
4
Distinguish between purposes
Riding for return is never acceptance. Riding for other purposes (e.g., going to market) is acceptance unless compelled by necessity. Carrying fodder for another animal while riding is always acceptance.
The key is the intention behind the action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللبس : [ أي لبس الثوب بعد اطلاعه على العيب عيني)] وكذا الإجارة والرهن والكتابة والعرض على البيع والسكنى والاستخدام، يعني إذا اشترى ثوباً أو دابة، ثم اطلع على عيب فلبس الثوب أو ركب الدابة أو داواها، فهو رضا بالعيب؛ لأنه دليل استبقائه، وقوله: "لا الركوب للسقي إلخ" أي لا تكون هذه الأشياء رضاً وهو محمول على ما إذا كان لا يجد بداً منه، إما لصعوبتها، أو لكونها جموحاً لا ذلولاً، أو لعجزه عن المشي، أو لكون العلف في عدل؛ إذ لا يستقر عليها، وفي عدلين يستقر أو أمكنه السقي في منزله أو قريباً منه من غير ركوب، وركب فهو رضا، وقيل: الركوب للرد لا يكون رضاً كيف ما كان، ولغيره يكون رضاً إلا الضرورة، ولو حمل علف دابة أخرى ركبها أو لم يركبها، فهو رضاً. (عيني، فتح)
And wearing: [i.e., wearing the cloth after discovering the defect. ('Ayni)] Similarly, leasing, pawning, writing (mukatabah), offering for sale, dwelling, and using. Meaning, if someone buys a cloth or an animal, then discovers a defect and wears the cloth or rides the animal or treats it medically, it is considered acceptance of the defect, because it indicates his desire to keep it. And his statement: 'not riding for watering, etc.' means these actions are not considered acceptance, and this is interpreted as when he has no alternative, either due to its stubbornness, or it being uncontrollable and not tame, or his inability to walk, or the fodder being on the animal's back; as it cannot be stabilized on it, and with two loads it stabilizes, or if he could water it at home or nearby without riding, but he rides, then it is acceptance. It is said: riding for the purpose of returning is not acceptance in any case, but for other purposes it is acceptance except necessity. If he carries fodder for another animal while riding it or not, it is acceptance.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains that after discovering a defect, certain actions by the buyer indicate acceptance (riḍā) and forfeit the right to return the item. Examples include wearing a cloth, riding an animal, treating it medically, leasing it, pawning it, writing a mukatabah (contract of manumission for a slave), offering it for sale, dwelling in a house, or using it generally. The rationale is that these actions demonstrate the buyer's intention to keep the item. However, there is an exception: riding for the specific purpose of watering the animal is not considered acceptance if it is necessary (e.g., the animal is stubborn or the owner cannot walk). The ruling distinguishes between riding for return (which is not acceptance) and other riding (which is acceptance unless forced by necessity). Carrying fodder for another animal while riding is considered acceptance.

Key Terms
رضا بالعيب (riḍā bi-l-ʿayb): Acceptance of the defect, which invalidates the option of return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer discovers a defect in a newly purchased horse but rides it to the water source because the horse is thirsty and there is no other way. Does this riding forfeit the right to return?
A) Yes, because riding indicates acceptance
B) No, because it was necessary watering
C) Only if the ride was for more than one mile
D) It forfeits the right only if the horse was also fed

Key Takeaway
Use of an item after knowing a defect implies acceptance, but necessary actions (watering, returning) do not.

Returning Only the Defective Item from a Pair
Ruling on khiyar al-'ayb with intra-madhhab dispute (Zufar).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a defect is found in one of two purchased items, explain the basis for permitting a partial return after delivery, and distinguish this from khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya.

Core Idea
After delivery, returning only the defective item is allowed; splitting a completed transaction is permissible.

When you buy two items together and later discover a defect in one, the question arises: must you return both, or can you keep the good and return only the defective? The Hanafi school gives you the option to return just the defective one, provided the transaction is completed by delivery. This ruling balances fairness to the buyer with recognition of the seller's deception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage addresses khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) when two items are sold together and one is defective. What options does the buyer have?
2
State the Hanafi position
The matn rules: 'رد المعيب فقط' — the buyer returns only the defective item. This is the relied-upon view.
3
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that returning only one splits the transaction (tafriq al-safqah) and harms the seller, because custom pairs good with bad. He analogizes to khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya.
Zufar is a Hanafi scholar whose view is recorded but not mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The transaction is completed by delivery (qabd). A defect does not prevent completion, unlike options that suspend consent. The seller's harm is due to his own deception, so it is not considered against the buyer.
The key distinction: completion versus suspension of the contract.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under the Hanafi ruling, the buyer keeps the good item and returns only the defective; under Zufar, the buyer must return both or keep both (and possibly seek arsh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم وجد بأحدهما عيباً ردّ المعيب فقط. [بحصة وحده لجواز التفريق بعد التمام. (ط)] وقال زفر: له ردهما، وليس له أن یرد وحده؛ لأن فيه تفريق الصفقة، والبائع يتضرر به؛ لأن العادة جرت بضم الجيد إلى الردي، فأشبه ما قبل القبض وخيار الشرط والرؤية قلنا إنه تفريق الصفقة بعد التمام؛ لأن الصفقة تتم بالقبض؛ لأن العيب لا يمنع تمام الصفقة، بخلاف خيار الشرط والرؤية؛ لأن الصفقة لا تتم معهما، وإن كان بعد القبض؛ لعدم تمام الرضا، وتضرر البائع من قبل تدليسه فلا يعتبر في حق المشتري. (عيني، فتح)
Then if a defect is found in one of them, he returns only the defective one. [With his own share due to permissibility of splitting after completion. (Tahawi)] Zufar said: He may return both, and he may not return only one, because that splits the transaction, and the seller is harmed by it, for custom has joined the good with the bad, so it resembles before delivery and the option of condition and seeing. We said: It is splitting the transaction after completion, because the transaction is completed by delivery, for the defect does not prevent completion of the transaction, unlike the option of condition and seeing, because the transaction is not completed with them, even if it is after delivery, due to incomplete consent, and the seller's harm is due to his own deception, so it is not considered against the buyer. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if a person buys two items (e.g., two slaves) in one contract, and later finds a defect in one of them, he may only return the defective one, not both. The commentator (Tahawi) notes that this is because splitting the transaction is permissible after it has been completed. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, disagrees: he says the buyer must return both, because returning only one splits the contract and harms the seller, as custom mixes good and bad together. The Hanafi response is that the contract is completed by delivery (qabd), and the defect does not prevent completion; thus splitting after completion is allowed. This differs from the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) or option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya), where the contract is not yet completed, so splitting is not allowed. The seller's harm is due to his own deception (for selling a defective item), so it is not considered in favor of the buyer.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return a defective item after purchase due to an existing defect.
صفقة (safqah): A transaction or sale contract involving one or more items.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting a single transaction into parts, e.g., returning only one item from a pair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, if a buyer purchases two slaves and later finds a defect in one, what is his option?
A) He must return both slaves.
B) He may return only the defective slave.
C) He may return only the non-defective slave.
D) He has no right to return any.

Key Takeaway
After delivery, the buyer may return only the defective item from a pair sold together, because the contract is complete and splitting is allowed.

Defect in Staple Goods: The All-or-Nothing Rule
Ruling on khiyar al-'ayb for fungible goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning defective goods sold by measure or weight and explain why the buyer cannot split the lot.

Core Idea
If a defect appears in part of a mikyal or mawzun lot, the buyer must take all or leave all.

When you buy a bulk commodity like wheat or dates, each individual unit is not economically significant on its own. The Hanafi school treats such fungible goods as a single legal entity. This lesson explores the ruling when only part of the lot is defective and the buyer's limited options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the first sentence: 'If a defect is found in part of what is sold by measure or weight, the buyer returns all of it or takes it.' Note that the buyer has two choices: return the entire lot or keep it (with compensation for the defect). He cannot return only the defective part.
This is a strict rule: no splitting.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
The text explains why: because goods sold by measure or weight of the same kind are considered as one thing legally (حكماً) and in estimation (تقديراً), even if they are many in reality. The value lies in the aggregate; a single grain has negligible value and cannot be sold alone.
The reasoning is based on the nature of mithli (fungible) goods.
3
Examine the Scope of the Ruling
The ruling applies whether the goods are in one container or multiple containers, and whether the defect is discovered before or after delivery. This shows the generality of the rule.
The text explicitly states 'سواء كان الكل في وعاء واحد أو في أوعية'.
4
Note the Alternative Opinion
The text mentions an opinion (قيل) that differentiates based on containers: if the goods are in two separate containers, they are like two separate items (like two slaves), and the buyer may return only the container with the defect.
This opinion is not the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد ببعض الكيلى أو الوزني عيبا ردّ المشتري كله أو أخذه. وليس له أن يردّ المعيب خاصة ويأخذ البعض، سواء كان الكل في وعاء واحد أو في أوعية، وسواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده؛ لأن المكيل والموزون إذا كان من جنس واحد كشيء واحد حكماً وتقديراً، وإن كان أشياء حقيقة؛ لأن المالية والتقوم في المكيلات والموزونات باعتبار الانضمام والاجتماع؛ إذ الحبة الواحدة ليست بمتقومة حتى لا يجوز بيعها، وقيل: هذا إذا كان الكل في وعاء واحد، وإذا كان في وعائين يكون بمنزلة العبدين حتى يرد الوعاء الذي فيه وجد العيب. (عيني، فتح)
If a defect is found in part of what is sold by measure or weight, the buyer returns all of it or takes it. And he may not return only the defective part and keep the rest, whether the whole is in one container or in many containers, and whether before or after delivery; because goods sold by measure or weight, if of one kind, are considered as one thing judicially and in estimation, even if they are multiple things in reality, because value and property value in measured and weighed goods are based on joining and aggregation, since a single grain is not valuable such that its sale would be permissible. It is said: This is when the whole is in one container; if in two containers, it is like two slaves, so he returns the container in which the defect was found. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage continues the matn on khiyar al-'ayb. For goods sold by volume (mikyal) or weight (mawzun), if a defect appears in part of the lot, the buyer has two options: return the entire lot or keep it (demanding compensation for the defect). He cannot return only the defective portion and keep the rest. This applies whether the goods are in one container or multiple, and before or after delivery. The reasoning is that such goods (e.g., wheat, barley) of the same kind are legally considered a single entity, since individual units have negligible value. However, there is a divergent opinion: if the goods are in two separate containers, they are treated like two separate items (like two slaves), so the buyer may return only the container with the defect.

Key Terms
مكيل (mikyal): Goods sold by volume measurement (e.g., grain).
موزون (mawzun): Goods sold by weight (e.g., dates, metal).
كشيء واحد حكماً (ka-shay' wahid hukman): Treated as a single entity by legal consideration, even if physically multiple.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling when a defect is found in part of goods sold by weight (mawzun) that are all of the same kind?
A) The buyer may return only the defective portion.
B) The buyer may return the entire lot or keep it, but cannot split.
C) The buyer may return the defective portion only if the goods are in separate containers.
D) The buyer has no option to return; the sale is final.

Key Takeaway
For mikyal and mawzun goods of the same kind, the buyer faced with a defect in part must either return the entire lot or keep it; splitting is not permitted.

Actions Indicating Acceptance of a Defect
Ruling with proof based on talil (reasoning).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions that indicate acceptance of a defect (forfeiting the right to return) and actions that do not, with specific focus on riding for necessity.

Core Idea
Necessary riding does not forfeit the right of return; only voluntary use implies acceptance.

After discovering a defect, the buyer has the right to return the item. However, certain actions by the buyer are legally interpreted as acceptance of the defect, thereby waiving that right. This lesson clarifies which actions constitute acceptance and which do not, especially regarding riding an animal for necessary purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text states that wearing, leasing, pledging, offering for sale, dwelling, using, riding, and medicating after discovery of a defect all indicate acceptance, forfeiting the option to return.
The rationale is that these actions show the buyer’s intent to keep the item.
2
Recognize the exception for riding
Riding for watering, returning the animal, or buying fodder does not indicate acceptance, because the buyer has no alternative.
The exception is based on necessity.
3
Understand the conditions for the exception
The text elaborates that the exception applies only when the buyer cannot avoid riding. If the animal is unruly, the buyer cannot walk, or fodder is in a single saddlebag, riding is not acceptance. If the buyer can water without riding but rides anyway, it is acceptance.
The exception is narrow and must be interpreted strictly.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi authorities hold that riding for the purpose of returning the item is never acceptance; others say it is acceptance unless there is necessity. The text leans towards the latter view.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللبس : [ أي لبس الثوب بعد اطلاعه على العيب عيني)] وكذا الإجارة والرهن والكتابة والعرض على البيع والسكنى والاستخدام، يعني إذا اشترى ثوباً أو دابة، ثم اطلع على عيب فلبس الثوب أو ركب الدابة أو داواها، فهو رضا بالعيب؛ لأنه دليل استبقائه، وقوله: "لا الركوب للسقي إلخ" أي لا تكون هذه الأشياء رضاً وهو محمول على ما إذا كان لا يجد بداً منه، إما لصعوبتها، أو لكونها جموحاً لا ذلولاً، أو لعجزه عن المشي، أو لكون العلف في عدل؛ إذ لا يستقر عليها، وفي عدلين يستقر أو أمكنه السقي في منزله أو قريباً منه من غير ركوب، وركب فهو رضا، وقيل: الركوب للرد لا يكون رضاً كيف ما كان، ولغيره يكون رضاً إلا الضرورة، ولو حمل علف دابة أخرى ركبها أو لم يركبها، فهو رضاً. (عيني، فتح) ثم قال: والركوب والمداواة رضاً بالعيب لا الرّكوبُ للسقى أو للرّد أو لشراء العلف، ولو لأنه دليل إبقائه وإمساكه للدابة لاحتياج إليه
Wearing: [meaning wearing the garment after learning of the defect ('Ayni)] and similarly leasing, pledging, writing (or being written upon), offering for sale, dwelling, and using. That is, if someone buys a garment or an animal, then learns of a defect and wears the garment, or rides the animal, or treats it medically, that is acceptance of the defect, because it is evidence of preserving it. And his statement: 'Not riding for watering, etc.' meaning that these actions are not acceptance, and it is interpreted as when he has no alternative, either because of its restiveness, or because it is not tame, or because he cannot walk, or because the fodder is in a saddlebag, so he does not settle on it; if there are two saddlebags it settles, or he can water at home or nearby without riding, but he rides, then it is acceptance. It is said: riding to return is never acceptance, while riding for other purposes is acceptance except necessity. And if he carries fodder for another animal while riding it or not, it is acceptance. ('Ayni, Fath) Then he said: Riding and medical treatment are acceptance of the defect; not riding for watering, or for returning, or for buying fodder, and so on, because it is evidence of preserving and retaining the animal due to need.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses actions by the buyer after discovering a defect that indicate his acceptance (rida) of the defective item, thereby forfeiting his right to return it. Wearing a garment after learning of the defect is acceptance. Similarly, leasing, pledging, writing (an iqala?), offering for sale, dwelling in a house, or using an animal all indicate acceptance. However, riding an animal for necessary purposes like watering, returning it to the seller, or buying fodder does not indicate acceptance, because the buyer has no alternative. The commentator ('Ayni) elaborates conditions: if the animal is unruly, if the rider is unable to walk, or if fodder is in a saddlebag that requires riding to fetch, then the riding is not acceptance. There is a view that riding specifically for the purpose of returning the item is never acceptance, while riding for other purposes is acceptance unless under necessity. The matn explicitly states: riding and medical treatment are acceptance, but riding for watering, returning, or buying fodder are not.

Key Terms
رضا بالعيب (rida bil-'ayb): Acceptance of the defect, forfeiting the option to return.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return a defective item upon discovery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions does NOT indicate acceptance of a defect in a purchased animal?
A) Riding it to water it.
B) Selling it to another person.
C) Treating it with medicine.
D) Using it for plowing.

Key Takeaway
Necessity negates the indication of acceptance for riding; voluntary use implies acceptance.

Defect from Pre-Existing Cause: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect from pre-existing cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding defects arising from pre-existing causes at the seller and state the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A defect caused by a pre-existing condition at the seller: Abu Hanifa allows full return; Sahibayn only partial compensation.

When a sold item develops a defect after delivery due to a cause that existed with the seller, the buyer's remedy depends on attribution. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the issue: does the buyer return the item for a full refund, or only claim compensation for the diminished value? We'll analyze the proofs of each position and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the key elements: a slave committed theft before sale, buyer unaware, hand amputated after delivery due to that theft.
The cause (theft) existed with the seller.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa: buyer can return slave and get full refund, or keep and get full refund. Sahibayn: buyer cannot return, only claims difference in value between thief and non-thief.
This is a classic khilaf within the school.
3
Analyze the proof of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa attributes the deficiency to the seller because the cause existed with him. He uses an analogy: like usurped property damaged after return due to a crime pre-existing with the usurper.
Dalil: cause leads to existence, so loss is linked to the cause.
4
Analyze the proof of Sahibayn
They argue the slave still has value; the contract is valid but the item became defective. The buyer should only receive compensation for the defect, not full refund.
They emphasize that the cause does not negate property value entirely.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, buyer gets full refund; under Sahibayn, only partial compensation (difference between value with and without defect).
The mufta bihi is often Sahibayn's view in later practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع، ردّه واسترة الثمن، ولو برئ من كل عيب صح، وإن لم يُسمّ الكل فلا يرد بعيب. ثم قال: قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع رده واسترد الثمن. قال: أي لو اشترى عبدا قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم المشتري به عند البيع ولا عند القبض فقطع يده في يد المشتري بتلك السرقة يرده ويسترد الثمن عند أبي حنيفة أو أمسكه ورجع الثمن، وقالا : لا يرده ولكن يرجع بحصة النقصان من الثمن؛ لأن الموجود في يد البائع سبب القطع، وهو لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه تعيب، فيرجع بنقصانه وهو فرق ما بين قيمتي العبد بأن يقوم سارقا وغير سارق، فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما من الثمن؛ لتعذر الرد. وله: أن سبب الوجوب وجد في يد البائع، والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود، فيضاف الوجود إلى السبب السابق، فصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل العبد بسبب وجد في يد البائع من قتل نفس أو قطع طريق أو ردة فعنده يرجع بكل الثمن، وعندهما بفضل ما بين كونه مباح الدم ومعصومه رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/٢] (فتح)
The cutting off of a possessed item due to a cause at the seller: he returns it and takes back the price. And if he is exonerated from every defect, it is valid, even if he does not name all, then he does not return due to a defect. Then he said: The cutting off of a possessed item due to a cause at the seller: he returns it and takes back the price. He said: For example, if he bought a slave that had stolen at the seller's, and the buyer did not know at the time of sale nor at the time of delivery, and then his hand is cut off while in the buyer's possession due to that theft; he returns him and takes back the full price according to Abu Hanifa, or he keeps him and takes back the price. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he does not return him but claims compensation for the diminution of the price, because the cause existed with the seller, and it does not negate property value, so the contract is executed but the item became defective, so he claims the difference between his value as a thief and non-thief. According to him (Abu Hanifa): because the cause of obligation existed with the seller, and obligation leads to existence, so the existence is attributed to the prior cause, just as if the usurped property is killed or cut off after return due to a crime found with the usurper. The same difference applies if the slave is killed due to a cause at the seller (murder, highway robbery, apostasy): according to him he gets the full price, according to them the difference between being blood-permissible and inviolable. (Fath, referencing Ramz al-Haqa'iq)
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when a defect arises from a pre-existing cause at the seller. Example: a slave committed theft before sale, the buyer does not know, and after delivery the slave's hand is amputated as punishment. According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer may return the slave and get full refund, or keep him and get full refund. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) say he cannot return the slave because the cause existed at the seller but the slave still has value; he can only claim the difference in value between a thief-slave and a non-thief. Abu Hanifa argues that the cause of amputation (theft) existed with the seller, so the resulting loss is attributed to that cause, analogous to usurped property damaged after return due to a pre-existing crime. The same applies if the slave is executed for murder, robbery, or apostasy: Abu Hanifa gives full refund; the Sahibayn give only compensation for the defect (difference in value).

Key Terms
سبب عند البائع (sabab 'ind al-ba'i'): A cause for a defect that existed with the seller before sale.
حصة النقصان (hissat al-nuqsan): The amount of compensation equal to the difference in value due to the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a slave's hand is amputated after delivery due to a theft that occurred before sale, what is the buyer's remedy?
A) He may return the slave and get full refund.
B) He may only claim compensation for the difference in value.
C) He has no remedy because the amputation occurred after delivery.
D) He must keep the slave and cannot claim anything.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa allows full refund when a defect arises from a pre-existing seller's cause; Sahibayn allow only partial compensation for the reduction in value.

Validity of Blanket Immunity from All Defects in Sale
Ruling on seller's stipulation of immunity from all defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blanket immunity from defects, explain why it is valid despite ignorance, and distinguish the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the scope of immunity.

Core Idea
Blanket immunity from defects is valid in Hanafi law because ignorance that does not cause dispute is tolerated.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq addresses a seller's stipulation that he is free from all defects ('bara'ah min kull 'ayb'). The Hanafi school permits such a blanket immunity even without enumerating the defects, while the Shafi'i school requires enumeration. Within the Hanafi school, there is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether the immunity covers defects arising after contract but before delivery. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows Abu Yusuf, including both existing and future defects up to delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and Hanafi ruling
The central issue is whether a seller can stipulate immunity from all defects without specifying them. The Hanafi ruling is that both the sale and the immunity clause are valid (sahih).
The key phrase is 'صح البيع والإبراء'.
2
Compare with opposing positions
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad require enumeration because they consider immunity from unknown rights invalid. Zufar (a Hanafi) holds the sale valid but the condition void if defects are unknown.
The opposition is based on the principle that transferring unknown rights is invalid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the ignorance (jahalah) in this case does not lead to dispute (la tufdi ila al-munaza'ah), so it does not impede validity.
The ultimate aim is to bind the contract (al-gharad ilzam al-'aqd).
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on scope
Abu Yusuf holds that immunity includes defects existing at contract and those arising before delivery. Muhammad limits it to existing defects only, arguing that exoneration only applies to what is established.
The disagreement hinges on whether the seller's aim of binding the contract requires covering future defects.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The commentaries (Fath, 'Ayni) prefer Abu Yusuf's view, stating 'the aim is to bind the contract', so immunity includes defects arising after contract but before delivery.
As a result, the buyer cannot return the item for any defect covered by the immunity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو برئ من كل عيب صح. كل عيب بأن باع عبدا واشترط أنه بريء من كل عيب. (عيني) صح إلخ: أي لو قال البائع: بعتك هذا العبد على أنني بريء من كل عيب صح البيع والإبراء وإن لم يسم جميع العيوب، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح إلا أن يعدد العيوب كلها؛ لأن الإبراء عن الحقوق المجهولة لا يصح عنده؛ لأنه في الإبراء معنى التمليك حتى يرتد بالرد، ولا يصح تعليقه بالشرط، وتمليك المجهول لا يصح كبيعه، وبه قال أحمد، ونحن نقول: هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا تمنع الصحة البراءة، وعند زفر: البيع جائز والشرط فاسد إذا كان مجهولاً، حتى إذا ذكر العيوب عددها صحت البراءة منها. ثم قال أبو يوسف يدخل في الإبراء الموجود من العيب والحادث منه عنده قبل القبض؛ لأن غرض البائع إلزام المشتري بالعقد، ولا يتم اللزوم إلا بأن الحادث والموجود، وقال محمد: لا يدخل الحادث؛ لأن البراءة إنما تكون عما هو ثابت فتختص بالموجود وبه قال مالك ولو شرط البراءة من كل عيب به لم ينصرف إلى الموجود وقت العقد بالبراءة. [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۰/۲] قلنا: الغرض إلزام العقد فيدخل في هذه البراءة من العيب الموجود عند العقد والعيب الحادث بعد العقد قبل القبض. (فتح) فلا يرد: المشتري بعد ذلك بعيب أصلا، فيدخل في هذه البراءة من العيب الموجود عند العقد والعيب الحادث بعد العقد قبل القبض. (عيني)
If he exonerates from every defect, it is valid. 'Every defect': for example, he sold a slave and stipulated that he is free from every defect. ('Ayni) 'It is valid etc.': i.e., if the seller says 'I sell you this slave on condition that I am free from every defect,' the sale and the exoneration are valid even if he does not enumerate all defects. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not valid unless he enumerates all defects, because exoneration from unknown rights is not valid according to him, since exoneration involves transfer of ownership such that it can be revoked by rejection, and it cannot be made conditional, and transferring unknown property is not valid like selling it. Ahmad said the same. We (Hanafis) say: This ignorance does not lead to dispute, so it does not preclude the validity of the exoneration. According to Zufar: The sale is valid but the condition is void if it is unknown, until if he mentions the defects by number, the exoneration from them is valid. Then Abu Yusuf said: The exoneration includes existing defects and those arising after contract before delivery, because the seller's aim is to bind the buyer to the contract, and binding is only complete by including both. Muhammad said: It does not include arising defects, because exoneration is only over what is established, so it is specific to existing defects. Malik said the same. If it is stipulated as exoneration from every defect in it, it does not extend to existing defects at the time of contract by exoneration. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/30] We say: The aim is to bind the contract, so it includes exoneration from defects existing at the time of contract and defects arising after contract before delivery. (Fath) So the buyer after that does not return it for any defect at all, and this exoneration includes the defect existing at the time of contract and the defect arising after contract before delivery. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses a seller's stipulation that he is free from all defects ('bara'ah min kull 'ayb'). The Hanafi position is that such a blanket immunity clause is valid, even without specifying each defect. The Shafi'i school and Ahmad hold it invalid because immunity from unknown rights is not permitted. Zufar (Hanafi) says the sale is valid but the condition is void if the defects are unspecified. There is an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the immunity covers defects that arise after the contract but before delivery: Abu Yusuf says yes, because the seller's aim is to ensure the contract is binding; Muhammad says no, because immunity only applies to existing defects. The relied-upon position (as per Fath and 'Ayni) is that the aim is to bind the contract, so it includes both existing and post-contract, pre-delivery defects. As a result, the buyer cannot return the item for any defect covered by the immunity.

Key Terms
براءة من العيب (bara'ah min al-'ayb): Exoneration from responsibility for defects; immunity clause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a seller says 'I sell you this slave on condition that I am free from every defect', what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid but the condition is void.
B) Both the sale and the immunity are valid.
C) The sale is void because the defects are unknown.
D) The buyer can return the slave if any defect appears.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a seller's blanket immunity from all defects is valid and prevents the buyer from returning the item for any defect, including those arising before delivery, per the mufta bihi view.

Actions That Constitute Acceptance of Defect
Ruling on implied acceptance of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions that constitute acceptance of a defect and those that do not, and apply the Hanafi ruling to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Necessity-driven actions do not imply acceptance of defect.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased animal, certain actions may waive the right to return. The Hanafi school distinguishes between actions showing satisfaction (like riding for pleasure) and actions driven by necessity (like watering). This lesson examines the rulings from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions mentioned
The text mentions riding and treating as acceptance, but provides exceptions: riding for watering, returning, or buying fodder.
2
Understand the rationale
The exception is based on need—these actions are necessary for the animal's care, not signs of satisfaction with the defect.
3
Apply to new cases
Consider if riding to the vet or to a stable constitutes acceptance. The guiding principle is intention and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
باب خيار العيب
والركوب والمداواة رضاً بالعيب لا الرّكوبُ للسقى أو للرّد أو لشراء العلف، ولو لأنه دليل إبقائه وإمساكه للدابة لاحتياج إليه
قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع، ردّه واسترة الثمن، ولو برئ من كل عيب صح، في يد المشتري أي بس بسبب عيب كان وإن لم يُسمّ الكل فلا أي جميع العيوب يرة بعيب. أي البائع عند البيع بنصف للسقي: أي لسقي الدابة من الحوض أو النهر ونحوهما. (عيني) بسبب عند البائع: كسرقة، ولم يعلم به المشتري عند البيع. (عيني) رده إلخ : أي لو اشترى عبدا قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم المشتري به عند البيع ولا عند القبض فقطع يده في يد المشتري بتلك السرقة يرده ويسترد الثمن عند أبي حنيفة أو أمسكه ورجع الثمن، وقالا : لا يرده ولكن يرجع بحصة النقصان من الثمن؛ لأن الموجود في يد البائع سبب القطع، وهو لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه تعيب، فيرجع بنقصانه وهو فرق ما بين قيمتي العبد بأن يقوم سارقا وغير سارق، فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما من الثمن؛ لتعذر الرد. وله: أن سبب الوجوب وجد في يد البائع، والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود، فيضاف الوجود إلى السبب السابق، فصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل العبد بسبب وجد في يد البائع من قتل نفس أو قطع طريق أو ردة فعنده يرجع بكل الثمن، وعندهما بفضل ما بين كونه مباح الدم ومعصومه رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/٢] (فتح)
كل عيب بأن باع عبدا واشترط أنه بريء من كل عيب. (عيني) صح إلخ: أي لو قال البائع: بعتك هذا العبد على أنني بريء من كل عيب صح البيع والإبراء وإن لم يسم جميع العيوب، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح إلا أن يعدد العيوب كلها؛ لأن الإبراء عن الحقوق المجهولة لا عنده؛ يصح لأن في الإبراء معنى التمليك حتى يرتد بالرد، ولا يصح تعليقه بالشرط، وتمليك المجهول لا يصح كبيعه، وبه قال أحمد، ونحن نقول: هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا تمنع الصحة البراءة، وعند زفر: البيع جائز والشرط فاسد إذا كان مجهولاً، حتى إذا ذكر العيوب عددها صحت البراءة منها.
ثم قال أبو يوسف يدخل في الإبراء الموجود من العيب والحادث منه عنده قبل القبض؛ لأن غرض البائع إلزام المشتري بالعقد، ولا يتم اللزوم إلا بأن الحادث والموجود، وقال محمد: لا يدخل الحادث؛ لأن البراءة إنما تكون عما هو ثابت فتختص بالموجود وبه قال مالك ولو شرط البراءة من كل عيب به لم ينصرف إلى الموجود وقت العقد بالبراءة. [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۰/۲] قلنا: الغرض إلزام العقد
خص الحادث في قولهم جميعاً؛ لأنه بإسقاط حقه عن صفة السلامة وذلك بالبراءة عن الموجود والحادث. (فتح)
فلا يرد: المشتري بعد ذلك بعيب أصلا، فيدخل في هذه البراءة من العيب الموجود عند العقد والعيب الحادث بعد العقد قبل القبض. (عيني)
The Book of Sales. Chapter on Option of Defect. Riding and treatment are acceptance of defect, but not riding for watering or for returning or for purchasing fodder, because it is evidence of retaining and keeping the animal due to need. If a defect occurs in the possession of the buyer due to a cause existing with the seller, he may return it and recover the full price. And if he declares himself free from every defect, it is valid, even if he did not specify all defects. 'Due to a cause existing with the seller': like theft, and the buyer did not know of it at the time of sale. 'Return it etc.': If a person buys a slave who had been stolen from the seller and the buyer did not know of it at the time of sale nor at the time of taking possession, and then the slave's hand is cut off in the buyer's possession as a penalty for that theft, then according to Abu Hanifa, he may return the slave and recover the full price, or keep him and claim the price. But Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: He may not return him, but he may claim the portion of the deficiency from the price, because the cause of the cutting existed with the seller, and that does not negate the property value, so the sale is effective but the slave has become defective, so he may claim the reduction, which is the difference between the value of the slave as a thief and as non-thief. He claims the excess of the price between the two, because return is not possible. Abu Hanifa's proof: The cause of obligation existed with the seller, and obligation leads to existence, so the existence is attributed to the earlier cause, just as if the misappropriated property was killed or cut after return due to a crime that occurred in the hand of the usurper. And the same dispute applies if the slave is killed due to a cause existing with the seller, such as murder, highway robbery or apostasy: according to Abu Hanifa, he recovers the full price; according to them, only the difference between being permissible to kill and being protected. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq 29/2) (Fath). 'Every defect': e.g., he sold a slave and stipulated that he is free from every defect. (Ayni). 'Valid etc.': If the seller said: I sell you this slave on condition that I am free from every defect, the sale and the disclaimer are valid even if he did not name all defects. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not valid unless he enumerates all defects, because a disclaimer of unknown rights is not valid, as it contains the meaning of transfer of ownership, and transfer of unknown property is not valid, like its sale. Ahmad said the same. We say: This unknownness does not lead to dispute, so it does not prevent the validity of the disclaimer. According to Zufar: The sale is valid but the condition is invalid if it is unknown, so if he mentions the defects one by one, the disclaimer from them is valid. Then Abu Yusuf said: The disclaimer covers the existing defect and the one that occurs before delivery, because the seller's purpose is to bind the buyer to the contract, and binding is not complete unless both existing and new are covered. Muhammad said: It does not cover the new one, because the disclaimer is about what is established, so it is limited to the existing. Malik said the same. If he stipulated disclaimer from every defect, it does not refer to the existing at the time of the contract. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq 30/2) We said: The purpose is to bind the contract, so it includes the new in both opinions, because it is dropping his right to the attribute of soundness, and that is by disclaimer from existing and new. (Fath). So the buyer cannot return afterwards due to any defect at all, and this disclaimer includes the defect existing at the time of contract and the defect occurring after the contract before delivery. (Ayni).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Option of Defect covers several rulings. First, it states that riding an animal or treating it is considered acceptance of the defect, meaning the buyer loses the right to return the item. However, riding for necessities like watering the animal, taking it to be returned, or buying fodder does not constitute acceptance, as these actions are evidence of keeping the animal out of need, not satisfaction with the defect. Next, the text discusses a case where a defect occurs on the item while in the buyer's possession, but the cause of the defect existed with the seller before sale—for example, a slave had been stolen from the seller and the buyer did not know, then the slave's hand is amputated as a penalty for that theft. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer can return the slave and recover the full price, or keep the slave and claim the full price, because the cause of the amputation existed in the seller's domain. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the buyer cannot return the slave, but can claim a reduction in price equal to the difference between the slave's value as a thief and as a law-abiding person. Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the principle that the cause of obligation is attributed to its place of origin, similar to the case of a usurped item that is later destroyed by a crime while in the usurper's hand. The same disagreement applies if the slave is killed due to a capital crime. Then the passage deals with a seller's disclaimer from all defects. The Hanafi position is that such a disclaimer is valid even without enumerating all defects, because the unknownness does not lead to dispute. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad require enumeration. Zufar says the sale is valid but the condition is invalid if the defects are unknown. Finally, there is a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether the disclaimer covers defects that arise after the contract but before delivery. Abu Yusuf says yes, because the seller's purpose is to bind the buyer; Muhammad says no, because a disclaimer only covers existing defects. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is often the view that the disclaimer covers both, as stated by the author of Fath al-Qadir: 'the buyer cannot return afterwards due to any defect at all, and the disclaimer includes both existing and new defects before delivery.'

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return a purchased item due to a defect existing at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered acceptance of a defect according to the text?
A) Riding the animal for pleasure
B) Treating the animal medically
C) Riding the animal to water it
D) Keeping the animal for an extended period

Key Takeaway
Actions driven by necessity do not waive the option to return a defective item.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Defect Arising from Seller's Cause: Full Return or Reduction?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect from seller's cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a defect caused by a pre-existing factor from the seller, and apply the ruling to similar cases.

Core Idea
If a defect occurs in the buyer's hand but the cause existed with the seller, Abu Hanifa allows full return; the Sahibayn only allow a reduction.

When a purchased slave has his hand amputated due to a theft committed before the sale, who bears the loss? This lesson explores the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether the buyer can return the slave for a full refund or only claim compensation for the reduced value.

Guided Path
1
Understand the facts of the case
A slave is stolen from the seller, then sold to the buyer without the buyer's knowledge of the theft. Subsequently, the slave's hand is cut off as a penalty for the theft while in the buyer's possession.
The defect (amputation) occurs in the buyer's hand, but the cause (theft) existed with the seller.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says the buyer can return the slave and get a full refund, or keep him and claim the full price, because the cause of the amputation is attributed to the seller.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the buyer cannot return the slave; instead, he claims the reduction in value (the difference between the slave's value as a thief and as innocent).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
He uses qiyas: if a usurped item is destroyed in the usurper's hand due to a crime, the usurper is liable. Here, the seller is like a usurper because the cause existed with him.
The argument is from 'dalil aqli' (analogical reasoning).
5
Apply to other scenarios
The same dispute applies if the slave is killed due to a capital crime (murder, apostasy) caused by the seller. Abu Hanifa gives full refund; Sahibayn give reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
باب خيار العيب
والركوب والمداواة رضاً بالعيب لا الرّكوبُ للسقى أو للرّد أو لشراء العلف، ولو لأنه دليل إبقائه وإمساكه للدابة لاحتياج إليه
قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع، ردّه واسترة الثمن، ولو برئ من كل عيب صح، في يد المشتري أي بس بسبب عيب كان وإن لم يُسمّ الكل فلا أي جميع العيوب يرة بعيب. أي البائع عند البيع بنصف للسقي: أي لسقي الدابة من الحوض أو النهر ونحوهما. (عيني) بسبب عند البائع: كسرقة، ولم يعلم به المشتري عند البيع. (عيني) رده إلخ : أي لو اشترى عبدا قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم المشتري به عند البيع ولا عند القبض فقطع يده في يد المشتري بتلك السرقة يرده ويسترد الثمن عند أبي حنيفة أو أمسكه ورجع الثمن، وقالا : لا يرده ولكن يرجع بحصة النقصان من الثمن؛ لأن الموجود في يد البائع سبب القطع، وهو لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه تعيب، فيرجع بنقصانه وهو فرق ما بين قيمتي العبد بأن يقوم سارقا وغير سارق، فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما من الثمن؛ لتعذر الرد. وله: أن سبب الوجوب وجد في يد البائع، والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود، فيضاف الوجود إلى السبب السابق، فصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل العبد بسبب وجد في يد البائع من قتل نفس أو قطع طريق أو ردة فعنده يرجع بكل الثمن، وعندهما بفضل ما بين كونه مباح الدم ومعصومه رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/٢] (فتح)
كل عيب بأن باع عبدا واشترط أنه بريء من كل عيب. (عيني) صح إلخ: أي لو قال البائع: بعتك هذا العبد على أنني بريء من كل عيب صح البيع والإبراء وإن لم يسم جميع العيوب، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح إلا أن يعدد العيوب كلها؛ لأن الإبراء عن الحقوق المجهولة لا عنده؛ يصح لأن في الإبراء معنى التمليك حتى يرتد بالرد، ولا يصح تعليقه بالشرط، وتمليك المجهول لا يصح كبيعه، وبه قال أحمد، ونحن نقول: هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا تمنع الصحة البراءة، وعند زفر: البيع جائز والشرط فاسد إذا كان مجهولاً، حتى إذا ذكر العيوب عددها صحت البراءة منها.
ثم قال أبو يوسف يدخل في الإبراء الموجود من العيب والحادث منه عنده قبل القبض؛ لأن غرض البائع إلزام المشتري بالعقد، ولا يتم اللزوم إلا بأن الحادث والموجود، وقال محمد: لا يدخل الحادث؛ لأن البراءة إنما تكون عما هو ثابت فتختص بالموجود وبه قال مالك ولو شرط البراءة من كل عيب به لم ينصرف إلى الموجود وقت العقد بالبراءة. [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۰/۲] قلنا: الغرض إلزام العقد
خص الحادث في قولهم جميعاً؛ لأنه بإسقاط حقه عن صفة السلامة وذلك بالبراءة عن الموجود والحادث. (فتح)
فلا يرد: المشتري بعد ذلك بعيب أصلا، فيدخل في هذه البراءة من العيب الموجود عند العقد والعيب الحادث بعد العقد قبل القبض. (عيني)
The Book of Sales. Chapter on Option of Defect. Riding and treatment are acceptance of defect, but not riding for watering or for returning or for purchasing fodder, because it is evidence of retaining and keeping the animal due to need. If a defect occurs in the possession of the buyer due to a cause existing with the seller, he may return it and recover the full price. And if he declares himself free from every defect, it is valid, even if he did not specify all defects. 'Due to a cause existing with the seller': like theft, and the buyer did not know of it at the time of sale. 'Return it etc.': If a person buys a slave who had been stolen from the seller and the buyer did not know of it at the time of sale nor at the time of taking possession, and then the slave's hand is cut off in the buyer's possession as a penalty for that theft, then according to Abu Hanifa, he may return the slave and recover the full price, or keep him and claim the price. But Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: He may not return him, but he may claim the portion of the deficiency from the price, because the cause of the cutting existed with the seller, and that does not negate the property value, so the sale is effective but the slave has become defective, so he may claim the reduction, which is the difference between the value of the slave as a thief and as non-thief. He claims the excess of the price between the two, because return is not possible. Abu Hanifa's proof: The cause of obligation existed with the seller, and obligation leads to existence, so the existence is attributed to the earlier cause, just as if the misappropriated property was killed or cut after return due to a crime that occurred in the hand of the usurper. And the same dispute applies if the slave is killed due to a cause existing with the seller, such as murder, highway robbery or apostasy: according to Abu Hanifa, he recovers the full price; according to them, only the difference between being permissible to kill and being protected. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq 29/2) (Fath). 'Every defect': e.g., he sold a slave and stipulated that he is free from every defect. (Ayni). 'Valid etc.': If the seller said: I sell you this slave on condition that I am free from every defect, the sale and the disclaimer are valid even if he did not name all defects. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not valid unless he enumerates all defects, because a disclaimer of unknown rights is not valid, as it contains the meaning of transfer of ownership, and transfer of unknown property is not valid, like its sale. Ahmad said the same. We say: This unknownness does not lead to dispute, so it does not prevent the validity of the disclaimer. According to Zufar: The sale is valid but the condition is invalid if it is unknown, so if he mentions the defects one by one, the disclaimer from them is valid. Then Abu Yusuf said: The disclaimer covers the existing defect and the one that occurs before delivery, because the seller's purpose is to bind the buyer to the contract, and binding is not complete unless both existing and new are covered. Muhammad said: It does not cover the new one, because the disclaimer is about what is established, so it is limited to the existing. Malik said the same. If he stipulated disclaimer from every defect, it does not refer to the existing at the time of the contract. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq 30/2) We said: The purpose is to bind the contract, so it includes the new in both opinions, because it is dropping his right to the attribute of soundness, and that is by disclaimer from existing and new. (Fath). So the buyer cannot return afterwards due to any defect at all, and this disclaimer includes the defect existing at the time of contract and the defect occurring after the contract before delivery. (Ayni).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Option of Defect covers several rulings. First, it states that riding an animal or treating it is considered acceptance of the defect, meaning the buyer loses the right to return the item. However, riding for necessities like watering the animal, taking it to be returned, or buying fodder does not constitute acceptance, as these actions are evidence of keeping the animal out of need, not satisfaction with the defect. Next, the text discusses a case where a defect occurs on the item while in the buyer's possession, but the cause of the defect existed with the seller before sale—for example, a slave had been stolen from the seller and the buyer did not know, then the slave's hand is amputated as a penalty for that theft. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer can return the slave and recover the full price, or keep the slave and claim the full price, because the cause of the amputation existed in the seller's domain. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the buyer cannot return the slave, but can claim a reduction in price equal to the difference between the slave's value as a thief and as a law-abiding person. Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the principle that the cause of obligation is attributed to its place of origin, similar to the case of a usurped item that is later destroyed by a crime while in the usurper's hand. The same disagreement applies if the slave is killed due to a capital crime. Then the passage deals with a seller's disclaimer from all defects. The Hanafi position is that such a disclaimer is valid even without enumerating all defects, because the unknownness does not lead to dispute. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad require enumeration. Zufar says the sale is valid but the condition is invalid if the defects are unknown. Finally, there is a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether the disclaimer covers defects that arise after the contract but before delivery. Abu Yusuf says yes, because the seller's purpose is to bind the buyer; Muhammad says no, because a disclaimer only covers existing defects. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is often the view that the disclaimer covers both, as stated by the author of Fath al-Qadir: 'the buyer cannot return afterwards due to any defect at all, and the disclaimer includes both existing and new defects before delivery.'

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for reduction in value due to a defect, calculated as the difference between the value before and after the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of the slave's hand being cut due to a pre-existing theft, which imam allows the buyer to return the slave for a full refund?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
When a defect originates from a cause with the seller, Abu Hanifa grants full rescission; the Sahibayn only grant compensation for the loss.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Seller's Disclaimer from All Defects: Validity and Scope
Ruling on disclaimer from defects with intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a seller's blanket disclaimer from all defects, the intra-Hanafi dispute on its scope, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A seller's blanket disclaimer from all defects is valid in Hanafi law even if unspecified; it covers existing and new defects before delivery.

When a seller says 'I am free from every defect,' is it valid without listing them? And does it cover defects that appear after the contract but before delivery? This lesson explores the Hanafi school's answer, the objection from Shafi'i, and the internal debate between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the validity of blanket disclaimer
The Hanafi school holds that if a seller stipulates that he is free from every defect, the disclaimer is valid even if defects are not specified. This is because the unknownness does not lead to dispute.
2
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad require enumeration of all defects because they view the disclaimer as a transfer of rights, and transferring unknown rights is invalid.
3
Consider Zufar's view
Zufar says the sale is valid but the condition (disclaimer) is invalid if the defects are unknown; it becomes valid only if defects are enumerated.
4
Analyze the scope: existing vs new defects
Abu Yusuf says the disclaimer covers defects existing at the time of contract and those arising before delivery. Muhammad says it covers only existing defects. The relied-upon view is that it covers both.
5
Apply the ruling
Once a valid disclaimer is made, the buyer cannot return the item for any defect, whether existing at sale or arising before delivery.

Source Text

Finally, there is a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether the disclaimer covers defects that arise after the contract but before delivery. Abu Yusuf says yes, because the seller's purpose is to bind the buyer; Muhammad says no, because a disclaimer only covers existing defects. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is often the view that the disclaimer covers both, as stated by the author of Fath al-Qadir: 'the buyer cannot return afterwards due to any defect at all, and the disclaimer includes both existing and new defects before delivery.'

Key Terms
إبراء (ibraa): A seller's declaration of being free from responsibility for defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, a seller's blanket disclaimer covers which defects?
A) Only defects existing at the time of sale.
B) Only defects arising before delivery.
C) Both existing defects and defects arising before delivery.
D) Only defects enumerated by the seller.

Key Takeaway
A seller's blanket disclaimer from all defects is valid in Hanafi law and covers defects existing at the time of sale and those arising before delivery.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

8 min
Distinguishing Batil, Fasid, and Makruh in Sales
Definitions of batil, fasid, and makruh sales with criterion for classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between batil, fasid, and makruh sales according to Hanafi fiqh and apply the criterion of mal mutaqawwim to classify any given sale.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if one exchanged item is not property in any heavenly religion; fasid if its essence is property but a condition is missing.

Not all invalid sales are the same. The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction between batil (void) and fasid (invalid), with significant practical consequences. This lesson parses the definitions and criteria provided in the classical texts, so you can accurately classify sales and understand their effects. Pay close attention to the role of property (mal) in different legal systems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Categories of Sale
Recognize that the text divides sales into sahih, batil, fasid, and mawquf. Your first task is to memorize the definitions of each, especially the key distinction: batil is not legitimate in its essence, while fasid is legitimate in essence but flawed in description.
The text explicitly says batil is 'not legitimate at all' whereas fasid is 'legitimate in essence but not in description'.
2
Understand the Criterion for Property
The text provides a rule based on whether the item is considered property (mal) in a heavenly religion. If the item is not property in any such religion, the sale is batil. If it is property in some but not others, and can serve as a price, the sale is fasid. Apply this rule step by step to examples like carrion, wine, and free persons.
What is property for a non-Muslim (e.g., wine) is not property for a Muslim. This relativity is key.
3
Apply the Criterion to Given Examples
Use the examples: selling carrion, blood, pig, wine, a free person, or umm walad are all batil because these are not property in any heavenly religion. Selling a slave for wine is fasid because wine is property for non-Muslims but not for Muslims, and it is used as a price.
The text specifies that if wine is the sold item (not the price), the sale is batil.
4
Distinguish Fasid from Batil in Effect
Understand the legal effect: batil never transfers ownership, even after delivery. Fasid can transfer ownership if delivery occurs after the defect is removed. This has practical implications for contracts already executed.
This is a unique Hanafi position; other schools often treat fasid like batil.
5
Identify the Role of Makruh Sales
The text mentions makruh as a third category in the chapter. A sale is makruh when it is valid in essence and description but is associated with something prohibited. Recognize that the chapter title uses 'fasid' generically to cover all three, because fasid is the broadest term.
The author explains: 'fasid is a comprehensive description like a general accident'.

(عيني] أخره؛ لأن العقود الفاسدة واجبة الرفع؛ لأنه يحرم مباشرتها، لكن إذا زال المفسد قبل تقرره يعود العقد إلى الجواز. ثم البيع على أربعة أقسام صحيح وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه كبيع ثوب وعبد بأحد النقدين ونحوهما، ويفيد الحكم بنفسه إذا خلا عن الموانع. وباطل: وهو غير مشروع أصلاً كبيع الخمر والخنزير والحر والميتة والدم ولا يفيد الملك أصلا، لا قبل القبض ولا بعده وفاسد وهو مشروع بأصله دون وصفه، ومرادهم من أصله كونه مالا متقوما لا جوازه، وصحته، وهو يفيد الحكم إذا اتصل به القبض والفاسد أعم . الباطل؛ لأن كل باطل فاسد ولا ينعكس.... وموقوف: وهو يفيد الحكم على سبيل التوقف، وامتنع إتمامه لأجل غيره، وهو بيع ملك الغير. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۱/۲] وهذا الباب مشتمل على ثلاثة أنواع باطل ،وفاسد، ومكروه، والمكروه مشروع بأصله ووصفه، لكن جاوره شيء آخر منهي . عنه، ثم لقب الباب بالفاسد دون الباطل والمكروه مع اشتمال الباب عليهما؛ لأن الفاسد وصف شامل كالعرض العام. ثم الضابط في تمييز الفاسد من الباطل أن أحد العوضين إذا لم يكن مالاً في دين سماوي، فالبيع باطل مبيعاً كان أو لمناً كبيع الميتة والحر ، وكذا البيع به، وإن كان في بعض الأديان مالاً دون البعض إن أمكن اعتباره ثمناً ، فالبيع فاسد كبيع العبد بالخمر أو الخمر بالعبد، وإن تعين كونه مبيعاً، فالبيع باطل كبيع الخمر بالدراهم، أو الدراهم بالخمر. لم يجز بيع الميتة عبر بعدم الجواز ؛ ليعم ما عقد الباب له من الباطل والفاسد أي يبطل بيع الميتة، ولا فرق في حق المسلم بين التي ماتت حتف أنفها أو كانت منخنقة أو موقوذة، أو ماتت بالجرح في غير المذبح. Chapter on Invalid Sale. Chapter on Invalid Sale, i.e., explaining its rulings. 'It is not permissible to sell carrion, blood, pig, wine, a free person, and an umm walad.' 'Legitimacy'. 'i.e., selling blood, selling a free person, selling an umm walad.' 'From'. Chapter on Invalid Sale: [It is that which is legitimate in its essence but not in its description, and it is more general than batil (void); because every batil is fasid but not vice versa. (Ayni)] 'Retarded'; because invalid contracts must be lifted; it is prohibited to engage in them, but if the corrupting element is removed before settlement, the contract returns to permissibility. Then sales are of four types: Sahih (valid) – legitimate in essence and description, like selling a garment or slave for one of the two currencies and similar; it gives effect by itself if free from impediments. Batil (void) – not legitimate at all, like selling wine, pig, a free person, carrion, and blood; it does not confer ownership at all, neither before nor after delivery. Fasid (invalid) – legitimate in essence but not in description; they mean by 'essence' that it is valuable property, not its permissibility and validity; and it gives effect if delivery is connected. Fasid is more general than batil because every batil is fasid but not vice versa... And Mawquf (suspended) – it gives effect on a suspended basis, and its completion is prevented due to something else, and that is the sale of another's property. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq 31/2) This chapter includes three types: batil, fasid, and makruh. Makruh is legitimate in essence and description but is adjacent to something else that is prohibited. Then the chapter is titled 'fasid' instead of batil or makruh despite including them, because fasid is a comprehensive description like a general accident. Then the criterion for distinguishing fasid from batil is that if one of the two considerations is not property in a heavenly religion, the sale is batil whether it is the sold item or the price, like selling carrion or a free person, and likewise selling with it. If it is property in some religions but not others, and it is possible to consider it as a price, then the sale is fasid, like selling a slave for wine or wine for a slave. If it is determined to be the sold item, then the sale is batil, like selling wine for dirhams or dirhams for wine. (Ayni, Fath) 'It is not permissible to sell carrion' – he expressed with impermissibility to cover both batil and fasid intended by the chapter, i.e., the sale of carrion is batil. For a Muslim, there is no difference between an animal that died naturally or was strangled, beaten, or died from a wound in a non-sacrificed part. (Fath) 'Pig' – i.e., selling pig; it is not permissible for a Muslim to sell these things because the pillar of sale, which is exchange of property for property, is missing; these are not property. (Ayni) 'Wine' – i.e., it is not permissible for a Muslim to sell wine. Carrion that died naturally is not property; as for others like the beaten animal, it is property according to the People of the Book, like wine. By 'carrion' he means other than fish and locusts. By 'blood' he means shed blood; thus the sale of liver and spleen is excluded. These sales are all batil. The skin of carrion is like wine; the preferred view is that it is like carrion because it is part of it. Similarly, the sale of urine is batil. The sale of game in the state of ihram is batil because it is prohibited for him like carrion. (Ayni, Fath) 'Free person etc.' – these things are not considered property by anyone. As for someone who has no heavenly religion, he is like an inanimate object. (Ayni, Fath) 'From heavenly; because the attribute of property is by general valuation or by the valuation of a religion.'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage from the Chapter on Invalid Sales defines key terms and classifications in Hanafi sales law. The chapter is titled 'Invalid Sale' (fasid), but it actually covers three categories: batil (void), fasid (invalid), and makruh (disliked). The text explains that a sale is batil if the object or price is not considered property in any divinely revealed religion—examples include carrion, blood, pig, wine, a free person, and an umm walad (mother of a child). These items are not considered property, so the sale is void from the outset and does not transfer ownership at all. A sale is fasid when it is valid in essence (the subject is valuable property) but lacks a condition of description, such as selling a slave for wine (wine is property for non-Muslims but not for Muslims). Fasid sales may become valid if the corrupting element is removed and delivery has taken place. The text also gives a criterion: if one of the exchanged items is not property in any heavenly religion, the sale is batil; if it is property in some religions and can be considered a price, the sale is fasid. Further, it clarifies that carrion means any animal that died without proper slaughter, excluding fish and locusts; blood means shed blood, not internal organs like liver. The skin of carrion is treated like carrion itself. The sale of urine and the sale of game for a pilgrim in ihram are also batil. The passage emphasizes that the basis of a valid sale is that the item must be 'mal mutaqawwim' (valuable property), and items that are not property in any divine law fail the pillar of sale, which is exchange of property.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is legitimate in its essence (the object is valuable property) but lacks a condition in its description.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and can be owned; it must be permissible to benefit from in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi criteria in this text, selling wine for dirhams is classified as what type of sale?
A) Sahih (valid), because wine is property for non-Muslims.
B) Fasid (invalid), because wine is not property in Islam but can be treated as a price.
C) Batil (void), because wine is not property in any heavenly religion when determined as the sold item.
D) Makruh (disliked), because it is close to prohibited.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of sales into batil and fasid hinges on whether the object or price is considered property (mal mutaqawwim) in at least one heavenly religion.

The Four Categories of Sale in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines and classifies sale contracts by validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sahih, batil, fasid, and mawquf sales and apply the correct ruling for each.

Core Idea
A sale's validity depends on its essence and description; Hanafis uniquely recognize fasid as a correctable intermediate category.

The Hanafi school classifies sales into four types based on legal validity. This lesson clarifies each category, the conditions that render a sale void or corrigible, and the practical consequences for ownership and delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize that sale contracts are categorized by their permissibility in essence (asl) and description (wasf). The text presents four categories: sahih, batil, fasid, and mawquf.
2
Distinguish the categories
Define each category based on the text: sahih (permissible in both), batil (not permissible in either), fasid (permissible in essence but not in description), mawquf (validity depends on ratification of another).
Note that fasid is more general than batil: every batil is fasid, but not vice versa.
3
Apply the ruling to examples
Using the text's examples (carrion, wine, free person, unknown price), practice classifying a sale and determining its effect on ownership before and after delivery.
Fasid can become effective if connected to delivery (al-qabd).

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on invalid (fāsid) sales by defining the categories of sale according to the Hanafi school...

Key Terms
فاسد (fāsid): A sale that is permissible in its essence but not in its description; e.g., a sale with an unknown price.
باطل (bāṭil): A sale that is not permissible at all, neither in essence nor in description; e.g., selling carrion.
صحيح (ṣaḥīḥ): A sale that is permissible in both essence and description, e.g., selling a garment for known cash.
موقوف (mawqūf): A sale that is valid in essence but its completion is suspended due to the right of another, e.g., selling property of another without permission.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property recognized by Islamic law that can be legally transacted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Fasid can become valid after delivery; batil never conveys ownership.
D) B and C.

Key Takeaway
Understanding the four categories of sale is essential for correctly assessing whether a contract can be corrected or must be voided.
