Ruling on Selling Impure and Non-Mal Items
States the prohibition of selling carrion, blood, pig, wine, and free persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that the sale of carrion, blood, pig, wine, and free persons is batil, and identify the key criterion of property (mal) in a revealed religion.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if the subject matter is not considered property (mal) by any revealed religion.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines the Hanafi ruling on items that cannot be sold because they lack the status of property (mal). The author lists specific items—carrion, blood, pig, wine, and free persons—and explains that the sale is void because the pillar of sale, exchange of property, is absent. Understanding the Hanafi definition of property is crucial for distinguishing valid from invalid sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed items and their common feature
The passage explicitly lists carrion (maytah), blood (dam), pig (khinzir), wine (khamr), and free persons (hurr). Notice that all are either impure, non-functional as property, or intrinsically non-commercial in a revealed religion.
Carrion includes various causes of death; blood is restricted to masfuh (poured forth).
2
Recognize the Hanafi condition for a valid sale: the item must be mal (property) according to a revealed religion
The Hanafi school defines mal as something that people usually consider valuable and that can be lawfully owned and traded, but with the additional criterion that it must be recognized as property by a revealed religion (din samawi). This excludes carrion, pig, etc., even if some people value them.
For dhimmis, items like wine may be considered mal because their religion permits it.
3
Apply the ruling to each item
Carrion: not property because it is impure and not lawful for Muslims. Blood: masfuh only; liver and spleen are excluded. Pig: impure and not property. Wine: for a Muslim it is not property, but for a dhimmi it is, so sale between non-Muslims may be valid. Free person: cannot be owned, so not property.
The hide of carrion is treated like carrion itself—its sale is also batil.
4
Note the exceptions and subsidiary rulings
Sale of land game by a muhrim (person in ihram) is batil because it is forbidden to him like carrion. Sale of urine is also batil. The passage emphasizes that one who has no revealed religion (e.g., an atheist) is considered like an inanimate object, so his concept of property is irrelevant.
The chosen view (mukhtar) is that carrion hide remains carrion; the contrary view allowing tanning is not adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يجز بيع الميتة عبر بعدم الجواز ؛ ليعم ما عقد الباب له من الباطل والفاسد أي يبطل بيع الميتة، ولا فرق في حق المسلم بين التي ماتت حتف أنفها أو كانت منخنقة أو موقوذة، أو ماتت بالجرح في غير المذبح. (فتح) والخنزير أي وبيع الخنزير، أي لا يجوز للمسلم بيع . هذه الأشياء لعدم ركن البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال وهذه ليست بمال. عيني (ط والخمر [ أي لم يجز بيع الخمر للمسلم] أي لم يجز بيع الميتة الذي مات حتف أنفها؛ لأنها ليست بمال، أما غيرها مثل الموقوذة فمال عند أهل الذمة كالخمر، وأراد بالميتة غير السمك والجراد، والمراد بالدم: المسفوح، فخرج بيع الكبد والطحال. وهذه البيوع كلها باطلة وجلد الميتة كالخمر والمختار أنها كالميتة؛ لأنه جزؤها، وكذا بيع البول باطل، وبيع صيد البر في حق المحرم باطل؛ لأنه حرام عليه كالميتة. (عيني، فتح) والحر إلخ: فإن هذه الأشياء لا يعد مالاً عند أحد . له البعض كما مر وكل ما ليس بمال فالبيع فيه ،باطل ومن ليس له دين سماوي فهو بمنزلة الجماد. (عيني، فتح) من سماوي؛ لأن صفة المالية بتمول كل الناس أو بتمول دین
It is not permissible to sell carrion—expressed by lack of permissibility to include what the chapter covers of void and invalid. That is, the sale of carrion is void, and there is no difference for a Muslim between one that died naturally, or was strangled, or beaten to death, or died from a wound in a non-slaughtering spot. (Fath) And pig: i.e., selling pig; it is not permissible for a Muslim to sell these things due to the absence of the pillar of sale, which is exchanging property for property, and these are not property. (al-`Ayni) And wine: i.e., the sale of wine for a Muslim is not permissible. i.e., the sale of carrion that died naturally is not permissible because it is not property. As for others like the beaten animal, it is property according to the People of the Covenant (ahl al-dhimma), like wine. By carrion is meant other than fish and locusts. By blood is meant that which is poured forth (masfuh), so the sale of liver and spleen is excluded. All these sales are void. The hide of carrion is like carrion; the chosen view is that it is like carrion because it is a part of it. Similarly, the sale of urine is void. The sale of land game for a person in ihram is void because it is forbidden for him like carrion. (al-`Ayni, Fath) And the free person, etc.: These things are not considered property by anyone. Some have said as mentioned, and whatever is not property, its sale is void. And one who has no revealed religion is like an inanimate object. (al-`Ayni, Fath) From revealed religion: because the attribute of property (māliyyah) is by the possession of all people or by the possession of a religion.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage details the rulings on selling items that are not considered property (mal) in a revealed religion...

Key Terms
مال (māl): Property that is considered valuable in a revealed religion and can be lawfully owned and traded.
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal that has not been slaughtered Islamically; its sale is batil.
مسفوح (masfūḥ): Blood that is poured forth, i.e., flowing blood; sale of liver and spleen is allowed because they are not masfuh.
باطل (bāṭil): Void; a contract that has no legal effect from inception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of carrion batil?
A) Because it is impure.
B) Because it is not considered property in any revealed religion.
C) Because the Prophet forbade it.
D) Because it is not in the possession of the seller.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines property (mal) by the standards of a revealed religion; if an item is not mal, its sale is automatically batil.

Prohibition of Selling Umm Walad, Mudabbar, and Mukatab
States the prohibition and liability for selling umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability if they perish after sale.

Core Idea
Slaves with vested rights to freedom cannot be sold; if sold, liability for loss is disputed among the Hanafi masters.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the sale of three categories of slaves whose freedom is imminent or contracted: umm walad (mother of the master's child), mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death), and mukatab (contracted freedom). The core ruling is that such sales are prohibited. However, if a sale does occur, what happens if the slave perishes before being reclaimed? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on the liability, and later scholars settled on a middle position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and the core ruling
Recognise umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab as slaves with special rights. State the general prohibition of their sale.
The prohibition is based on the slave's right to freedom.
2
Understand the basis for each
For umm walad, the Prophet's hadith; for mudabbar, the immediate cause of freedom; for mukatab, the binding contract.
Also note the exception for mukatab with consent.
3
Note the exception for mukatab
If the mukatab consents to being sold, the sale is valid in the stronger narration, and the kitaba contract is rescinded.
Without consent, the sale is invalid.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability
Abu Hanifa: no liability (trust). The Sahibayn: liability for umm walad and mudabbar.
The liability amounts differ: originally one-third/two-thirds, later settled on half.
5
Examine the later tarjih
The most correct view is half the value of a regular slave for both umm walad and mudabbar; this is the fatwa. Mukatab has no liability by consensus.
Tarjih by al-`Ayni and Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمدبر والمكاتب فلو هلكوا عند المشتري لم يضمن والسمك قبل الصيد المشتري شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة أي ولم يجز بيع السمك وهو والمدبر والمراد بالمدير المدبر المطلق، وهو أن يقال: أنت مدبر، أو إن مت فأنت حر، واحترز به عن المدبر المقيد، أن يقال: إن مت في هذا المرض فأنت حر، فإنه يجوز بيعه بالاتفاق وقال الشافعي: يجوز بيع المدبر المطلق، وهذه المسألة أي أم الولد والمدبر كانت مختلفاً فيها في الصدر الأول، وكان عمر ه لا يجيز بيعهما، وكان بيع علي الله يجيز بيعهما، ثم أجمع التابعون على عدم جواز بيعهما. (عيني، فتح) والمكاتب أي ولم يجز بيع المكاتب، سواء رضي ببيعه أو لا؛ لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت في حق أم الولد بقوله عليه: "أعتقها ولدها، وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال؛ لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت، والمكاتب استحق هذا على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك، نعم ! لو رضي المكاتب بالبيع جاز في أظهر الروايتين، وتنفسخ الكتابة في ضمنه؛ لأن اللزوم كان لحقه، وقد رضي بإسقاطه، وإذا بيع بغير رضاه، فأجازه لم يجز رواية واحدة وولد هؤلاء مثلهم في الحكم. (عيني، فتح) فلو هلكوا أي لو باع الحر وأم الولد والمدبر والمكاتب وهلكوا. (عيني، مسكين) لم يضمن لأن العقد في الباطل غير معتبر فبقي القبض بإذن المالك، أي فيكون أمانة ولا ضمان فيها، وقيل: يضمن في المدبر وأم الولد؛ لأنه لا يكون أولى حالاً من المقبوض على سوم الشراء، وقيل: الأول قول أبي حنيفة، والثاني قولهما رمز الحقائق: ۳۱/۲] وقالا يضمن في المدير وأم الولد قيمتهما وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، فيضمن في أم الولد ثلث قيمتها قنة، وفي المدير ثلثي قيمته قناً؛ لأنه فات بالاستيلاد منفعة البيع والسعاية، وبقي منفعة الاستخدام، والوطء من الاستخدام، وفي المدبر منفعة الاستخدام والسعاية باقية، ومنفعة البيع زائلة، والأصح أن قيمة كل من المدبر وأم الولد على النصف من قيمة القن، وعليه الفتوى، وأما المكاتب فلا يضمن بالاتفاق. (عيني، فتح)
And the mudabbar and the mukatab—if they perish in the possession of the buyer, there is no liability and the fish before catching—the buyer does not owe anything according to Abu Hanifa. That is, it is not permissible to sell fish before catching. And the mudabbar: what is meant by mudabbar is the absolute mudabbar, which is that one says to his slave: 'You are mudabbar' or 'If I die, you are free.' ...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of three categories of slaves with rights to freedom: umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab...

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; she cannot be sold and becomes free upon the master's death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death; sale of such a slave is not permissible.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments; his sale is not permissible without his consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Full value as a slave.
B) Half of her value as a slave.
C) One-third of her value.
D) No liability.

Key Takeaway
The sale of slaves with rights to freedom is invalid; if a sale occurs, the mufta bihi on liability for umm walad and mudabbar is half their value as a slave.

Ruling on Selling Fish Before and After Catching
States prohibition and conditions for selling fish before and after catching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fish before and after catching, identify the condition of gharar, and apply the conditions for a valid sale of fish in an enclosure.

Core Idea
Selling fish before catching is batil due to gharar; after catching, validity depends on enclosure and deliverability.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a classic fiqh problem: can you sell fish that have not yet been caught? The ruling hinges on the prohibition of gharar (uncertainty). We will examine the hadith evidence, the two scenarios, and the intra-Hanafi difference on what constitutes sufficient possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of fish sale.
The text distinguishes selling fish before catching (batil) and after catching (conditional). Note the two sub-cases of the second scenario depending on enclosure.
2
State the ruling for each scenario.
First scenario: haram/batil due to gharar. Second scenario: valid if the fish are in an enclosure that prevents escape without fishing, and invalid if the enclosure is open or the fish cannot be delivered.
Ruling based on hadith 'no gharar'.
3
Analyze the dalil.
The hadith 'نهى عن بيع الغرر' (prohibition of gharar sales) is used. Explain why selling uncaptured fish constitutes gharar.
The fish may not be caught or may not exist.
4
Examine the conditions for the second scenario.
If the fish are in a hazirah (enclosure) that is large enough that they cannot be taken without a trick (hila), the sale is valid. If they gather naturally and the entrance is not blocked, sale is invalid.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: some say blocking the entrance suffices, others require ability to take without fishing.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical difference: whether the buyer can acquire the fish if the enclosure is blocked but fishing is still needed.
The mufta bihi position is that sale is valid if fish can be taken without fishing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسمك قبل الصيد [ أي قبل الاصطياد ؛ لأنه لا يتملك ويحرز قبله. (عيني)] بيع سمك على نوعين: إما أن يبيعه قبل أن يصطاده أو بعده، والأول لا يجوز؛ لما روي أنه عليها نهى عن بيع الغرر وهو ماله ظاهر لغير المشتري، وباطن مجهول، رواه أحمد ومسلم وأبو داود والثاني: إن ألقاه في الحظيرة، فإن كانت كبيرة بحيث لا يمكن أخذه بغير حيلة جاز؛ لأنه باع ما يملكه وهو مقدور التسليم والحظيرة: الحوض والبركة، ويثبت للمشتري خيار الرؤية، ولا يفيد الرؤية في الماء. وإن اجتمع السمك في الحظائر بنفسه من غير صنعه، ولم يسد عليه المدخل لا يجوز بيعه، سواء أمكنه الأخذ بحيلة أو لا، وإن سد موضع دخول الماء بحيث لا يستطيع الخروج عنها لا يصح البيع عند بعض المشايخ؛ لأنه لا يملك بهذا القدر من الإحراز، وقيل: يصح إن أمكنه الأخذ بلا اصطياد رمز الحقائق: ٣٢/٢] (فتح)
And fish before catching: i.e., before it is caught because it cannot be owned and possessed before that. (al-`Ayni) The sale of fish is of two types: either he sells it before he catches it or after. The first is not permissible due to the hadith that he (peace be upon him) prohibited the sale of gharar (uncertainty)—that which is apparent to others but hidden and unknown—narrated by Ahmad, Muslim, and Abu Dawud. The second: if he throws it into an enclosure (hazirah), and if it is large such that it cannot be taken without a trick, it is permissible because he is selling what he owns and can deliver. The hazirah is a basin or pool. The buyer has the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'ya), and seeing in water does not suffice. If the fish gather in the enclosures by themselves without his action, and he did not block the entrance, its sale is not permissible, whether he can take it by a trick or not. If he blocked the entrance of water so that they cannot exit, the sale is not correct according to some mashayikh because he does not own it by that degree of possession. It is said: it is correct if he can take it without fishing (by hand). (Fath)
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of fish, distinguishing between before and after catching...

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty in a sale where the item is not clearly known or deliverable.
حظيرة (hazīrah): An enclosure or basin where fish are kept; its size and control affect the validity of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling fish before they are caught considered batil?
A) Because fish are not property.
B) Because of gharar (uncertainty) in existence and delivery.
C) Because the seller does not own them.
D) Because the buyer cannot see them.

Key Takeaway
Selling fish before catching is batil due to gharar; after catching, validity depends on the enclosure and ability to deliver without fishing.

Selling Slaves with Freedom Rights: Mudabbar, Umm Walad, Mukatab
Rulings on selling slaves with partial freedom rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sale of mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute on guarantee if they perish.

Core Idea
Sale invalid when freedom right is established; guarantee differs by imam.

This passage examines two related fiqh issues: the validity of selling slaves who have partial freedom rights, and the liability (daman) if such a slave perishes with the buyer. The Hanafi school draws on early consensus to prohibit such sales, but faces an intra-madhhab split on the guarantee. Understanding these rulings requires careful attention to the nature of the freedom right (right to freedom) and the different categories of slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and their freedom status
List the mudabbar (absolute vs restricted), umm walad, and mukatab. Note how each has a right or cause of freedom that is already instantiated.
The restricted mudabbar (conditioned on illness) is sold by consensus.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof for prohibition of sale
For umm walad, the hadith 'Her child frees her' establishes the right of freedom. For mudabbar, the cause of freedom is instantiated now (since capacity after death is null). For mukatab, the contract is binding. Sale would nullify these rights.
The tabi'un consensus is cited as the primary proof for mudabbar and umm walad.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the absolute mudabbar can be sold. The Hanafi rebuttal is that the right to freedom cannot be defeated by sale.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Shafi'is allow such sales; Hanafis do not.
4
Examine the guarantee dispute if the slave perishes
Abu Hanifa: no guarantee (contract invalid, possession is trust). Sahibayn: guarantee full value. Another narration from Abu Hanifa: partial guarantee (one-third for umm walad, two-thirds for mudabbar). The mufta bihi is half-value for each.
For mukatab, all agree no guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمدبر والمكاتب فلو هلكوا عند المشتري لم يضمن والسمك قبل الصيد المشتري شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة أي ولم يجز بيع السمك وهو والمُدبَّر والمراد بالمدبر المدبر المطلق، وهو أن يقال: أنت مدبر، أو إن مت فأنت حر، واحترز به عن المدبر المقيد، أن يقال: إن مت في هذا المرض فأنت حر، فإنه يجوز بيعه بالاتفاق وقال الشافعي: يجوز بيع المدبر المطلق، وهذه المسألة أي أم الولد والمدبر كانت مختلفاً فيها في الصدر الأول، وكان عمر ه لا يجيز بيعهما، وكان علي الله يجيز بيعهما، ثم أجمع التابعون على عدم جواز بيعهما. (عيني، فتح) والمكاتب أي ولم يجز بيع المكاتب، سواء رضي ببيعه أو لا؛ لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت في حق أم الولد بقوله عليه: "أعتقها ولدها، وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال؛ لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت، والمكاتب استحق هذا على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك، نعم ! لو رضي المكاتب بالبيع جاز في أظهر الروايتين، وتنفسخ الكتابة في ضمنه؛ لأن اللزوم كان لحقه، وقد رضي بإسقاطه، وإذا بيع بغير رضاه، فأجازه لم يجز رواية واحدة وولد هؤلاء مثلهم في الحكم. (عيني، فتح) فلو هلكوا أي لو باع الحر وأم الولد والمدبر والمكاتب وهلكوا. (عيني، مسكين) لم يضمن لأن العقد في الباطل غير معتبر فبقي القبض بإذن المالك، أي فيكون أمانة ولا ضمان فيها، وقيل: يضمن في المدبر وأم الولد؛ لأنه لا يكون أولى حالاً من المقبوض على سوم الشراء، وقيل: الأول قول أبي حنيفة، والثاني قولهما رمز الحقائق: ۳۱/۲] وقالا يضمن في المدبر وأم الولد قيمتهما وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، فيضمن في أم الولد ثلث قيمتها قنة، وفي المدبر ثلثي قيمته قناً؛ لأنه فات بالاستيلاد منفعة البيع والسعاية، وبقي منفعة الاستخدام، والوطء من الاستخدام، وفي المدبر منفعة الاستخدام والسعاية باقية، ومنفعة البيع زائلة، والأصح أن قيمة كل من المدبر وأم الولد على النصف من قيمة القن، وعليه الفتوى، وأما المكاتب فلا يضمن بالاتفاق. (عيني، فتح)
And the mudabbar and the mukatab: if they perish with the buyer, he does not guarantee. The intended meaning by mudabbar is the absolute mudabbar, which is that one says: 'You are mudabbar' or 'If I die, you are free.' It is distinguished from the restricted mudabbar, which is to say: 'If I die in this illness, you are free,' for its sale is permissible by agreement. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of the absolute mudabbar is permissible. This issue, i.e., umm walad and mudabbar, was disputed in the early period. Umar (r) did not permit their sale, Ali (r) permitted their sale, then the tabi'un agreed on their impermissibility. (Ayni, Fath) And the mukatab: i.e., the sale of the mukatab is not permissible, whether he consents or not, because the entitlement to freedom is established for the umm walad by his statement: "Her child frees her," and the cause of freedom is instantiated for the mudabbar at the present moment due to the nullification of capacity after death. The mukatab has established this upon himself as binding upon the master. If ownership were established by sale, that would be nullified. Yes, if the mukatab consents to the sale, it is permissible in the more apparent of the two narrations, and the kitabah is dissolved within it, because the binding was for his right, and he has consented to its dropping. If he was sold without his consent and then he ratifies it, it is not permissible in one narration. Their children are like them in ruling. (Ayni, Fath) 'If they perish' i.e., if he sold the free [and the umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab] and they perished. (Ayni, Miskin) 'He does not guarantee' because the contract in [the case of] the invalid is not considered, so the possession remains with the permission of the owner, i.e., it is a trust and there is no guarantee for it. And it is said: He guarantees for the mudabbar and umm walad because they are not in a better state than the item possessed on the basis of purchase. And it is said: The first is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and the second is the statement of them both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/31. And they two said: He guarantees their value for the mudabbar and umm walad, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa. So he guarantees for the umm walad one-third of her value as a slave, and for the mudabbar two-thirds of his value as a slave, because by the birth of her child, the benefit of sale and earning is lost, and the benefit of service remains, and intercourse is from service. For the mudabbar, the benefit of service and earning remain, and the benefit of sale is gone. The most correct is that the value of each of the mudabbar and umm walad is half the value of a full slave, and upon this is the fatwa. As for the mukatab, he does not guarantee by agreement. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of slaves who have partial freedom rights: the mudabbar (whose freedom is deferred to the master's death), the umm walad (mother of a child from the master), and the mukatab (who has a contract of manumission). The passage first defines the absolute mudabbar (sale invalid) versus the restricted mudabbar (sale valid by consensus). Al-Shafi'i holds that the absolute mudabbar can be sold, but the Hanafi school follows the consensus of the tabi'un that it is invalid. The reason: these slaves have an established right to freedom that would be nullified by sale. The passage then discusses the guarantee (daman) if such slaves die in the buyer's possession. The ruling of Abu Hanifa is that the buyer does not guarantee, because the invalid contract gives no effect, so possession is a trust. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the buyer guarantees the value, because the slave is not in a better state than if it were a valid sale with option. There is also a narration from Abu Hanifa that he guarantees but with a fraction: for umm walad one-third of her value, for mudabbar two-thirds. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that each is worth half a slave. For the mukatab, there is no guarantee by agreement of all.

Key Terms
المدبر المطلق (al-mudabbar al-mutlaq): A slave whose freedom is tied to the master's death in an absolute manner (e.g., 'you are free after my death'), without restriction to a specific illness.
أم الولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) to purchase freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling an absolute mudabbar?
A) Valid, by consensus.
B) Invalid, because of the consensus of the tabi'un.
C) Valid, according to Imam Shafi'i.
D) Invalid, but only if the mudabbar objects.

Key Takeaway
The sale of any slave with an established right or cause of freedom is invalid in the Hanafi school, and the guarantee if they perish depends on which imam's view is followed.

Invalidity of Selling Birds in the Air: Ownership and Delivery Conditions
Ruling with proof by analysis of conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling birds in the air and explain the two reasons for its invalidity: lack of ownership and inability to deliver.

Core Idea
You cannot sell what you do not own or cannot deliver — both conditions invalidate the sale.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a classic fiqh scenario: selling birds while they are flying. The Hanafi school rules the sale invalid, whether the bird was previously caught and released or not. The ruling hinges on two fundamental conditions of a valid sale: ownership by the seller and ability to deliver the object. We will unpack the reasoning and explore related cases.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the primary ruling for selling birds in the air as batil (void) in the apparent narration.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about batil vs fasid.
2
Examine the two scenarios
Analyze the two cases: before capture (not owned) and after capture and release (cannot deliver).
The reasoning (talil) is based on missing conditions.
3
Distinguish batil vs fasid
Understand the difference between batil (void ab initio) and fasid (defective) in this context.
The mufta bihi position is batil.
4
Apply to analogous cases
Consider game on land, honey, and trapped animals to see how ownership and delivery conditions operate.
Honey on land is owned because it settles, unlike fleeting birds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطير في الهواء سواء أخذه ثم أرسله أو لا، وسواء يرجع إليه بعد الإرسال أو لا في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأنه لو كان البيع قبل أخذه، فهو غير مملوك له، ولو كان بعد الأخذ والإرسال فهو ليس بقادر على التسليم، وهل هذا البيع باطل أو فاسد؟ قولان ولو اجتمع في أرضه الصيد، فباعه من غير أخذه لا يجوز؛ لأنه لم يملكه، ولو باض فيها صيد أو تكنس فيها ظبي، يكون لمن أخذه؛ لعدم ملكه إياه، بخلاف ما إذا عسل فيها النحل حيث يملكه؛ لأن العسل قام بأرضه على وجه القرار كالأشجار، وإن حفر في أرضه بئراً للاصطياد أو نصب شبكة، فدخل فيها صيد، أو تعلق بها ملكه وقيد الطير في الهواء؛ لأنه لو اصطاده ثم حبسه في مكان يمكن أخذه من غير حيلة جاز، وإن لم يمكن لا يجوز؛ لعدم القدرة على التسليم. (عيني، فتح)
And birds in the air, whether he caught them then released them or not, and whether they return to him after release or not, in the apparent narration; because if the sale was before catching, it is not owned by him; if it was after catching and release, he is not capable of delivery. And is this sale batil or fasid? Two opinions. And if game birds gather on his land, and he sells them without catching them, it is not permissible because he does not own them. And if a game bird lays eggs on his land or a gazelle curls up on his land, it belongs to whoever catches it because he does not own it. Unlike the case if bees make honey on his land, he owns it because honey settles on his land with stability like trees. And if he digs a well on his land for hunting or sets up a net, and game enters it or gets caught, he owns it. And the restriction 'birds in the air' because if he catches them then confines them in a place where they can be taken without a trick, it is valid; but if not possible, it is not valid due to inability to deliver. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of birds that are in the air — i.e., not yet captured. The ruling is that such a sale is not valid because the birds are not owned by the seller before capture, and even if he had caught and released them, he is not capable of delivery. There is a difference of opinion on whether the sale is batil (void) or fasid (defective). If birds gather on his land, he does not own them until actual capture; if they lay eggs or a gazelle takes shelter, the eggs or animal belong to whoever catches them. This is contrasted with honey from bees that settle on his land: the owner owns the honey because it becomes fixed like plants. If the seller digs a pit or sets a net, and game enters, he then owns it. For a captured bird confined so that it can be taken easily, the sale is valid; otherwise invalid due to inability to deliver.

Key Terms
الطير في الهواء (al-tayr fi al-hawa'): Birds that are flying and not captured or confined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, if a person catches a bird, releases it, and then sells it while it is flying, the sale is:
A) Valid
B) Batil (void)
C) Fasid (defective)
D) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
A sale is invalid if the seller does not own the object or cannot deliver it, as with birds in the air.

Selling Unborn Animals and Their Offspring
Ruling on selling unborn animal and expected offspring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling unborn animals and identify the key prophetic evidence that establishes its invalidity.

Core Idea
Selling an unborn animal is invalid because it is non-existent at contract—explicitly prohibited by the Prophet.

This lesson addresses a specific type of sale that was common in pre-Islamic Arabia: selling an animal still in the womb or its future offspring. The Hanafi school, based on clear hadith, declares such sales invalid (batil) due to the non-existence of the subject matter at the time of contract. We will examine the primary evidence and understand why this ruling is universally agreed upon among the schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the sale of an unborn animal (haml) and the sale of the offspring of that unborn (nitaj / habl al-habalah). The ruling is that such sales are invalid (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) forbade the sale of al-habl and habl al-habalah.' This is reported by Muslim, Ahmad, and Abu Dawud.
Additional hadiths in the passage reinforce the principle by prohibiting other uncertain sales.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates that the sale is invalid. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition of a transaction implies its nullity (fasād/buṭlān).
The sale is invalid because the object does not exist at the time of contract.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no significant disagreement among the schools; all major madhhabs agree that selling a non-existent object is invalid.
The passage notes that this practice was from Jahiliyyah and was abrogated by the prohibition.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
If such a sale is contracted, it is void ab initio. No ownership transfers, and any payment must be returned.
This ruling applies to both the unborn animal and its future offspring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحمل والنتاج: لنهيه عليه عن بيع الحبل وحبل الحبل، رواه مسلم وأحمد وأبو داود، فالحبل هو الحمل، والنتاج ما يحمل هذا الحمل وهو حبل الحبلة، وقد كانوا يعتادون ذلك في الجاهلية فأبطل ذلك بالنهي، ونهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن شراء ما في بطون الأنعام حتى تضع وعن بيع ما في ضروعها إلا بكيل، وعن شراء العبد وعن شراء الغنائم حتى تقسم وعن شراء الصدقات حتى تقبض وعن ضربة القانص، رواه أحمد والترمذي وابن ماجة.
And the unborn and the offspring: because of his (peace be upon him) prohibition of selling 'al-habl' and 'habl al-habalah', reported by Muslim, Ahmad, and Abu Dawud. 'Al-habl' is the unborn, and 'al-nataj' is what this unborn carries, i.e., 'habl al-habalah'. They used to do that in Jahiliyyah, and it was invalidated by the prohibition. And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade buying what is in the wombs of livestock until they deliver, and selling what is in their udders except by measure, and buying the runaway slave, and buying spoils until they are divided, and buying charity until it is received, and the throw of the hunter. Reported by Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that selling an unborn animal (al-haml) or its expected offspring (al-nitaj, i.e., the offspring of that unborn) is prohibited. It cites multiple hadith: the Prophet prohibited the sale of 'al-habl' (pregnancy) and 'habl al-habalah' (the offspring of that pregnancy), which was a practice in pre-Islamic times. The hadith also prohibits buying what is in the wombs until born, selling milk in udders except by measure, and other uncertain sales. The key principle: selling something that does not yet exist is invalid.

Key Terms
الحبل (al-habl): The unborn animal (fetus) in the womb.
حبل الحبل (habl al-habalah): The offspring of the unborn; i.e., a future uncertain generation.
النتاج (al-nitaj): The offspring of an unborn animal, synonymous with habl al-habalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Selling a pregnant animal.
B) Selling the offspring of an unborn animal.
C) Selling milk in the udder.
D) Selling an animal that has not been born yet.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that selling an unborn animal or its future offspring is batil because the subject matter does not exist at the time of contract, based on explicit prophetic prohibition.

Selling Pearls in the Shell
Ruling on selling pearls while still in the oyster shell

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the gharar in selling pearls in the shell and explain why the Hanafi fatwa follows Imam Muhammad's ruling of invalidity.

Core Idea
Pearls in the shell: invalid sale due to unknown existence and size – gharar.

In this passage, we examine a specific case of gharar (uncertainty) in sales – pearls still inside oyster shells. The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is invalid because the pearl's existence and quality are unknown. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf permits it while Imam Muhammad prohibits it. The relied-upon fatwa follows Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of the relied-upon view: selling pearls in the shell is invalid (batil) due to gharar.
Gharar is the uncertainty that invalidates a contract.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf's opposing opinion: the sale is permissible because breaking the shell is not harmful.
Abu Yusuf considers that the shell has no other use, so breaking it is not a loss.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that since the shell is only useful when broken, breaking it does not constitute harm (darar) that would prevent delivery.
The key is the classification of 'harm' (darar) in delivery.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Imam Muhammad rebuts that even if breaking is not harmful, the pearl itself remains unknown – its existence, size, and quality are uncertain. This uncertainty is gharar regardless of the shell.
The gharar is in the subject matter itself, not in the mode of delivery.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Yusuf, the contract is valid and binding; under Muhammad, it is void. The fatwa follows Muhammad: invalid.
Thus a buyer cannot enforce such a sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللؤلؤ في الصدف : لأن فيه غرراً ، وقد نهى عليه عنه ألا ترى أنه مجهول ولا يعلم وجوده في الصدف ولا قدره؛ ولأنه لا يمكن تسليمه إلا بضرر، وهو كسر الصدف، بخلاف بيع الحبوب في غلافها، فإنه جائز لكونها معلومة وكذا تراب الذهب إذا بيع بخلاف جنسه ، والصدف بالتحريك غشاء الدر، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز بيعه؛ لأن الصدف لا ينتفع به إلا بالكسر ، فلا يعد ضرراً، لكن الفتوى على قول محمد أنه لا يجوز؛ لأنه مجهول. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢/٢] (فتح)
And pearls in the shell: because there is gharar in it, and he (peace be upon him) forbade it. Do you not see that it is unknown, and one does not know its existence in the shell nor its size, and because it cannot be delivered except with harm, i.e., breaking the shell, unlike the sale of grains in their husks, which is permissible because they are known, and similarly gold dust if sold against a different type. Al-Sadaf (with vowel movement) is the covering of the pearl. Abu Yusuf said: its sale is permissible because the shell is not benefited from except by breaking, so it is not considered harm. But the fatwa is on the statement of Muhammad that it is not permissible because it is unknown. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/32] (Fath)
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of pearls that are still inside their oyster shells. The Hanafi ruling is that it is invalid due to gharar (uncertainty): one does not know if a pearl is inside, its size, or quality. Additionally, delivery requires breaking the shell, which damages the container. This is distinguished from selling grains in their husks, which is permissible because the grain is known, or selling gold dust in its raw form if sold against a different currency (since it can be assayed). Abu Yusuf differed, opining that the sale is permissible because the shell has no use except to be broken, so breaking it is not a harm. However, the relied-upon fatwa is according to Imam Muhammad: it is not permissible due to the uncertainty.

Key Terms
الصدف (al-sadaf): The shell or covering of a pearl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Muhammad consider the sale of pearls in the shell invalid?
A) Because the shell is impure.
B) Because the pearl is unknown and may not even exist.
C) Because only the state can sell pearls.
D) Because breaking the shell is wasteful.

Key Takeaway
The sale of pearls still in the shell is invalid due to gharar, following Imam Muhammad's view.

Selling Wool on the Sheep's Back: Nass Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on selling wool on the sheep's back

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wool on a living sheep and explain why Abu Yusuf's dissenting view is not the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Selling wool on a living sheep is invalid until cut — explicit hadith overrides analogy.

When can wool be sold while still on a sheep? The Hanafi school rules that such a sale is invalid, relying on a prophetic prohibition. However, Abu Yusuf made an exception if the wool is shorn immediately. Understanding this dispute reveals how explicit texts (nass) take precedence over qiyas, and how custom ('urf) can permit sales where no text exists.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The majority Hanafi position is that selling wool on a living sheep is invalid. The basis is a hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni in which the Prophet forbade selling wool until it is cut.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing view and his proof
Abu Yusuf permits the sale if the wool is shorn immediately at the time of sale. His proof is that the wool becomes deliverable immediately, analogous to selling leeks or poplar branches which are cut at sale.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response: why the analogy is rejected
The Hanafi response is that reasoning (ta'lil) in opposition to a clear text (nass) is rejected (mardud). The hadith is explicit, so analogy cannot override it.
The principle: 'لا اجتهاد مع النص' (no ijtihad against a text).
4
Understand the exception for leeks etc. and why it does not extend to wool
Leeks, poplar branches, willow, and mulberry leaves are permitted due to custom ('urf) and because there is no explicit prohibition text covering them. Wool has an explicit prohibition, so it cannot be analogized to them.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and why
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority Hanafi view that the sale is invalid. The tarjih is based on the strength of the hadith and the rejection of qiyas against nass.
This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصوف على ظهر الغنم لما روينا أنه عليه نهى أن يباع ثمر حتى يطعم وصوف على ظهر الغنم حتى يقطع ولبن في ضرع حتى يخرج وسمن في لبن"، رواه الدار قطني، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز بيعه بشرط جزه في الحال؛ لأنه مقدور التسليم في الحال، وبه قال مالك كما في الكراث وقوائم الخلاف قلنا التعليل بمقابلة النص مردود، وإنما أريد في الكراث وقوائم الخلاف والصفصاف وأوراق التوت بأغصانها للتعامل؛ إذ لا نص فيه، فلا يلحق به =
And wool on the back of sheep, because of what we narrated that he (peace be upon him) forbade selling fruit until it ripens, and wool on the back of sheep until it is cut, and milk in the udder until it is extracted, and ghee in milk. Reported by al-Daraqutni. And from Abu Yusuf that it is permissible to sell it with the condition of shearing it immediately, because it is capable of delivery immediately, and Malik said the same, as with leeks and poplar branches. We say: the reasoning in opposition to the text is rejected; rather what is intended in leeks, poplar branches, willow, and mulberry leaves with their twigs is due to custom, as there is no text on them, so it is not to be analogized to this.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage rules on selling wool that is still on a live sheep. The Hanafi position is that such a sale is invalid, based on a hadith that the Prophet forbade selling wool on the back of sheep until it is cut. Abu Yusuf, however, permitted it if the wool is to be shorn immediately upon sale, because it becomes deliverable at that moment. Imam Malik also reportedly allowed it by analogy with the sale of leeks and poplar branches (which are cut at sale). The response of the Hanafi commentators is that the analogy is invalid because the hadith provides an explicit text that overrides analogy. The exceptions for leeks and branches are based on custom and lack of prohibition text. Therefore, the ruling for wool remains prohibited according to the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
الصوف على ظهر الغنم (al-suf 'ala zahr al-ghanam): Wool that is still attached to a living sheep.
الكراث (al-kurrath): Leeks; an example of produce sold while still in the ground but cut at sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when can wool be sold?
A) Anytime, even before cutting.
B) Only after it is cut.
C) Only if the sheep is sold along with it.
D) Only in bulk orders.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits selling wool on a living sheep because an explicit hadith forbids it until cut; analogy (qiyas) cannot override a clear text (nass).

Selling Milk in the Udder: Hanafi vs Maliki
Ruling on selling milk in the udder and comparison with Imam Malik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits selling milk in the udder and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument using gharar and dalil.

Core Idea
Selling milk still in the udder is invalid due to prophetic prohibition and gharar, even if quantity is estimated.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of milk that is still in the udder. The Hanafi school, relying on a direct prophetic prohibition and the principle of gharar, declares such a sale invalid. Imam Malik, however, permits it under certain conditions. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how different proofs interact with the same contractual issue.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school rules selling milk in the udder as not permissible (invalid).
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam Malik permits the sale if the period is known and the quantity estimated.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand the Maliki basis: estimation removes uncertainty, making the sale valid.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Note that the Hanafi school rejects this because the hadith is explicit and gharar remains even with estimation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Realize the practical consequence: Hanafis void the sale entirely; Malikis validate it with conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللبن في الضرع: أي لا يجوز لما روينا، ويجوز عند مالك إذا كان أياماً معلومة لو علم قدر حلابها. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢/٢] ولنا: "أنه عليها فهى أن يباع لبن في ضرع حتى يخرج"، رواه الدار قطني؛ ولأن فيه غرراً؛ لجواز أن يكون الضرع منتفخاً من الريح أو الدم. والضرع لذات الظلف والخف من ذوات الأربع كالثدي للمرأة. (فتح)
And milk in the udder: it is not permissible based on what we have narrated (i.e., the hadith). And it is permissible according to Malik if it is for known days, if the amount of its milk is known. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 32/2] And our proof: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling milk in the udder until it is extracted,' reported by al-Daraqutni; and because there is gharar (uncertainty) in it, because the udder may be swollen from wind or blood. And the udder (dar') is for cloven-hoofed and padded-foot animals as the breast is for a woman. (Fath)
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding the sale of milk in the udder. The Hanafi position is that such a sale is not permissible. The proof is a hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni that the Prophet forbade selling milk in the udder until it is extracted. Additionally, there is gharar (uncertainty) because the udder may be swollen from wind or blood, and the actual amount of milk is unknown. Imam Malik allows it if the milk is for a known number of days and the quantity is estimated. The Hanafi school rejects this because the hadith and the element of gharar override any permissibility. The text also defines the udder (dar') as the milk-producing organ for animals with cloven hooves or padded feet, analogous to the human breast.

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk that may lead to dispute; a legal cause for invalidating a sale.
ضرع (ḍarʿ): Udder; the milk-producing organ of certain animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is unknown whether the milk will be extracted
B) Because of gharar and a prophetic prohibition
C) Because milk is not a commodity
D) Only because of gharar

Key Takeaway
Selling milk still in the udder is invalid (batil) in the Hanafi school due to a clear prophetic prohibition and the presence of gharar, and this ruling prevails over the Maliki view based on estimation.

Selling Wool on the Sheep
Ruling on selling wool on the sheep's back and Abu Yusuf's permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wool on the sheep, identify the hadith proof, and explain why Abu Yusuf's view is rejected.

Core Idea
Selling wool while still on the sheep is invalid because a hadith prohibits it until cut.

This lesson examines a classic fiqh issue: the sale of wool that is still attached to the sheep. A hadith from al-Daraqutni explicitly forbids such a sale until the wool is cut. However, Abu Yusuf argued for permissibility if the wool is sheared immediately. We will explore the proof, the dispute, and why the Hanafi school adheres to the explicit text.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that selling wool on the sheep before cutting is invalid (haram). The prohibition is from a hadith.
The hukm is haram, not simply makruh.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from al-Daraqutni that the Prophet forbade selling wool until it is cut. This is the core proof.
The hadith also includes fruit, milk, and butter.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'until it is cut' indicates that the wool must be separated from the animal to be sold; while attached, the sale is invalid. The prohibition (nahy) triggers invalidity in contracts.
In Hanafi usul, nahy in contracts indicates fasid/batil.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf permits the sale if the buyer shears immediately, arguing it becomes deliverable. He draws analogy to selling leeks and willow cuttings.
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Evaluate the rebuttal and tarjih
The majority reject Abu Yusuf's reasoning because it contradicts an explicit text. The examples of leeks etc. are allowed only because there is no text prohibiting them, so they are not comparable. Thus, the mufta bihi is the prohibition.
The principle 'al-ta'lil bi-muqabil al-nass mardud' is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصوف على ظهر الغنم لما روينا أنه عليه فهى أن يباع ثمر حتى يطعم وصوف على ظهر الغنم حتى يقطع ولبن في ضرع حتى يخرج وسمن في لبن"، رواه الدار قطني، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز بيعه بشرط جزه في الحال؛ لأنه مقدور التسليم في الحال، وبه قال مالك كما في الكراث وقوائم الخلاف قلنا التعليل بمقابلة النص مردود، وإنما أريد في الكراث وقوائم الخلاف والصفصاف وأوراق التوت بأغصانها للتعامل؛ إذ لا نص فيه، فلا يلحق به =
And wool on the back of the sheep: because of what we narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling fruit until it ripens, and wool on the back of the sheep until it is cut, and milk in the udder until it is extracted, and clarified butter in milk. Reported by al-Daraqutni. And from Abu Yusuf that he allows its sale on condition of shearing it immediately, because it can be delivered immediately; and Malik also said that, as in the case of leeks and the cuttings of the willow tree. We say: reasoning that opposes the text is rejected. The permissibility in leeks, willow cuttings, willow twigs, and mulberry leaves with their branches is only for customary dealing, because there is no text concerning them, so they are not analogous to the text.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the sale of wool on the sheep's back. The text cites a hadith from al-Daraqutni that the Prophet forbade selling fruit until it ripens, wool until it is cut, milk until extracted, and clarified butter in milk. Therefore, selling wool on the sheep is invalid. However, Abu Yusuf allows it if the buyer shears it immediately, as delivery becomes possible. Imam Malik also held this view, comparing it to the sale of leeks or willow cuttings, which are sold attached but can be cut immediately. The Hanafi response is that reasoning that contradicts a clear text (the hadith) is rejected. The examples of leeks, etc., are permitted only because there is no explicit prohibition for them, not by analogy to wool. Thus, the ruling for wool remains with the hadith.

Key Terms
صوف (ṣūf): Wool; the fleece on the sheep's back.
جز (jazz): Shearing or cutting the wool from the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, is it valid to sell wool on the sheep with the condition of immediate shearing?
A) Yes, following Abu Yusuf
B) No, because the hadith prohibits it
C) Yes, if the price is agreed beforehand
D) No, unless the sheep is also sold

Key Takeaway
Selling wool on the sheep is invalid until cut; the explicit hadith prohibits it and cannot be overridden by reasoning.

Selling a Cubit of Cloth: Conditions for Validity
Ruling statement on conditions for valid partial sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which selling a cubit from a cloth is valid in Hanafi fiqh and explain the distinction between cloth that is harmed by cutting and cloth that is not.

Core Idea
Selling a partial piece of cloth is valid if cutting does not harm the rest, or if the cutting is actually done before delivery.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the Hanafi ruling on selling a specific length (a cubit) from a piece of cloth. The validity depends on whether cutting harms the remaining cloth and whether the cutting is executed. This ruling is especially relevant for transactions involving fabric sold by the yard or meter. Let's break down the conditions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is selling a cubit (specific measured length) from a larger piece of cloth. The default ruling is that it is not permissible if cutting harms the cloth, because the sale of a part that damages the whole is akin to selling something with an attached harmful condition.
The harm is to the remaining cloth, not to the sold portion itself.
2
Distinguish between two types of cloth
The text distinguishes cloth that is harmed by cutting (e.g., cloth prepared for a garment) from cloth that is not harmed (e.g., cloth sold by the yard from a roll where cutting is standard). For the first type, the initial sale is invalid unless the cutting is done; for the second, the sale is valid from the outset.
The key factor is the customary effect of cutting on the value or usability of the remaining cloth.
3
Understand the effect of actual cutting and delivery
Even for cloth that is harmed by cutting, if the seller actually cuts the cubit and delivers it, the sale becomes permissible. This is because the harm is removed by the cutting itself; the remaining cloth is no longer part of the sale.
The cutting must occur before or at the time of delivery to validate the transaction.
4
Note the analogy to selling from a heap
The text compares selling a cubit from unharmed cloth to selling a qafiz (a measure) from a heap of grain. In both cases, the portion can be separated without harming the remainder, making the sale valid without needing to specify the exact portion at contract.
This analogy shows that the ruling is based on the general principle that part sales are valid when separation does not cause damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذراع من ثوب ذكر موضع القطع أو لا ; لأن التبعيض يضره، ولو قطع الذراع وسلمه جاز، وهذا في ثوب يضره القطع كالمهيأ للبس، وإن كان لا يضره القطع جاز بيع ذراع منه كالقفيز من الصبرة. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳/۲]
And a cubit of cloth: whether the place of cutting is mentioned or not, because the part that would be cut harms it. But if he cuts the cubit and delivers it, it is permissible. This applies to cloth that cutting harms, like that prepared for wearing. But if cutting does not harm it, then selling a cubit from it is permissible, like selling a qafiz (measure) from a heap. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 33/2]
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the sale of a cubit (dhirā') from a piece of cloth. The text distinguishes two types of cloth: one that is harmed by cutting (like cloth prepared for making a garment) and one that is not harmed. In the first case, the sale is not valid at the time of contract because cutting would harm the remaining cloth, but if the seller cuts the cubit and delivers it, the sale becomes permissible. In the second case, where cutting does not harm the cloth (e.g., cloth sold by the yard from a roll), selling a cubit is valid from the outset, similar to selling a specific measure (qafiz) from a heap of grain. The mention of the place of cutting is not required.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhirāʿ): A cubit, a unit of length (approximately 45-50 cm); here meaning a specific measured length of cloth.
قفيز (qafīz): A measure of volume or weight; here used as an analogy for selling a portion from a bulk quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At the time of contract, if the price is fixed
B) Only if the seller cuts the cubit and delivers it
C) Only if the buyer cuts it himself
D) Never, if cutting harms the cloth

Key Takeaway
The sale of a partial measure of cloth is valid either because cutting does not harm the remainder or because the cutting is executed before delivery.

Muzabanah and Related Prohibited Sales
Ruling on muzabanah and related prohibited sales with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muzabanah, mulaqalah, and mukhādarah, state the Hanafi ruling of prohibition, explain the illa of riba, and compare the Shafi'i exception of 'araya.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is forbidden due to the suspicion of riba in same-type estimation sales; the 'araya exception does not override the general prohibition.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays out three prohibited sales—muzabanah, mulaqalah, and mukhādarah—all involving estimation of like commodities or premature sale. The Hanafi school insists on strict prohibition, while the Shafi'i school allows a narrow exception for 'araya. Understanding the illa of riba is key to grasping the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify each prohibited sale and its definition
Read the Arabic definitions of muzabanah, mulaqalah, and mukhādarah. Note that all involve estimation (kharṣ) or sale before ripening.
Muzabanah: fresh dates for dried by estimation; Mulaqalah: wheat in ear for wheat by estimation; Mukhādarah: selling fruit before it becomes beneficial.
2
Understand the common element: estimation of same-type food
The shared illa (effective cause) is that the sale involves exchanging a same-type commodity (food) by estimation rather than exact measure or weight, leading to potential inequality (riba).
This is why the Hanafi school treats these as prohibited: they resemble riba al-fadl.
3
Analyze the hadith prohibition and its wajh al-dalalah
The hadith from Anas (ra) explicitly forbids these transactions. The prohibition (nahy) indicates invalidity. The mention of estimation shows the illa is gharar resembling riba.
The hadith is ahad but accepted; dalala is zanni but clear in meaning.
4
Consider the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i cites the 'araya hadith as an exception for small quantities (less than five awsuq). The Hanafi response confines 'araya to the case of gifts, not general sales, so the general prohibition remains.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis invalidate all such estimation sales, while Shafi'is allow them for 'araya up to 5 awsuq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمزابنة فحديث أنس له أنه عليها نهى عن المحاقلة والمخاصرة والمنابذة والملامسة رواه البخاري، والمحاقلة: بيع الحنطة في سنبلها بحنطة مثل كيلها خرصاً، فإنه لا يجوز؛ لكون بيع المكيل بالمكيل من جنسه بطريق الخرص، والمخاصرة – بالمعجمة والصاد -: بيع الثمار قبل أن تنتهي أي تصير منتفعاً بها، ولو لعلف الدواب، والمزابنة بيع الثمر على النخل بتمر محذوذ مثل كيله خرصاً، وهو لا يجوز لشبهة الربا، وقال الشافعي: يجوز المزابنة فيما دون خمسة أو سق؛ لما روي عن أنس أنه عليها نهى عن المزابنة بيع التمر بالتمر إلا أصحاب العرايا، فإنه أذن لهم، ولنا ما روينا، ومعنى العرايا فيما اه: العطايا، رواه البخاري والترمذي، وزاد فيه: وعن بيع العنب بالزبيب، وعن كل ثمر = رواه:
And muzabanah: the hadith of Anas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade mulaqalah, mukhādarah, munābadhah, mulāmasah. Reported by al-Bukhari. Mulaqalah: selling wheat in its ear for wheat by measure on estimation; it is not permissible because it is selling a measured commodity for its own type by estimation. Mukhadarah (with mu'jamah and sād): selling fruits before they ripen, i.e., before they become beneficial, even for animal fodder. Muzabanah: selling fruit on the palm tree for dried dates by measure on estimation; it is not permissible due to suspicion of riba. Al-Shafi'i said: muzabanah is permissible for less than five awsuq (measures), based on the hadith that the Prophet forbade muzabanah except for the owners of 'araya (gift trees), whom he permitted. And our proof is what we narrated. The meaning of 'araya is gifts. Reported by al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi, and added: 'And forbade selling grapes for raisins, and every fruit...'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining muzabanah and related prohibited sales. Muzabanah is the sale of fresh dates on the tree for dried dates by estimation, which is prohibited due to the suspicion of riba (since it is an exchange of the same type of food, dry for fresh, by estimation, which may lead to inequality). Also discussed are mulaqalah (selling wheat in ear for wheat by estimation) and mukhādarah (selling fruits before they ripen). All these are prohibited by hadith. The text mentions that Imam al-Shafi'i makes an exception for 'araya (gift trees) where the sale of fresh dates for dried dates is permitted in small quantities (less than five awsuq). The Hanafi school, however, adheres to the general prohibition, as their proof is the hadith that forbids muzabanah absolutely, and the 'araya exception is understood as a gift, not a sale. The text ends with additional prohibitions.

Key Terms
مزابنة (muzābanah): Selling fresh fruit on the tree for dried fruit of the same type by estimation (kharṣ).
محاقلة (muḥāqalah): Selling wheat in the ear for wheat by estimation.
عرايا (ʿarāyā): Trees gifted to someone; the recipient is allowed to sell the fresh fruit for dried fruit in small quantities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary reason for prohibiting muzabanah?
A) Because the fruit is not yet ripe
B) Because it involves estimation of same-type food, resembling riba
C) Because the Prophet forbade all sales of fruit on trees
D) Because the buyer cannot inspect the fruit

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits muzabanah due to suspicion of riba in same-type estimation sales; the 'araya exception is limited to gifts and does not override the general prohibition.

Selling a Beam in the Ceiling: Invalidity and Cure
Ruling on the invalidity and cure of selling a fixed beam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a beam fixed in a ceiling, explain the condition that cures the invalidity, and distinguish it from cases involving attached animal skin and seeds in watermelon.

Core Idea
A beam in the ceiling cannot be sold unless the seller removes it before rescission, because delivery requires causing harm.

Selling a beam that is part of a ceiling is not straightforward. The Hanafi school rules that such a sale is invalid due to the inability to deliver it without harming the structure. However, the seller can salvage the contract by physically removing the beam before the buyer cancels. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and compares it to other similar cases in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The act is selling a beam (jadh') that is fixed in a ceiling. The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajuz) — meaning the sale is fasid (invalid) at the time of contract.
The text begins 'والجذع في السقف' and declares the sale invalid.
2
Understand the reason (ʿilla) for invalidity
The invalidity is due to the inability to deliver the beam without causing harm (ḍarar) to the building. The text says: 'لأنه لا يمكن التسليم إلا بضرر يلحقه' — because delivery is impossible without harm.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the requirement that delivery must be possible without excessive harm.
3
Identify the condition that removes the invalidity
If the seller removes the beam and delivers it before the buyer rescinds, the sale becomes valid. The text states: 'ولو قلعه البائع وسلمه عاد صحيحاً إن كان قبل فسخ المشتري البيع؛ لزوال المانع' — if he removes it and delivers it, it returns to being valid if before rescission, because the impediment is removed.
Condition: removal and delivery before rescission.
4
Distinguish from similar cases
The text contrasts with two cases: (1) selling an animal skin still attached — even if the seller slaughters and skins it later, the sale does not become valid because the attachment is by creation (khilqah), making the sold item a subordinate. (2) selling seeds in a watermelon — even if split open and delivered, the sale remains invalid due to uncertainty (gharar) about the seeds' existence. In contrast, grains in husks can be sold because their existence is known.
These distinctions help clarify the exact scope of the rule: the inability to deliver must be temporary and not based on original creation or uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجذع في السقف: [ أي ولم يجز بيع الجذع إلخ، وهي القطعة من النخل أو من غيرها يوضع عليه الأخشاب. (عيني)] لأنه لا يمكن التسليم إلا بضرر يلحقه، ولو قلعه البائع وسلمه عاد صحيحاً إن كان قبل فسخ المشتري البيع؛ لزوال المانع، بخلاف ما إذا باع جلد الحيوان وذبحه وسلخ جلده، حيث لا . يعود صحيحاً؛ لأن المبيع وإن كان موجودا فيه، لكنه متصل بغيره اتصال خلقة، فكان تبعا له، فكان العجز عن التسليم هناك . أصليا. معنى وبخلاف ما إذا باع بذراً في البطيخ ونحوه حيث لا يجوز وإن شقه وسلمه؛ لأن فساده لاحتمال العدم، بخلاف الحبوب في غلافها حيث يجوز بيعها وإن كانت مستورة؛ لأن وجودها ،معلوم ولهذا يسمى الحبوب به، فيقال: هذا باقلاء، وهذه حنطة، ولا يقال للبطيخ : هذا بذر. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٣/٢] (فتح، عناية)
And the beam in the ceiling: [i.e., it is not permissible to sell the beam, etc., and it is a piece of palm tree or other wood upon which timbers are placed. (Ayni)] Because delivery is not possible except with harm that affects him, and if the seller removes it and delivers it, it becomes valid if it is before the buyer rescinds the sale; due to the removal of the impediment, unlike when he sells the skin of an animal and slaughters it and skins it, where it does not become valid again; because the sold item although existing within it, is connected to another by a connection of creation, so it is a subordinate to it, so the inability to deliver there is original. Meaning, and unlike when he sells seeds in a watermelon and the like where it is not permissible even if he splits it and delivers it; because its corruption is due to the possibility of non-existence, unlike grains in their husks where selling them is permissible even if they are covered; because their existence is known, and for this reason grains are named by it, so it is said: This is broad beans, this is wheat, and it is not said for watermelon: this is seeds. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 33/2) (Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on selling a beam (jadh') that is fixed in a ceiling. The commentary begins by stating that such a sale is invalid because delivery would require causing harm (e.g., removing the beam from the ceiling, which might damage the structure). However, if the seller removes the beam before the buyer rescinds the contract, the sale becomes valid because the impediment has been removed. This is contrasted with selling the skin of an animal that is still attached: even if the seller slaughters and skins it, the sale does not become valid because the sold item was originally connected by creation (khilqah), making it subordinate to the animal. Similarly, selling seeds inside a watermelon is invalid even if the watermelon is split open and delivered, because there is uncertainty (gharar) about the existence of the seeds. In contrast, grains in their husks can be sold because their existence is known and they are commonly referred to by the name of the grain (e.g., 'this is wheat'). The citation (Ramz al-Haqa'iq, Fath, Inayah) confirms this as the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الجذع (al-jadh'): A beam or piece of wood (from palm or other) placed horizontally to support the ceiling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller removes the beam from the ceiling before the buyer rescinds the contract, what happens to the sale?
A) The sale remains invalid because the initial obstacle is not removed.
B) The sale becomes valid because the impediment is removed.
C) The sale becomes valid only if the buyer agrees to it.
D) The sale remains invalid because the beam was originally part of the structure.

Key Takeaway
A beam fixed in a ceiling can be sold, but only if the seller removes it and delivers it before the buyer cancels the contract, thereby eliminating the harm that initially invalidated the sale.

Selling a Hunter's Strike (Batil due to Gharar)
Ruling on invalidity of selling hunter's strike with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a hunter's single strike and identify the specific conditions of valid sale that are violated.

Core Idea
A sale of unknown, non-existent items is void — gharar invalidates the contract.

This lesson examines a classical ruling on the sale of a hunter's single strike, where the object is unknown in quantity and existence. The Hanafi school declares such a transaction batil (void) due to gharar (excessive uncertainty) and lack of possession. The same rule applies to a diver's single dive for pearls. Understanding this ruling clarifies the importance of certainty in the subject matter of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being sold? The catch from a single cast of the hunter's net. The ruling is that such a sale is batil.
The act is 'bay' darbat al-qanis (sale of a hunter's strike).
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith narrated by Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi prohibiting such sales. This is the primary proof.
The exact wording of the hadith is not given but is referenced.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly prohibits the sale, indicating that it is invalid. The prohibition implies the ruling is batil.
In Hanafi usul, prohibition (nahy) leads to fasid or batil; here it is batil due to the nature of the contract.
4
Identify the rationale (illah)
The text gives two reasons: the object is majhul (unknown) and there is gharar (excessive risk). These are the effective causes of invalidity.
The same illah applies to the diver's dive.
5
Extend the ruling by analogy
The same ruling applies to the diver's single dive because the same illah of uncertainty exists.
This demonstrates qiyas: the diver's dive is analogous to the hunter's strike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضربة القانص [ أي ولم يجز بيع ضربة إلخ وهو ما يخرج من الصيد بضرب الشبكة مرة. (مسكين)] القنص محركا بالقاف والنون الصيد وجه عدم جواز بيع ضربة القانص: ما روي نهيه عليه كما مر في حديث أحمد والترمذي، وروي في تهذيب الأزهري" عن ضربة الغائص بالغين المعجمة والياء التحتية بعد الألف، وهو الغواص بأن يقول: أغوص لك غوصة فما أخرجته من اللآلي فهو لك بكذا، والبيع فيهما باطل؛ لأنه مجهول؛ ولأن فيه غرراً، والقانص هو الصائد بالآلة أعم من كونه في البر أو البحر، والغائص ضياد البحر. (عيني، فتح)
And the strike of the hunter [i.e., it is not permissible to sell the strike, etc., and it is what comes out of hunting by striking the net once. (Miskin)] The reason for the impermissibility of selling the hunter's strike is what is narrated of his prohibition as mentioned in the hadith of Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi, and it is narrated in Tahdhib al-Azhari concerning the strike of the diver (al-gha'is) – the diver (ghawas) says: I will dive for you once, and whatever pearls I bring out are yours for such and such. The sale in both is batil because it is unknown (majhul) and because there is gharar. The hunter (qanis) is the one who hunts with a tool, general to land or sea, and the diver is the hunter of the sea. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of a hunter's single strike (darbat al-qanis), meaning the catch resulting from one cast of the net. Such a sale is invalid due to uncertainty: the buyer does not know what, if anything, will be caught. The same ruling applies to a diver's single dive, where the diver agrees to give whatever pearls he retrieves for a fixed price. Both transactions are considered batil (void) because of ignorance (jahl) and gharar (excessive risk). The proof for the hunter's strike is a hadith narrated by Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi prohibiting it. The commentary also clarifies that 'qanis' is a general term for a hunter using a tool, whether on land or sea, while 'gha'is' specifically refers to a sea diver.

Key Terms
القانص (al-qāniṣ): A person who hunts using a tool such as a net, on land or sea.
الغائص (al-ghāʾiṣ): A diver who retrieves pearls from the sea.
غرر (gharar): Excessive risk or uncertainty in a transaction.
مجهول (majhūl): Unknown or indeterminate in essential terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hunter might not catch anything.
B) The sale is batil because it involves gharar and is prohibited by hadith.
C) The price is unknown.
D) The buyer does not own the prey.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a hunter's single strike is batil due to gharar and jahl, a principle that upholds certainty in transactions.
