Muzabanah and the 'Araya Exception: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on muzabanah and the 'araya exception with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi prohibition of muzabanah, identify the 'araya exception, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is invalid due to riba; 'araya is not a sale but a permissible gift.

This passage tackles a classic dispute over exchanging fresh fruit for dried by estimation. The Hanafi school absolutely prohibits muzabanah, citing the general hadith against such exchanges. The Shafi'i school, however, permits it for small amounts based on the 'araya exception. Understanding how each school reads the same hadith reveals the importance of usul principles in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on muzabanah according to the Hanafi school: it is prohibited due to shubhat al-riba (suspicion of interest). The key phrase in the passage is 'وهو لا يجوز لشبهة الربا'.
The prohibition is based on the hadith of Anas (ra) which prohibits muhaqalah, mukhadarah, munabadhah, and mulamasah.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i permits muzabanah for less than five awsuq, citing the hadith that exempts 'araya (gifted fruit trees) from the prohibition.
The passage states: 'وقال الشافعي: يجوز المزابنة فيما دون خمسة أو سق'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i relies on the report that the Prophet ﷺ prohibited muzabanah except for the owners of 'araya. The exemption implies that muzabanah is normally prohibited, but permissible in limited cases.
The opponent's dalil is the same hadith but with the addition: 'إلا أصحاب العرايا'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that the 'araya transaction is not a sale but a gift. The permission was a specific concession to avoid hardship, not a general permissibility for muzabanah. They contend that the narrator may have misunderstood the event and that the five awsuq limit was incidental.
The passage explains that the Hanafi view is that 'araya is a metaphorical sale; in reality it is a fresh gift (هبة مبتدأة).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Shafi'is allow small-scale exchanges of fresh dates for dried dates by estimation (within five awsuq), while Hanafis consider such transactions invalid and only allow the 'araya arrangement as a non-sale expedient.
This affects everyday transactions in agricultural communities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمزابنة فحديث أنس له أنه عليها نهى عن المحاقلة والمخاصرة والمنابذة والملامسة رواه البخاري، والمحاقلة: بيع الحنطة في سنبلها بحنطة مثل كيلها خرصاً، فإنه لا يجوز؛ لكون بيع المكيل بالمكيل من جنسه بطريق الخرص، والمخاصرة – بالمعجمة والصاد -: بيع الثمار قبل أن تنتهي أي تصير منتفعاً بها، ولو لعلف الدواب، والمزابنة بيع الثمر على النخل بتمر محذوذ مثل كيله خرصاً، وهو لا يجوز لشبهة الربا، وقال الشافعي: يجوز المزابنة فيما دون خمسة أو سق؛ لما روي عن أنس أنه عليها نهى عن المزابنة بيع التمر بالتمر إلا أصحاب العرايا، فإنه أذن لهم، ولنا ما روينا، ومعنى العرايا فيما اه: العطايا، رواه البخاري والترمذي، وزاد فيه: وعن بيع العنب بالزبيب، وعن كل ثمر = رواه: والملامسة، وإلقاء الحجر، وثوب من ثوبين، والمراعي وإجارتها محذوذ منه بخرصه", وبه قال أحمد، وفي الخمسة للشافعي قولان وفي الزائدة يبطل قولاً واحداً. وتفسير العرايا: أن يهب رجل ثمرة نخل من بستانه ولا يسلمها إليه ثم يشق على المعري دخول المعرى له في بستانه كل ساعة، ولا يرضى من نفسه أن يخلف الوعد، ويرجع فيه، فيعطيه قدره محذوذا بالخرص بدله، ليدفع ضرره عن نفسه، ولا يكون مخالفاً للوعد، وهو جائز عندنا، وسمي ذلك بيعاً مجازاً؛ لأن الموهوب له لم يملك الثمرة؛ لعدم القبض، فصار بائعاً ملكه بملكه، وهو في الصورة عوض مما أعطاه أولاً، وهو هبة مبتدأة في الحقيقة، وكأنه اتفق في الواقعة خمسة أوسق أو دونه فظن الراوي أن الرخصة مقصورة عليه، فنقل كما وقع عنده، وسكت عن السبب ويحتمل أن الراوي ظن أنه بيع. (عيني، فتح)
And muzabanah: the hadith of Anas that he (the Prophet) prohibited muhaqalah, mukhadarah, munabadhah, and mulamasah, reported by al-Bukhari. Muhaqalah: selling wheat in its ear for wheat by its estimated measure, it is not permissible because it is selling a measured foodstuff for the same kind by estimation. Mukhadarah: selling fruits before they ripen (i.e., become usable), even for fodder. Muzabanah: selling fresh dates on the palm for dried dates by estimated measure, it is not permissible due to the suspicion of riba. Al-Shafi'i said: Muzabanah is permissible for less than five awsuq, because of the report from Anas that he prohibited muzabanah except for the owners of 'araya, for whom he permitted it. Our evidence is what we have narrated. The meaning of 'araya is 'gifts' – reported by al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi, adding: 'and from selling grapes for raisins, and from every fruit' = reported: and mulamasah, ilqa' al-hajar, thobe from two thobes, and pastures and their rental, measured from it by its estimation. And Ahmad (Ibn Hanbal) held the same. Regarding the five awsuq, al-Shafi'i has two opinions, and for additional amounts he invalidates it in one opinion. Explanation of 'araya: A man grants the fruit of a palm tree from his orchard but does not hand it over; then it becomes difficult for the grantor for the grantee to enter his orchard every hour, and he is not satisfied to break his promise and take it back. So he gives him its estimated amount in exchange to remove harm from himself and not violate his promise. This is permissible with us. It is called a sale metaphorically because the donee did not own the fruit due to lack of possession, so he becomes a seller of his own property for his own property. In form it is compensation for what he gave first, but in reality it is a fresh gift. Perhaps in the incident it was five awsuq or less, so the narrator thought the concession was limited to that and narrated it as it occurred, but omitted the reason. The narrator might have thought it was a sale. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage explains the prohibition of muzabanah – exchanging fresh fruit on trees for dried fruit by estimation, which is invalid because it resembles riba (interest) due to the uncertainty in quantity. The Hanafi position is that it is absolutely invalid. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, permits it for amounts less than five awsuq, based on the hadith that exempts 'araya (gifts of fruit trees). The Hanafi response is that the general prohibition takes precedence over the exception, and the 'araya permission is not a sale but a metaphorical arrangement. The commentary explains that 'araya occurs when a person gives his palm tree's fruit to someone but doesn't deliver it, and then to avoid continuous entry into his garden, he compensates the donee with an estimated amount of dried dates. In Hanafi fiqh, this is not a real sale but a fresh gift. The reported hadith limited it to five awsuq, but the Hanafis hold that this was incidental to that specific case; the permission is general. Thus, the dispute between the schools is over the scope of the 'araya exception. The Hanafi madhhab maintains that muzabanah is invalid in all cases, and the 'araya transaction is not a sale but a legitimate expedient (hila) to avoid harm.

Key Terms
المزابنة (al-muzābanah): Exchanging fresh fruit on trees (like dates) for dried fruit of the same kind by estimation, not by precise measure.
العرايا (al-ʿarāyā): Singular 'ariyyah: a gift of a fruit tree where the donee does not take possession; the grantor compensates with an estimated amount of dried fruit to avoid ongoing entry into his garden.
خرص (kharṣ): Estimation of the quantity of fresh fruit on trees measured by its dried equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is muzabanah invalid?
A) Because it involves qimar (gambling).
B) Because it involves riba (interest).
C) Because the fruit is not yet ripe.
D) Because it is a pre-Islamic practice.

Key Takeaway
Muzabanah is absolutely prohibited in the Hanafi school due to riba; the 'araya transaction is a permissible gift, not a sale.

The Invalidity of Pre-Islamic Sales: Mulamasah, Ilqa' al-Hajar, and Munabadhah
Ruling on pre-Islamic invalid sales involving touch or throwing stones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three prohibited sales and explain why they are batil (void) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Sales based on touch or chance are batil because they lack proper consent and constitute gambling.

Before Islam, the Arabs practiced certain sales that relied on physical acts rather than clear verbal agreement. The Hanafi school classes these as batil (void) because they involve uncertainty (gharar) and gambling (qimar). In this lesson, we examine the rulings on mulamasah, ilqa' al-hajar, and munabadhah, and understand why they are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sales
From the text, list the three pre-Islamic sales: mulamasah (touch sale), ilqa' al-hajar (stone-throwing sale), and munabadhah (throwing goods sale). Understand the mechanism of each.
The text defines them briefly; refer to the commentary for fuller definitions.
2
Understand the ruling
The text states that these sales are prohibited (نهي عنها). Determine the hukm level: batil (void), not just makruh or haram with possibility of validity.
The ruling is batil because the defect is in the essence of the contract.
3
Analyze the legal reason (illah)
The text gives the illah: they involve suspending ownership on risk (تعليق التمليك بالخطر), which makes them qimar (gambling). Gambling is haram. Understand why this renders the contract batil.
4
Connect to broader principles
Recognize that these sales violate the requirement of clear offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) explicit or implicit with mutual consent. The Hanafi school requires certainty in the essential terms of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والملامسة: وهذه بيوع كانت في الجاهلية وهو أن يتساوم الرجلان على سلعة، فإذا لمسها المشتري أو وضع عليها حصاة أو نبذ إليها البائع لزم البيع، فالأول بيع الملامسة، والثاني إلقاء الحجر، والثالث المنابذة، وقد نهي عنها؛ ولأن فيها تعليقا للتمليك بالخطر، فيكون قمارا، والقمار حرام، وذكر في "المنتقى" قال أبو حنيفة: الملامسة أن يقول: أبيعك هذا المتاع بكذا، فإذا لمستك وجب البيع، أو يقول المشتري كذلك، وإلقاء الحجر بأن يقول المشتري أو البائع: إذا ألقيت الحجر وجب البيع [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤/٢] (مسكين) وإلقاء الحجر: أي ولا بيع بإلقاء الحجر، أي إذا وضع المشتري على السلعة حصاة لزمه البيع. (عيني)
And mulamasah: these are sales that existed in the Jahiliyyah, which is that two men bargain over a commodity, and if the buyer touches it or places a pebble on it or the seller throws it to him, the sale becomes binding. The first is the sale of mulamasah, the second ilqa' al-hajar, and the third munabadhah. They have been prohibited; because they involve the suspension of ownership on risk, so they are gambling, and gambling is haram. It is mentioned in al-Muntqa that Abu Hanifa said: Mulamasah is that one says: I sell you this commodity for such, and when I touch you the sale is binding, or the buyer says likewise. And ilqa' al-hajar is that the buyer or seller says: When I throw the stone, the sale becomes binding. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 34/2) (Miskin) And ilqa' al-hajar: i.e., there is no sale by throwing stones, when the buyer places a pebble on the commodity, the sale becomes binding. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains three types of pre-Islamic sales that are invalid in Islam: mulamasah (sale by touch), ilqa' al-hajar (sale by throwing a stone), and munabadhah (sale by throwing the goods). In mulamasah, the buyer's touching of the goods made the sale binding. In ilqa' al-hajar, the buyer or seller would throw a stone, and whichever item the stone hit became the sold item. All such practices are prohibited because they make ownership contingent on a risky act, which is akin to gambling (qimar), which is haram. The commentary cites Abu Hanifa's definition: the contract is formed by a statement linking the sale to a physical act (touching or throwing). The Hanafi ruling is that these contracts are batil (void) due to gharar and qimar.

Key Terms
الملامسة (al-mulāmasah): A sale where the buyer's touching the goods makes the sale binding, without a clear offer and acceptance.
إلقاء الحجر (ilqāʾ al-ḥajar): A sale where a stone is thrown and the sale becomes binding on the item the stone hits.
المنابذة (al-munābadhah): A sale where the seller throws the commodity to the buyer, and the sale is considered binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are mulamasah and ilqa' al-hajar invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because they involve selling before possession.
B) Because they involve uncertainty and gambling.
C) Because they are only permissible in the Jahiliyyah.
D) Because the goods are not specified.

Key Takeaway
Sales that are contingent on a physical act of touch or an element of chance are batil because they constitute gharar and qimar.

Selling One Unspecified Garment from Two: The Role of Khiyar al-Ta'yin
Ruling on one unspecified garment from two with khiyar al-ta'yin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling one unspecified garment from two and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
Selling one unspecified garment from two is invalid due to uncertainty, unless the buyer is given the option to choose (khiyar al-ta'yin).

This lesson examines a common sales scenario: selling one of two identical garments without specifying which. The Hanafi school rules this sale invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) of the subject matter, which leads to disputes. However, if the parties stipulate khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification), the uncertainty is resolved and the sale becomes valid. We will also look at the consequence if the buyer takes possession of both garments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is selling one unspecified item from a set of two. Without any additional stipulation, the sale is invalid (fasid) due to ignorance of the sold item.
This is the default position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The ruling changes from invalid to valid when the parties stipulate khiyar al-ta'yin, giving the buyer the right to choose which garment to take.
The condition is a contractual stipulation, not a mere intention.
3
Understand the reason (illah) for the ruling
The invalidity is because uncertainty (jahalah) leads to disputes. With khiyar al-ta'yin, the uncertainty does not lead to dispute because the choice is predetermined to be made later.
The illah is the potential for dispute, which is removed by the option.
4
Apply the consequence rule for fasid contracts
If the buyer takes possession of both garments and they are destroyed, the buyer must guarantee half the value of each. This is because a fasid contract is treated like a valid one in matters of liability (damān).
This is a secondary ruling derived from the principle 'al-fāsid muʿtabar bi al-ṣaḥīḥ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثوب من ثوبين بأن يقول: بعت منك أحد هذين الثوبين، فلا يجوز؛ لجهالة المبيع التي تفضي إلى المنازعة، وهذا إذا لم يشترط فيه خيار التعيين، فإن اشترطا فيه بأن اشترى أحدهما على أن يأخذ أيهما شاء جاز؛ لأن هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلو قبضهما وهلكا معاً، ضمن نصف قيمة كل؛ إذ الفاسد معتبر بالصحيح. (عيني، فتح، كفاية)
And a garment from two garments: such that he says: I sold you one of these two garments. This is not permissible due to ignorance of the sold item, which leads to dispute; this is when khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification) is not stipulated. If they stipulate it, such that he buys one of them on condition that he takes whichever he wishes, it is permissible; because this ignorance does not lead to dispute. If he takes possession of both and they are destroyed together, he guarantees half the value of each; for the invalid (fasid) is considered like the valid. (Ayni, Fath, Kifayah)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses selling one unspecified garment out of two. If the seller says 'I sell you one of these two garments' without specifying which, the sale is invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) of the subject matter, because it can lead to disputes. However, if the parties stipulate khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification), giving the buyer the right to choose which garment to take, the sale becomes valid. This is because the uncertainty does not lead to dispute when the choice is left to the buyer. The commentary adds that if the buyer takes possession of both garments and they are destroyed, he must guarantee half the value of each, because a fasid (invalid) contract is treated like a valid one in terms of liability for destruction.

Key Terms
خيار التعيين (khiyār al-taʿyīn): The option given to the buyer (or seller) to choose which specific item from a set they will take as the sold object.
جهالة (jahāla): Ignorance or uncertainty that affects the validity of a contract.
فاسد (fāsid): Invalid but not void; a contract that is defective and may be corrected or has legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does selling one of two garments become valid?
A) When the seller specifies which garment.
B) When the buyer chooses one immediately.
C) When the parties stipulate khiyar al-ta'yin.
D) It is never valid.

Key Takeaway
Selling an unspecified item from a set is invalid unless a choice option (khiyar al-ta'yin) is stipulated, and even then, if the buyer takes both, liability follows half-value per item.

Selling and Renting Natural Pasture (Kala')
Ruling on the impermissibility of selling or renting natural pasture (kala').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling naturally grown grass, identify the condition that makes it permissible, and explain the legal basis from the hadith.

Core Idea
Natural pasture is common property – selling it without possession is haram.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on selling or renting natural pasture. While it may seem that grass growing on one's land belongs to the owner, Islamic law places natural resources in common ownership unless they are actively cultivated or harvested. We will explore the hadith evidence and the crucial concept of possession (ihraz).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states it is not permissible (la yajuz) to sell the grass (kala') that grows in pastures, nor to rent the pastures. Determine the hukm level: haram (impermissible) due to lack of ownership.
The ruling applies only when the grass grows naturally and the owner has not taken possession.
2
Understand the reason for the ruling
The text explains: 'because the grass is not owned by him; he does not own it by its growing in his land unless he has harvested (ahraza) it.' The underlying legal cause ('illa) is the absence of ownership.
In Hanafi fiqh, sale requires the item to be owned by the seller.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith 'Muslims are partners in three: water, grass, and fire' establishes communal ownership of grass. The addition 'and its price is haram' reinforces the prohibition. This dalil shows that without possession (ihraz), the grass remains common property.
This hadith is ahad and zanni in thubut and dalala, but sufficient for this ruling.
4
Distinguish between naturally grown and cultivated grass
The passage contrasts: if the grass grows naturally (naba al-hashish binafsihi), it is not owned. However, if the owner cultivates it by irrigating, fencing, and preparing the land (anbatuhu), he becomes the owner and may sell it.
Cultivation is a form of ihraz (possession) that establishes ownership.
5
Define the scope of kala'
Kala' includes all vegetation that animals graze, fresh or dry, but excludes trees (ashjar) because they have trunks. Trees are owned by the landowner when they grow naturally and may be sold.
The distinction is based on the presence of a stem (saq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراعي وإجارتها: أي لم يجز بيع الكلأ الذي في مرعى الدواب، ولا يجوز إجارة المراعي أيضاً، وإنما لم يجز البيع والإجارة فيها؛ لأن الكلأ ليس بمملوك له؛ إذ لا يملكه بنباته في أرضه ما لم يحرزه؛ لقوله عليه: "المسلمون شركاء في ثلاثة في الماء والكلأ ، والنار"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود وابن ماجه من حديث ابن عباس ما وزاد فيه: " وثمنه حرام"، وهو محمول على ما إذا لم يحرزه؛ لأن الشركة ثابتة بالنص، فلا تنقطع بدون الحيازة. وهذا إذا نبت الحشيش بنفسه، وإن أنبته صاحب الأرض بأن سقاها أو حدق حولها، وهيأها للإنبات ملكه وجاز بيعه، ويدخل في الكلأ جميع أنواع ما يرعاه المواشي رطباً كان أو يابساً، دون الأشجار؛ لأن الكلاً اسم لما لا ساق له، والأشجار لها ساق، فلا تدخل في الكلاً، حتى جاز بيعها إذا نبت في أرضه؛ لأنه ملكها بالنبات فيها. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤/٢] (فتح)
And pastures and their rental: i.e., it is not permissible to sell the grass (kala') that is in the pasture of animals, nor is it permissible to rent the pastures. The reason is that the grass is not owned by him; he does not own it by its growing in his land unless he has harvested (ahraza) it, because of his saying: 'Muslims are partners in three things: water, grass, and fire', reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Ibn Majah from Ibn Abbas, and added: 'and its price is haram'. This is interpreted for when he has not harvested it; because partnership is established by text, so it does not end without possession. This applies when the herbage grows naturally. If the landowner cultivates it by irrigating or fencing and preparing it for growth, he owns it and its sale becomes permissible. All types of vegetation that animals graze, whether fresh or dry, are included in kala', but not trees; because kala' is a name for what has no trunk, and trees have trunks, so they are not included in kala'. Therefore, it is permissible to sell them if they grow on his land; because he owns them by their growing there. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 34/2) (Fath)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale and rental of natural pasture. The Hanafi ruling is that it is impermissible to sell grass (kala') growing on common land or to rent out pastures. The reason is that naturally grown grass is not owned by any individual; it is a common resource shared by all Muslims, based on the hadith: 'Muslims are partners in three: water, grass, and fire.' Therefore, one cannot sell what he does not own. However, if the landowner takes the effort to cultivate the grass by irrigating, fencing, and preparing the land, he becomes the owner and may sell it. The definition of kala' includes all types of vegetation that animals graze, whether fresh or dry, but excludes trees because they have trunks. Trees that grow naturally on one's land are owned and can be sold. This distinction emphasizes the importance of possession (ihraz) through physical effort to establish ownership over natural resources. The hadith's addition 'and its price is haram' reinforces the prohibition of selling unpossessed common resources.

Key Terms
الكلأ (al-kala'): Natural vegetation that animals graze, including both fresh and dry grass, but excluding trees.
الحيازة (al-ḥiyāzah): Taking possession or harvesting; physical control that establishes ownership over a natural resource.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the landowner own the grass growing on his land and may sell it?
A) As soon as it grows naturally.
B) After he cultivates it by watering and fencing.
C) Only after he harvests it.
D) He never owns it.

Key Takeaway
Selling or renting natural pasture is impermissible until the grass is possessed through cultivation or harvesting, as it is common property under the hadith.

The Ruling on 'Araya: Sale or Gift?
Commentary on the permissibility of 'Araya sale with estimation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on 'Araya and distinguish it from a regular sale, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
'Araya is a permissible concession; metaphorically a sale but actually a new gift to avoid hardship.

In this lesson, we examine the unique transaction known as 'Araya. The Hanafi school permits it as a concession to relieve the donor from hardship, while Imam al-Shafi'i has a differing view. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the nature of the transaction and the reasoning behind the concession.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'Araya
Read the source text to grasp the scenario: a donor gifts fruit but keeps possession, then later gives an estimated equivalent to avoid inconvenience. Note that this is called a sale metaphorically.
The key is that the donee never took possession of the original gift.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
The text states it is permissible (ja'iz) according to us (Hanafis). Recognize that the ruling is based on necessity and the avoidance of harm.
The Hanafi school does not restrict it to a specific quantity.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i has two sayings: one allowing, one prohibiting. He also invalidates the excess beyond five wasq. Understand his basis: prohibition of selling fruit before delivery.
The narrator reported the incident of five wasq, so the concession might be thought limited.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the concession is general, not restricted to five wasq, and the transaction is not a real sale but a gift. Thus, the prohibition on selling before delivery does not apply.
This resolves the apparent contradiction with the general principle.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, Hanafis allow 'Araya for any amount, while Shafi'is may restrict or prohibit it. This difference affects day-to-day transactions in fruit-giving scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محذوذ منه بخرصه"، وبه قال أحمد، وفي الخمسة للشافعي قولان وفي الزائدة يبطل قولاً واحداً. وتفسير العرايا: أن يهب رجل ثمرة نخل من بستانه ولا يسلمها إليه ثم يشق على المعري دخول المعرى له في بستانه كل ساعة، ولا يرضى من نفسه أن يخلف الوعد، ويرجع فيه، فيعطيه قدره محذوذا بالخرص بدله، ليدفع ضرره عن نفسه، ولا يكون مخالفاً للوعد، وهو جائز عندنا، وسمي ذلك بيعاً مجازاً؛ لأن الموهوب له لم يملك الثمرة؛ لعدم القبض، فصار بائعاً ملكه بملكه، وهو في الصورة عوض مما أعطاه أولاً، وهو هبة مبتدأة في الحقيقة، وكأنه اتفق في الواقعة خمسة أوسق أو دونه فظن الراوي أن الرخصة مقصورة عليه، فنقل كما وقع عنده، وسكت عن السبب ويحتمل أن الراوي ظن أنه بيع. (عيني، فتح)
Measured from it by estimation. And Ahmad said the same. Concerning the five (wasq), Al-Shafi'i has two sayings, and regarding the excess, he invalidates it with one saying. The interpretation of 'Araya: that a man gifts the fruit of a palm tree from his orchard and does not hand it over to him, then it becomes difficult for the donor for the recipient to enter his orchard every hour, and he does not consent to himself to break his promise or retract, so he gives him its equivalent measured by estimation as compensation, to avoid harm to himself and not to be contrary to the promise. It is permissible according to us. It is called a sale metaphorically because the donee has not owned the fruit due to lack of possession, so he becomes a seller of his own property with his property, and in form it is a substitute for what he gave initially, but in reality it is a new gift. It happened that the incident involved five wasq or less, so the narrator thought that the concession was limited to that, so he transmitted it as it occurred, and remained silent about the reason. It is possible that the narrator thought it was a sale.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on 'Araya, which is a concession in the Hanafi school. It begins by stating the matn phrase 'محذوذ منه بخرصه' meaning the fruit is estimated and then sold back. The commentator mentions that Imam Ahmad agreed with this concession, while Imam al-Shafi'i had two views on the matter: one allowing it and the other prohibiting it. The text then defines 'Araya: a person gifts the fruit of his palm tree to another but does not hand it over. Later, the donor finds it difficult to allow the recipient into his orchard frequently, and he does not wish to break his promise. To avoid hardship, he gives the recipient an estimated amount of fruit as compensation. This is permissible in the Hanafi school. Although it is called a sale (bay'), it is metaphorically so because the donee never actually took possession of the original gift, so he is selling something he did not own. In reality, it is a new gift. The narrator of the hadith may have thought the concession was limited to a specific incident involving five wasq or less, hence the transmission. The commentator clarifies that the ruling is general.

Key Terms
العرايا (al-'araya): A specific type of transaction where the owner of fruit gifts it to someone, then buys it back by estimated amount to avoid hardship.
محذوذ (mahdhudh): Measured or estimated by estimation (khars).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on 'Araya (selling fruit by estimation)?
A) It is prohibited because it involves selling fruit before delivery.
B) It is permissible and called a sale metaphorically; in reality it is a gift.
C) It is only permissible if the amount is five wasq or less.
D) It is valid only if the buyer takes possession immediately.

Key Takeaway
'Araya is a permissible Hanafi concession to avoid hardship; it is not a real sale but a gift, and is not limited to a specific quantity.

The Sale of Bees and Beehives: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of bees as beneficial animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the two shaykhs and Imam Muhammad regarding the sale of bees, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Bees can be sold if confined and beneficial — the fatwa follows Muhammad's view, not the two shaykhs.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over the sale of bees and beehives. While Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider bees as unsaleable vermin, Imam Muhammad permits their sale when confined and useful. Understanding this dispute reveals how the school weighs necessity (hajah) and benefit (manfaah) in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of sale
Determine what is being sold: bees alone, beehives with or without honey, or bees as an appurtenance.
The ruling may differ depending on whether honey is present.
2
State the position of the two shaykhs
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf prohibit the sale of bees, whether alone or with hives, unless honey is present (the hives then can be sold as containers). Their reasoning: bees are vermin (hawamm) like wasps.
They also distinguish hives with honey vs. without.
3
State the position of Imam Muhammad
Muhammad permits the sale if bees are confined (muhraz) because they are useful animals capable of delivery. He analogizes to a donkey, which is not eaten but can be sold due to benefit.
Al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik agree with Muhammad.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the fatwa follows Muhammad, allowing beekeeping trade. Had the two shaykhs' view prevailed, selling bees would be invalid.
The text explicitly says 'وبه يفتى' (and it is the fatwa).
5
Apply the overarching principle
The permissibility of sale revolves around the permissibility of benefit. If an animal is beneficial and can be delivered, its sale may be allowed by istihsan despite general prohibition of selling vermin.
This principle also explains exceptions for leeches and certain sea creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنحل: [ أي ولم يجز بيع النحل وهو دود العسل عند الشيخين. (عيني)] أي لم يجز بيع النحل لا مقصوداً ولا تبعاً للكوارات، أي لم يجوز بيوت النحل إذا لم يكن فيها عسل على قول الشيخين، واختلف في بيعه للكوارات إذا كان فيها عسل لا مطلقاً، وقال صاحب "المجمع": يجوز بيع النحل تبعاً للكوارات عند الشيخين إذا كان فيها عسل، وإن لم يكن فيها عسل فلا يجوز عندهما، سواء كان مجموعاً أو لم يكن، وعند محمد والشافعي: يجوز بيعه إذا كان محرزاً؛ لأنه حيوان منتفع به مقدور التسليم، وإن كان لا يؤكل كالحمار فيجوز للحاجة، وبه قال أحمد ومالك وبه يفتى ولهما أنه من الهوام فلا يجوز بيعه كالزنبور والانتفاع بما يخرج منه لا بعينه. وجوز أبو الليث بيع العلق، وبه يفتى؛ للحاجة بخلاف غير النحل والعلق من الهوام كحيات وضب وعقارب والوزغ والقنافذ وغيرها، فلا يجوز بيعها اتفاقاً إلا ما جاز الانتفاع بجلده أو عظمه، والحاصل: أن جواز البيع يدور مع حل الانتفاع وقالوا: لا يجوز بيع كل شيء من حيوانات البحر من ضفادع والسرطان وغيرهما إلا السمك (عيني، فتح)
And bees: [i.e., it is not permissible to sell bees, which are the worms of honey, according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).] i.e., it is not permissible to sell bees neither alone nor as an appurtenance to hives, i.e., beehives are not permissible to sell if they contain no honey according to the two shaykhs. There is a difference of opinion regarding their sale when they are in hives containing honey, but not absolutely. The author of al-Majma' said: It is permissible to sell bees as an appurtenance to hives according to the two shaykhs if there is honey, but if there is no honey it is not permissible according to them, whether gathered or not. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is permissible to sell them if they are confined, because it is a useful animal capable of delivery, even if it is not eaten, like a donkey, it is permissible due to need. Ahmad and Malik also said this, and it is the fatwa. Their (the two shaykhs') argument: it is among vermin (hawamm), so its sale is not permissible like the wasp, and the benefit derived from it is not in its essence. Abu al-Layth permitted the sale of leeches, and the fatwa is on that due to need, contrary to other vermin like snakes, lizards, scorpions, geckos, hedgehogs, etc., whose sale is not allowed by agreement, except where benefit from skin or bone is permissible. The conclusion: the permissibility of sale revolves around the permissibility of benefit. And they said: It is not permissible to sell any sea animal except fish.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of bees (al-nahl) and beehives (al-kawarat). According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), selling bees alone is not permissible because they are considered vermin (hawamm) like wasps. However, if bees are confined (muhraz) and there is benefit, Muhammad and al-Shafi'i allow their sale, and the fatwa is on this view. Similarly, beehives without honey cannot be sold according to the two shaykhs, but if honey is present, they differ. The permissibility ultimately depends on whether the animal is beneficial and capable of delivery. Other vermin like snakes, scorpions, etc., are not salable unless their skin or bone is usable. Sea creatures other than fish are also not salable.

Key Terms
النحل (al-nahl): Bees, considered by some as insects producing honey.
الكوارات (al-kawarat): Beehives, the containers where bees live.
الهوام (al-hawamm): Vermin, small harmful creatures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that it is not permissible.
B) The view of Muhammad that it is permissible if the bees are confined.
C) The view of al-Shafi'i that it is always permissible.
D) The view that only beehives with honey can be sold.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa permits the sale of bees if they are confined and beneficial, following Imam Muhammad's view.

Selling Silkworms and Their Eggs: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with tarjih and subsidiary rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on selling silkworms and eggs, and identify the mufta bihi with its justification.

Core Idea
Silkworms are valuable property; their sale is permissible on Muhammad's view, which is the fatwa.

Silkworms and their eggs are a unique commercial item. Although they are insects, they produce valuable silk. The Hanafi school debated their status as saleable property: Abu Hanifa prohibited it, Abu Yusuf conditioned it on the appearance of silk, while Muhammad allowed it unconditionally. This lesson unpacks that ikhtilaf and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
State the rulings of Abu Hanifa (prohibition), Abu Yusuf (conditional permission), and Muhammad (unconditional permission) regarding the sale of silkworms and their eggs.
Note the reasoning for each: worms are vermin, eggs have no independent benefit, etc.
2
Analyze the tarjih
The text declares 'والفتوى على قول محمد' – the fatwa is on Muhammad's view. Understand why: later Hanafis adopted it due to prevalent custom and benefit.
This reflects the principle that fatwa can shift to meet changing needs.
3
Examine the salam ruling
Salam (advance sale) of silkworms by weight is valid only within their season, because delivery must be possible at the agreed time.
Out-of-season salam is invalid due to inability to deliver.
4
Apply the liability principle
Muhammad holds the killer of silkworms liable since they are mal mutaqawwim. This confirms their status as valuable property.
Abu Hanifa's contrary report is not relied upon.
5
Extend to analogous partnerships
The arrangements for silkworm eggs, cows, and hens on a profit-sharing basis all follow the same rule: the offspring/produce belongs to the owner of the original asset, and the worker gets compensation for inputs.
This is a manifestation of the maxim: al-nama' li'l-asl (growth belongs to the origin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويباع دود القز وبيضه: أي يجوز بيعهما عند محمد والثلاثة، وعند أبي حنيفة: لا يجوز بيعهما، أما الدود؛ فلأنه من الهوام، وأما بيضه؛ فلأنه غير منتفع به باعتبار ذاته بل باعتبار غيره، وذلك الغير معدوم، وقال أبو يوسف: يصح بيع الدود إذا ظهر القز فيه وإلا لا، واضطرب في بيضه والفتوى على قول محمد، فلذلك نص عليه الشيخ بالجواز، ويجوز السلم فيه كيلاً إذا كان في وقته وجعل أجله في وقته، أما بعد وقته، فلا يجوز السلم؛ لعدم قدرته على التسليم. وقال محمد: يضمن من قتل دود القز؛ لكونه مالاً متقوماً، وإتلافه مضمون، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا يضمن قتله، ولو أن امرأة أعطت بذر القز وهو بذر الفيلق بالنصف إلى امرأة فقامت عليه حتى أدرك، فالفيلق لصاحبة البذر؛ لأنه حدث من بذرها، ولها على الأخرى قيمة الأوراق وأجرة مثلها، وعلى هذا إذا دفع البقرة إلى إنسان بالعلف ليكون الحادث بينهما بالنصف، فالحادث كله لصاحب البقرة وله على صاحبها ثمن العلف وأجرة المثل، وكذا إذا دفع الدجاجة؛ لتكون البيضة بالنصف. (عيني، فتح)
And silkworms and their eggs are sold: i.e., it is permissible to sell them according to Muhammad and the three (Abu Yusuf, Shafi'i, etc.), but according to Abu Hanifa it is not permissible to sell them. As for the worms, because they are vermin; as for the eggs, because they are not beneficial in themselves but by something else, and that something else is nonexistent. Abu Yusuf said: The sale of the worms is valid if silk appears in them, otherwise not, and he had different opinions about the eggs. The fatwa is on Muhammad's saying, so the author (of Kanz) stated it as permissible. Salam (advance sale) in them by weight is permissible if it is in their season and the delivery time is set in the season; after the season, salam is not allowed due to inability to deliver. Muhammad said: Whoever kills silkworms is liable for their value because they are valuable property, and destruction is compensated. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he does not hold him liable. If a woman gives silkworm eggs (mulberry seeds?) to another woman on the condition of half the produce, and the other tends them until they mature, then the produce belongs to the owner of the seeds, and she has a claim for the value of the leaves and her labor. Similarly, if one gives a cow to another to feed and the offspring is shared half, the offspring belongs to the owner of the cow, and the feeder gets the cost of fodder and wages. Likewise for a hen given to produce eggs half.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of silkworms (dood al-qazz) and their eggs. According to Abu Hanifa, selling them is not permissible because worms are vermin and eggs are not independently beneficial. Abu Yusuf conditioned the validity on the emergence of silk. However, Muhammad allows their sale, and the fatwa is on his view. Silkworms are considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), so killing them incurs liability. The passage also covers salam (advance sale) of silkworms by weight, which is valid within their season. Additionally, it provides analogical rulings for partnerships involving animals: if a person gives silkworm eggs to another for cultivation on a profit-sharing basis, the produce belongs to the owner of the eggs, and the worker is entitled to the costs of leaves and labor. Similar rules apply to cows and hens given for feeding or egg production.

Key Terms
دود القز (dood al-qazz): Silkworms, the larvae that produce silk.
بذر القز (badhr al-qazz): Silkworm eggs (or seeds of the mulberry?); the starting material for silk production.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property that can be legally traded and compensated if destroyed.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment sale where goods are delivered later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always permissible.
B) No, never permissible.
C) Only permissible if silk appears in them.
D) Only permissible if sold by weight.

Key Takeaway
Silkworms and their eggs are permissible to sell based on Muhammad's view, which is the fatwa, due to their status as valuable property and custom.

Selling a Runaway Slave: General Prohibition and Exception
Ruling on selling a runaway slave and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling on selling a runaway slave, identify the dalil, and apply the exception when the seller claims possession.

Core Idea
A runaway slave cannot be sold unless the seller claims the slave is in his possession at the time of contract.

The Hanafi school, based on a prophetic prohibition, rules that selling a runaway slave is invalid due to inability to deliver. However, there is a key exception: if the seller affirms that the slave is with him, the sale becomes valid. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the logical condition of possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Determine what the passage rules on: the sale of a runaway slave (al-abiq). The default ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites a hadith: the Prophet forbade selling a slave while he is a runaway. Also a logical reason: inability to deliver.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but its indication is clear.
3
Understand the exception
The text says 'except that he sells him (i.e., the runaway) to someone whom the seller claims the slave is with him'. This makes the sale valid because the slave is not a runaway in the seller's view.
The prohibition only applies when the slave is truly absconding from both parties.
4
Contrast with a slave on an errand
A slave sent on an errand is saleable because he is deemed capable of delivery at the time of contract, as the presumption is he will return.
This contrast highlights the condition of ability to deliver.
5
Analyze the buyer's possession scenario
If the buyer already has the slave in his possession and took him with witnesses to return him to the master, the sale is concluded by the contract alone.
This part is cut off in the source but indicates another exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآبق [بالجر عطف على دود القز، ولم يجز أيضاً بيع العبد الآبق. (عيني)] أي لا يجوز أيضاً بيع العبد الآبق؛ لأنه عليه نهى عن بيع العبد وهو آبق؛ ولأنه لا يقدر على التسليم، وهو شرط جواز البيع، بخلاف العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى، فإنه يجوز بيعه؛ لأنه مقدور التسليم وقت العقد حكماً؛ إذ الظاهر عوده، وقوله: "إلا أن يبيعه" أي لا يجوز الآبق إلا أن بيع يبيع الآبق ممن يزعم البائع أن العبد عنده، فإن هذا البيع يجوز؛ لأن النهي ورد في العبد الآبق وهو أن يكون آبقاً عند المتعاقدين، وهذا ليس بآبق في حق من هو في يده، فلا يتناوله النص المطلق. المشتري قابضاً . يصير بمجرد العقد إذا كان في يده بشرط إن كان أشهد عند الأخذ أنه يأخذه ليرده على = المطلق، ثم لا
And the runaway (slave): [in the genitive, referring to silkworms; i.e., the sale of a runaway slave is also not permissible.] i.e., it is not permissible to sell a runaway slave because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling a slave while he is a runaway, and because he cannot deliver, which is a condition for the permissibility of sale. This is unlike the slave sent on an errand of the master, whose sale is permissible because he is deemed to be capable of delivery at the time of contract, as the presumption is that he will return. His saying: 'except that he sells him' meaning: the runaway is not permissible except if he sells the runaway to someone whom the seller claims the slave is with him, then this sale is permissible. Because the prohibition came regarding the runaway slave, i.e., one who is a runaway at the time of the contracting parties, but this (slave) is not a runaway in the eyes of the one in whose hands he is, so the absolute text does not include him. The buyer becomes possessor by the contract alone if he is in his hand, provided that when he took him, he had witnesses that he took him to return him to the absolute (master?), then not?
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage rules on selling a runaway slave (al-abiq). The default ruling is that such a sale is not permissible because the seller cannot deliver the slave, and the Prophet forbade it. However, there is an exception: if the seller claims that the slave is currently with him (i.e., he has possession of the slave), then the sale is valid because the slave is not actually a runaway at the time of sale. The prohibition applies only to a slave who is truly absconding from the contracting parties. The last part discusses that if the buyer already has the slave in his possession, the sale is concluded by the contract alone, provided that when he took the slave, he made it known (with witnesses) that he was taking him to return him to the master. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abiq): A slave who has run away from his master.
القدرة على التسليم (al-qudra 'ala al-taslim): Ability to deliver the sold item at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when is the sale of a runaway slave permissible?
A) Never permissible.
B) Permissible if the buyer can locate the slave.
C) Permissible if the seller claims that the slave is with him (in his possession).
D) Permissible if the slave is sent on an errand.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a runaway slave is invalid unless the seller claims possession at the time of contract.

Sale of Bees and the Principle of Permissible Benefit
Ruling on sale of bees with inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the sale of bees, explain the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute, and apply the maxim that sale permissibility depends on the permissibility of use.

Core Idea
Bees can be sold if confined because they are beneficial and deliverable, even if non-edible.

Selling live bees presents a fiqh puzzle: they are insects, not livestock, yet they provide valuable honey and pollination. The Hanafi school has an internal split between the two shaykhs, who view bees as vermin, and Muhammad, who permits their sale by analogy with donkeys. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows how the later fatwa resolved the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two shaykhs' ruling
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the sale of bees is not permissible, either alone or as an appurtenance to beehives, unless honey is present in the hive.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Examine Muhammad's counter-opinion
Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with al-Shafi'i and others, permits the sale of bees if they are confined, using qiyas with the donkey – a non-edible yet beneficial animal.
The key condition is 'confined' (muhraz) – meaning the bees can be delivered.
3
Trace the qiyas argument
Identify the asl (donkey), the far' (confined bees), and the illah (benefit and deliverability). Understand why the two shaykhs reject the analogy.
4
Apply the same principle to leeches
Abu al-Layth permitted the sale of leeches due to medical necessity, following the general rule that sale permissibility turns on permissible use.
This is an exception; other vermin like snakes are unanimously non-saleable.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon fatwa is Muhammad's view: confined bees may be sold. The commentator explicitly states 'and by it the fatwa is given'.
This shows how later Hanafi practice adopted the more lenient view to meet public need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنحل: أي لم يجز بيع النحل لا مقصوداً ولا تبعاً للكوارات، أي لم يجوز بيوت النحل إذا لم يكن فيها عسل على قول الشيخين، واختلف في بيعه للكوارات إذا كان فيها عسل لا مطلقاً، وقال صاحب "المجمع": يجوز بيع النحل تبعاً للكوارات عند الشيخين إذا كان فيها عسل، وإن لم يكن فيها عسل فلا يجوز عندهما، سواء كان مجموعاً أو لم يكن، وعند محمد والشافعي: يجوز بيعه إذا كان محرزاً؛ لأنه حيوان منتفع به مقدور التسليم، وإن كان لا يؤكل كالحمار فيجوز للحاجة، وبه قال أحمد ومالك وبه يفتى ولهما أنه من الهوام فلا يجوز بيعه كالزنبور والانتفاع بما يخرج منه لا بعينه. وجوز أبو الليث بيع العلق، وبه يفتى؛ للحاجة بخلاف غير النحل والعلق من الهوام كحيات وضب وعقارب والوزغ والقنافذ وغيرها، فلا يجوز بيعها اتفاقاً إلا ما جاز الانتفاع بجلده أو عظمه، والحاصل: أن جواز البيع يدور مع حل الانتفاع
And bees: that is, the sale of bees is not permitted, neither independently nor as an appurtenance to beehives, i.e., it is not permissible to sell beehives if there is no honey in them according to the two shaykhs. And there is a difference of opinion regarding their sale as appurtenance to beehives if there is honey in them—not absolutely. The author of al-Majma' said: The sale of bees as appurtenance to beehives is permissible according to the two shaykhs if there is honey in them; if there is no honey, it is not permissible according to them, whether they are gathered or not. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i: its sale is permissible if it is confined, because it is a beneficial animal capable of delivery, even if it is not eaten, like a donkey—it is permissible due to necessity. Ahmad and Malik also held this, and it is the fatwa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is among the vermin so its sale is not permissible, like the wasp, and the benefit from what it produces is not from itself. Abu al-Layth permitted the sale of leeches, and it is the fatwa due to necessity, in contrast to other than bees and leeches among vermin like snakes, lizards, scorpions, geckos, hedgehogs, etc., their sale is not permissible by agreement, except what is permissible to benefit from its hide or bone. The conclusion: the permissibility of sale revolves around the permissibility of benefit.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling bees (النحل) and similar vermin. It begins by stating the position of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) that selling bees is not allowed, whether alone or as part of a beehive without honey. The author of al-Majma' clarifies that if honey is present, selling bees as an appurtenance is permissible. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam al-Shafi'i, along with Ahmad and Malik, permit the sale of bees if they are confined, analogizing them to a donkey—a non-edible but beneficial animal whose sale is allowed out of necessity. The two shaykhs argue that bees are vermin, like wasps, and thus not salable. The passage then notes that Abu al-Layth permitted the sale of leeches (العلق) due to necessity, but for other vermin like snakes, lizards, etc., sale is not permitted by consensus unless the hide or bone is beneficial. The overarching principle is that the permissibility of sale depends on the permissibility of using the item (جواز البيع يدور مع حل الانتفاع). This is a key Hanafi usuli principle applied to commercial transactions.

Key Terms
النحل (al-nahl): Bees; in this context, the insect that produces honey.
الكوارات (al-kawwarat): Beehives; the structures in which bees live and produce honey.
العلق (al-'alaq): Leeches; a type of worm used for bloodletting or medical purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling bees that are confined?
A) It is not permissible under any condition.
B) It is permissible because they are beneficial and capable of delivery.
C) It is only permissible if sold with the beehive and honey.
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of selling an animal depends on whether it provides lawful benefit and can be delivered, not on whether it is edible.

Sale of Silkworms: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf and Its Practical Application
Intra-Hanafi dispute on silkworm sale with subsequent qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on selling silkworms, identify the mufta bihi, and apply the qiyas of livestock sharing to similar partnerships.

Core Idea
Silkworms are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) — their sale is permitted on Muhammad's view, which is the fatwa.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a niche but instructive fiqh issue: the sale of silkworms and their eggs. The Hanafi school has an intra-madhhab dispute on whether these are salable. Imam Muhammad considers them valuable property; Abu Hanifa deems them vermin; Abu Yusuf has a conditional opinion. The author resolves the dispute by stating the fatwa follows Muhammad. The passage then extends the discussion to salam contracts and liability, and concludes with analogies (qiyas) to partnerships in livestock. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your skill in applying the principle that 'the fatwa is on the view that meets people's needs', and in analogical reasoning from a clear asl.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the three positions: Abu Hanifa (prohibition), Abu Yusuf (conditional on silk appearance), Muhammad (unconditional permission). Note that the fatwa follows Muhammad.
2
Analyze the reasoning behind each position
Abu Hanifa considers worms as vermin (hawamm) with no benefit; eggs have no intrinsic benefit. Muhammad considers them valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on whether silk is visible.
The core issue is whether silkworms qualify as mal mutaqawwim.
3
Identify the mufta bihi and its tarjih authority
The author explicitly states 'al-fatwa ala qawl Muhammad' (الفتوى على قول محمد). The fatwa adopts Muhammad's permissibility due to public need and the recognition of silkworms as commercial goods.
This is a clear example of how later Hanafi authorities select the more practically suitable opinion.
4
Apply the qiyas in the passage
The text gives three analogous cases: (1) silkworm seed, (2) cow for feeding, (3) hen for eggs. In each, the offspring belongs to the owner of the original asset, and the worker is compensated for inputs. Derive the illa: the produce follows the source of generation.
This is qiyas jali (clear analogy). The asl is the silkworm seed case; the far' is the cow and hen cases.
5
Derive the ruling for salam on silkworms
Salam (advance sale) is permissible only if it is within the silkworm season and the delivery term is set within that season. After the season, salam is invalid due to inability to deliver.
This follows the general Hanafi rule for salam: the subject must be available for delivery at the agreed time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويباع دود القز وبيضه: أي يجوز بيعهما عند محمد والثلاثة، وعند أبي حنيفة: لا يجوز بيعهما، أما الدود؛ فلأنه من الهوام، وأما بيضه؛ فلأنه غير منتفع به باعتبار ذاته بل باعتبار غيره، وذلك الغير معدوم، وقال أبو يوسف: يصح بيع الدود إذا ظهر القز فيه وإلا لا، واضطرب في بيضه والفتوى على قول محمد، فلذلك نص عليه الشيخ بالجواز، ويجوز السلم فيه كيلاً إذا كان في وقته وجعل أجله في وقته، أما بعد وقته، فلا يجوز السلم؛ لعدم قدرته من على التسليم. وقال محمد: يضمن من قتل دود القز؛ لكونه مالاً متقوماً، وإتلافه مضمون، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا يضمن قتله، ولو أن امرأة أعطت بذر القز وهو بذر الفيلق بالنصف إلى امرأة فقامت عليه حتى أدرك، فالفيلق لصاحبة البذر؛ لأنه حدث من بذرها، ولها على الأخرى قيمة الأوراق وأجرة مثلها، وعلى هذا إذا دفع البقرة إلى إنسان بالعلف ليكون الحادث بينهما بالنصف، فالحادث كله لصاحب البقرة وله على صاحبها ثمن العلف وأجرة المثل، وكذا إذا دفع الدجاجة؛ لتكون البيضة بالنصف.
And silkworms and their eggs are sold: i.e., their sale is permissible according to Muhammad and the three (Abu Yusuf, Shafi'i, Ahmad, Malik?). According to Abu Hanifa: their sale is not permissible. As for the worm, because it is among the vermin; as for its eggs, because they are not useful in themselves but through something else, and that something else is non-existent. Abu Yusuf said: Sale of the worm is valid if the silk has appeared in it, otherwise not. And he was conflicted regarding its eggs. The fatwa is upon Muhammad's opinion, therefore the author stated it as permissible. Salam (advance sale) in it is permissible by measure if it is in its season and its term is set within its season. As for after its season, salam is not permissible due to inability to deliver. Muhammad said: Whoever kills silkworms is liable for them because they are valuable property, and their destruction is compensable. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he does not hold the killer liable. And if a woman gave silkworm seed—which is the seed of filaq—to another woman on halves, and she tended it until it matured, then the filaq belongs to the owner of the seed because it came from her seed, and she is owed the value of the leaves and her wages. Similarly, if one gives a cow to someone for feeding on halves of the offspring, the entire offspring belongs to the owner of the cow, and he is owed the price of the fodder and wages. Likewise if one gives a hen to have the eggs on halves.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage deals with the sale of silkworms (دود القز) and their eggs. The ruling is presented with intra-Hanafi differences: Imam Muhammad and the 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and others) permit the sale. Abu Hanifa prohibits it, considering worms as vermin and eggs as without intrinsic benefit. Abu Yusuf has a conditional view: sale is valid only if silk has appeared. The fatwa is based on Muhammad's permissibility. The text further discusses salam (advance sale) of silkworms: it is permissible only if within the season and delivery is possible. Muhammad also holds that killing silkworms incurs liability (ضمان) because they are valuable property (مال متقوم), whereas a narration from Abu Hanifa says no liability. The passage then gives analogies: if a woman provides silkworm seed (بذر القز) to another to tend on halves, the product belongs to the seed owner, and the worker gets compensation for leaves and labor. This is analogous to sharing the offspring of a cow or hen when one provides the animal and the other feeds it. These analogies illustrate the principle that the offspring follow the owner of the original asset, not the labor provider.

Key Terms
دود القز (dud al-qazz): Silkworms; the larvae that produce silk.
السلم (al-salam): Advance sale; a contract where the price is paid upfront for goods to be delivered later.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property; something which is legally recognized as having value and compensable if destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in this passage, what is the ruling on selling silkworms?
A) Not permissible under any condition.
B) Permissible only if silk has appeared.
C) Permissible unconditionally.
D) Makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The sale of silkworms and their eggs is permissible according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa, and in partnerships where one provides the asset and the other provides labor, the offspring belongs to the asset owner.

The Prohibition of Selling an Absconding Slave and Its Exception
Prohibition and exception for selling an absconding slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling for selling an absconding slave and identify the one exception where the sale becomes valid.

Core Idea
Sale of an absconding slave is invalid due to inability to deliver, except if sold to the person who already has him.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave who has run away (al-abiq). The text first establishes the general prohibition based on a hadith and the principle that delivery must be possible. Then it introduces an important exception: if the buyer already has the slave in his possession, the sale is valid because the slave is not absconding from him. We will parse the key terms and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central term and the act
Look at the term 'al-abiq' (the absconder). What does it mean and what act is being ruled upon?
The act is selling an absconding slave.
2
State the general ruling and its proofs
The text says 'la yajuz' (not permissible). Find the two proofs: a prophetic prohibition and the condition of ability to deliver (qudra 'ala al-taslim).
The hadith is mentioned implicitly; the condition is explicit.
3
Distinguish from the case of a slave sent on an errand
The text contrasts with a slave sent on an errand (al-mursal). Why is that sale valid? Because he is capable of delivery in legal consideration (hukman) as he is expected to return.
This highlights that constructive possession suffices.
4
Understand the exception
The phrase 'illa an yabee'ahu' introduces the exception. The sale is valid if the seller sells to someone who claims the slave is with him. Why? Because the slave is not absconding from the buyer's perspective, so the prohibition does not apply.
The 'absolute text' (al-nass al-mutlaq) of the hadith is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآبق أي لا يجوز أيضاً بيع العبد الآبق؛ لأنه عليه نهى عن بيع العبد وهو آبق؛ ولأنه لا يقدر على التسليم، وهو شرط جواز البيع، بخلاف العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى، فإنه يجوز بيعه؛ لأنه مقدور التسليم وقت العقد حكماً؛ إذ الظاهر عوده، وقوله: "إلا أن يبيعه" أي لا يجوز الآبق إلا أن يبيع الآبق ممن يزعم البائع أن العبد عنده، فإن هذا البيع يجوز؛ لأن النهي ورد في العبد الآبق وهو أن يكون آبقاً عند المتعاقدين، وهذا ليس بآبق في حق من هو في يده، فلا يتناوله النص المطلق. المشتري قابضاً يصير بمجرد العقد إذا كان في يده بشرط إن كان أشهد عند الأخذ أنه يأخذه ليرده على المطلق، ثم لا إلا أن يبيعه ممن يزعم أنه عنده. ولو باعه ممن قال: "هو عند فلان" لم يجز؛ لأنه أبق عندهما، ولو باعه ثم عاد قبل الفسخ لم يعد صحيحاً؛ لوقوعه باطلاً؛ لكونه مقدور التسليم، بخلاف ما إذا باعه ثم أبق قبل التسليم ثم عاد وسلم، حيث يجوز بيعه؛ لأن البيع وقع صحيحاً، ثم فسد بعارض الإباق، وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يعود صحيحاً، ولو أعتقه نفذ عتقه؛ لعدم اشتراط القبض فيه، ولو علم حياته في وقت العتق أجزأه عن الكفارة.
And the absconder: i.e., the sale of an absconding slave is also not permissible because there is a prohibition on selling a slave while he is absconding, and because he cannot be delivered, which is a condition for the validity of sale, unlike the slave sent on an errand by his master, for his sale is permissible because he is capable of delivery at the time of the contract in legal consideration, as he is expected to return. And his statement: "unless he sells him" means that the absconder is not allowed to be sold unless the seller sells him to someone who claims that the slave is with him, then this sale is permissible because the prohibition applies to the absconding slave who is absconding in relation to both contracting parties, and this one is not absconding in relation to the one who has him, so the absolute text does not include him. The buyer becomes a possessor by the mere contract if the slave is in his hand, on condition that at the time of taking he witnessed that he took him to return him to the one who let him go, then no unless he sells him to someone who claims he is with him. If he sells him to someone who says, "He is with so-and-so," it is not permissible because he is absconding according to both. And if he sells him and then he returns before the contract is rescinded, it does not become valid because the sale occurred invalidly, unlike the case where he sells him and then he absconds before delivery and then returns and delivers—then his sale is valid because the sale occurred validly, then became invalidated by the incidental absconding. From Abu Hanifa, it returns to validity. And if he emancipates him, his emancipation is effective because possession is not a condition for it. And if he knows of his life at the time of emancipation, it suffices for expiation.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on selling an absconding slave (العبد الآبق). It begins by stating the general prohibition, citing a hadith prohibition and the condition of ability to deliver (التسليم). An exception is made when the slave is sold to someone who already has him in his possession (i.e., the slave is with the buyer). In that case, the sale is valid because the slave is not 'absconding' from the buyer's perspective. The text then discusses the condition of possession: if the buyer takes the slave with a witness that he is taking him to return him to the owner (i.e., as an amana), he does not become a possessor of the sale item. If he does not witness, he becomes a possessor by mere contract according to the two shaykhs, but Abu Yusuf differs. Further, if the sale is made to someone who says the slave is with a third party, it is invalid because the slave is still absconding from the seller's perspective. If the slave returns after the sale but before rescission, the sale does not become valid because it was originally void. However, if the slave was already in the seller's possession at the time of sale, then absconded before delivery, and later returned and was delivered, the sale is valid because it was validly contracted before the absconding. A narration from Abu Hanifa states that if the slave returns, the sale becomes valid again. Emancipation of an absconding slave is effective even without possession, and if the owner knows he is alive, it counts for expiation.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abiq): A slave who has run away from his owner; an absconder.
القدرة على التسليم (al-qudra 'ala al-taslim): Ability to deliver the sold item; a condition for the validity of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the sale of an absconding slave valid?
A) The slave returns before the contract is completed.
B) The buyer already has the slave in his possession.
C) The seller guarantees delivery within a week.
D) The slave is not actually absconding but merely delayed.

Key Takeaway
The sale of an absconding slave is invalid except when the buyer already has the slave, because then delivery is possible.

Conditions of the Exception and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Effect of Return
Detailed conditions and disputes on the exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions for the valid sale of an absconding slave to the holder, and evaluate the conflicting views on whether the slave's return after a void sale can revive the contract.

Core Idea
Even if the sale is void ab initio, later return of the slave does not make it valid according to the two shaykhs; only Abu Hanifa differs.

This lesson dives into the detailed conditions of the exception for selling an absconding slave. The text specifies that if the buyer takes the slave with a witness that he will return him, he does not become owner. Then it addresses the scenario where the slave returns after the sale: the relied-upon view says the sale remains invalid, but a narration from Abu Hanifa says it becomes valid. We will parse these nuanced rules.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the possession condition
The text says the buyer becomes a possessor (qabid) by mere contract if the slave is in his hand, but only if he did not witness that he took him to return. If he witnessed, he does not become possessor.
This is about the transfer of possession for ownership.
2
Recognize the limit of the exception
The exception only applies when sold to the one who claims the slave is with him. If sold to someone who says the slave is with a third party, it is invalid.
'He is absconding according to both' means seller and buyer both consider him absconding.
3
Differentiate two return scenarios
Case 1: Sale then slave returns before rescission – invalid because sale was void. Case 2: Sale then slave absconds before delivery then returns – valid because sale was originally valid and only became invalid due to the absconding incident.
Contrast the timing of the absconding.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa reportedly says that if the slave returns, the sale becomes valid. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding the invalidity persists.
The relied-upon view is that of the two shaykhs.
5
Distinguish emancipation from sale
Emancipation of an absconding slave is valid even without possession, and if the owner knows he is alive, it counts for expiation.
This highlights that possession is not a condition for 'itq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآبق أي لا يجوز أيضاً بيع العبد الآبق؛ لأنه عليه نهى عن بيع العبد وهو آبق؛ ولأنه لا يقدر على التسليم، وهو شرط جواز البيع، بخلاف العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى، فإنه يجوز بيعه؛ لأنه مقدور التسليم وقت العقد حكماً؛ إذ الظاهر عوده، وقوله: "إلا أن يبيعه" أي لا يجوز الآبق إلا أن يبيع الآبق ممن يزعم البائع أن العبد عنده، فإن هذا البيع يجوز؛ لأن النهي ورد في العبد الآبق وهو أن يكون آبقاً عند المتعاقدين، وهذا ليس بآبق في حق من هو في يده، فلا يتناوله النص المطلق. المشتري قابضاً يصير بمجرد العقد إذا كان في يده بشرط إن كان أشهد عند الأخذ أنه يأخذه ليرده على المطلق، ثم لا إلا أن يبيعه ممن يزعم أنه عنده. ولو باعه ممن قال: "هو عند فلان" لم يجز؛ لأنه أبق عندهما، ولو باعه ثم عاد قبل الفسخ لم يعد صحيحاً؛ لوقوعه باطلاً؛ لكونه مقدور التسليم، بخلاف ما إذا باعه ثم أبق قبل التسليم ثم عاد وسلم، حيث يجوز بيعه؛ لأن البيع وقع صحيحاً، ثم فسد بعارض الإباق، وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يعود صحيحاً، ولو أعتقه نفذ عتقه؛ لعدم اشتراط القبض فيه، ولو علم حياته في وقت العتق أجزأه عن الكفارة.
And the absconder: i.e., the sale of an absconding slave is also not permissible because there is a prohibition on selling a slave while he is absconding, and because he cannot be delivered, which is a condition for the validity of sale, unlike the slave sent on an errand by his master, for his sale is permissible because he is capable of delivery at the time of the contract in legal consideration, as he is expected to return. And his statement: "unless he sells him" means that the absconder is not allowed to be sold unless the seller sells him to someone who claims that the slave is with him, then this sale is permissible because the prohibition applies to the absconding slave who is absconding in relation to both contracting parties, and this one is not absconding in relation to the one who has him, so the absolute text does not include him. The buyer becomes a possessor by the mere contract if the slave is in his hand, on condition that at the time of taking he witnessed that he took him to return him to the one who let him go, then no unless he sells him to someone who claims he is with him. If he sells him to someone who says, "He is with so-and-so," it is not permissible because he is absconding according to both. And if he sells him and then he returns before the contract is rescinded, it does not become valid because the sale occurred invalidly, unlike the case where he sells him and then he absconds before delivery and then returns and delivers—then his sale is valid because the sale occurred validly, then became invalidated by the incidental absconding. From Abu Hanifa, it returns to validity. And if he emancipates him, his emancipation is effective because possession is not a condition for it. And if he knows of his life at the time of emancipation, it suffices for expiation.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on selling an absconding slave (العبد الآبق). It begins by stating the general prohibition, citing a hadith prohibition and the condition of ability to deliver (التسليم). An exception is made when the slave is sold to someone who already has him in his possession (i.e., the slave is with the buyer). In that case, the sale is valid because the slave is not 'absconding' from the buyer's perspective. The text then discusses the condition of possession: if the buyer takes the slave with a witness that he is taking him to return him to the owner (i.e., as an amana), he does not become a possessor of the sale item. If he does not witness, he becomes a possessor by mere contract according to the two shaykhs, but Abu Yusuf differs. Further, if the sale is made to someone who says the slave is with a third party, it is invalid because the slave is still absconding from the seller's perspective. If the slave returns after the sale but before rescission, the sale does not become valid because it was originally void. However, if the slave was already in the seller's possession at the time of sale, then absconded before delivery, and later returned and was delivered, the sale is valid because it was validly contracted before the absconding. A narration from Abu Hanifa states that if the slave returns, the sale becomes valid again. Emancipation of an absconding slave is effective even without possession, and if the owner knows he is alive, it counts for expiation.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abiq): A slave who has run away from his owner; an absconder.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession; a condition for transfer of ownership in sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what happens if a person buys an absconding slave and the slave returns before the contract is rescinded?
A) The sale becomes valid.
B) The sale remains invalid.
C) The seller must refund the price.
D) The buyer becomes owner by default.

Key Takeaway
The sale of an absconding slave is void ab initio; later return does not validate it, except when the slave was in the buyer's possession at the time of contract.

The Prohibition of Selling Pig Hair and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Its Use
Prohibition of sale and dispute on use for stitching

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling pig hair, distinguish between the positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on its use for stitching, and identify the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Pig hair is najis al-‘ayn: its sale is haram; its use for stitching is makruh according to the correct view.

This passage from the matn explores two distinct legal issues surrounding pig hair: its sale and its utilitarian use. While the prohibition of sale is unequivocal due to its essential impurity, the permissibility of using pig hair for stitching leather generated a well-known disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. Understanding this khilaf illustrates how the Hanafi school balances text, necessity, and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage first addresses the sale of pig hair. State the ruling: it is not permissible (haram). Why? Because it is najis al-‘ayn (impure in essence).
The ruling is based on the principle that anything najis al-‘ayn cannot be sold.
2
Identify the second act and note the ikhtilaf
The second issue is using pig hair for stitching leather (kharz). Muhammad permits it due to historical necessity; Abu Yusuf considers it makruh because alternatives exist.
The khilaf is within the Hanafi school, not between madhahib.
3
Compare the proofs
Muhammad relies on necessity (الضرورة) at a time when iron needles were unavailable. Abu Yusuf relies on the availability of other materials and the impurity of pig hair.
This is a classic case of applying necessity (darura) vs. caution (ihtiyat).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text explicitly states 'the correct view is the statement of Abu Yusuf'. Therefore, the operative ruling is that using pig hair for stitching is makruh.
Later Hanafi scholars uphold this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الخنزير: أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع شعر الخنزير؛ لأنه نجس العين فلا يجوز بيعه. وينتفع به للخرز: للأساكفة؛ لأن خرز النعال والأخفاف لا يتأتى إلا به فكان فيه ضرورة؛ إذ لم يكن الخرز بالحديد في زمنه وهذا عند محمد وعند أبي يوسف أنه يكره ذلك؛ لأن الخرز يتأتى بغيره، وإذا وقع في الماء القليل يفسده عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، والصحيح قول أبي يوسف.
And pig hair: i.e., the sale of pig hair is also not permissible because it is impure in essence, so its sale is not permissible. And it is used for stitching leather (for cobblers) because the stitching of sandals and boots cannot be accomplished except with it, so there was necessity, as stitching with iron did not exist in his time. This is according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, he considers it disliked because stitching can be accomplished with other things. And if it falls into a small amount of water, it makes it impure according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. The correct view is the statement of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage deals with two issues related to pig hair: its sale and its use. First, the sale of pig hair is prohibited because it is najis al-'ayn (impure in essence). Second, using pig hair for stitching leather (خرز النعال والأخفاف) is discussed. According to Muhammad, it is permissible due to necessity in a time when iron needles were not available. Abu Yusuf, however, considers it makruh because other materials exist. The passage also notes a difference regarding water impurity: if pig hair falls into a small amount of water (الماء القليل), Abu Yusuf rules it becomes impure, while Muhammad disagrees. The correct view is given as Abu Yusuf's (that it makes water impure).

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Impure in its very essence; a substance that is inherently impure and cannot be purified.
الخرز (al-kharz): Stitching or sewing, especially of leather items like sandals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible unconditionally.
B) Disliked (makruh).
C) Absolutely prohibited.
D) Permissible only for Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Sale of pig hair is haram; its use for stitching is makruh according to the correct Hanafi view.
