The Ruling on Selling Human Hair: Honoring vs Degradation
Ruling on sale of human hair with resolution of a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of human hair and explain the principle of honoring and degradation that resolves the apparent contradiction with the sale of pig hair.

Core Idea
Selling human hair is void because it degrades what is honored; selling pig hair is void because it honors what is degraded.

The sale of human hair is a specific example where the Hanafi school analyzes the intended effect of a transaction on the status of the item. While one might think all hair sales are similar, the Shari'ah's view of the source—human versus pig—determines the ruling. In this lesson, you will learn the ruling and the elegant principle that resolves an apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on human hair
Identify that the sale of human hair is void (batil) according to the author, like the sale of human dung.
2
Identify the exceptional case
Note the exception where soil or dung prevails, but that pertains to impurity, not hair.
The exception is about mixing with filth, not directly relevant to hair.
3
Understand the apparent contradiction
Earlier, selling pig hair was considered 'honoring' (i'zaz) the pig, while selling human hair is 'degradation' (ihanah). Why the difference?
Both are prohibited, but for opposite reasons.
4
Grasp the resolution principle
The sale is an exchange. If the item is contemptible in Shari'ah, selling it honors it; if honored, selling it degrades it. Thus, both are prohibited to avoid opposite evils.
This is a subtle usuli point: the effect on the item's status determines the ruling.
5
Extract the legal maxim
The underlying principle is that a transaction must not lead to honoring what Shari'ah degrades nor degrading what Shari'ah honors.
This can be applied to similar cases (e.g., sale of human body parts, sale of impurity for beneficial use?).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الإنسان: أي ولم يجز بيع شعر الإنسان، فإنه باطل، كرجيعه في الأصح إلا إذا غلب عليه التراب أو السرقين، فإن قيل: جعل المصنف بيع شعر الخنزير إعزازاً فيما تقدم وجعل بيع شعر الإنسان إهانة، والبيع حقيقة واحدة، فكيف يجوز أن يكون موجبا للأمرين المتنافيين؟ أجيب بأن البيع مبادلة فلابد فيه من المبيع، فإن كان مما حقره الشرع فبيعه ومبادلته بما لم يحقره إعزاز له فلا يجوز؛ لإفضائه إلى إعزاز ما حقره الشرع، وإن كان مما عظمه الشرع فبيعه إهانة فلا يجوز؛ لإفضائه إلى إهانة ما عظمه الشرع، وبهذا التفصيل ارتفع التنافي.
And human hair: i.e., the sale of human hair is not permissible, and it is void, like his dung according to the most correct view, unless soil or dung prevails upon it. If it is said: The author considered the sale of pig hair as honoring in what preceded, and considered the sale of human hair as degradation, while sale is one reality. How can it cause two opposite things? The answer: Sale is an exchange, so it requires a sold item. If it is something that the Shari'ah has disparaged, then its sale and exchange with something not disparaged is honoring it, so it is not permissible because it leads to honoring what the Shari'ah has disparaged. And if it is something that the Shari'ah has honored, then its sale is degradation, so it is not permissible because it leads to degrading what the Shari'ah has honored. With this detail, the contradiction is resolved.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage rules that the sale of human hair is invalid (باطل), similar to the sale of human dung (رجيع) according to the most correct view (الأصح). The exception is when soil or animal dung has mixed with it, but that is about impurity, not hair. The passage then addresses an apparent contradiction: earlier, selling pig hair was considered 'honoring' (إعزاز) because pig hair is considered vile by the Shari'ah, and selling it elevates it. Selling human hair, however, is 'degradation' (إهانة) because humans are honored by the Shari'ah. The explanation is that sale is an exchange: if the item is generally disrespected by the Shari'ah (like pig hair), then putting it into commercial circulation honors it, which is prohibited. If the item is generally honored by the Shari'ah (like human body parts), then selling it degrades it, which is also prohibited. Thus, the prohibition in both cases prevents either honoring the low or degrading the high. This is a subtle usuli point about the purpose of prohibitions in sales.

Key Terms
إعزاز (i'zaz): Honoring; treating something with respect by giving it a higher status.
إهانة (ihanah): Degradation; treating something with disrespect by lowering its status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of human hair void?
A) Because human hair is impure (najis).
B) Because selling it degrades what the Shari'ah honors.
C) Because human hair has no benefit.
D) Because there is a consensus (ijma) on its prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The sale of human hair is void because it degrades the honored status of humans, and this is consistent with the principle that a transaction must not lead to honoring what is contemptible nor degrading what is honored.

Selling an Absconding Slave: The Exception and Return Before Annulment
Expands the ruling on selling an absconding slave with exceptions and disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an absconding slave, identify the exception when the buyer claims possession, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the slave's return before annulment.

Core Idea
A sale requires deliverability; an absconding slave is unsellable unless the buyer claims possession — and a return before annulment does not revive an invalid contract.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses the sale of an absconding slave, a classic fiqh example of an item that cannot be delivered at the time of contract. The Hanafi school generally rules such a sale invalid (fasid), but makes an exception if the buyer claims to have possession. The text then explores two important disputes: whether witnessing is required for taking possession of a trust, and whether the sale becomes valid if the slave returns before the contract is annulled — a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its exception
Start by noting that the default ruling is that selling an absconding slave is invalid because the seller cannot deliver. Then note the exception: if the buyer claims "he is with me," the sale is permissible. Understand that this exception is based on the assumption that the buyer can actually deliver.
The exception is only valid if the buyer's claim is credible.
2
Analyze the possession without witnesses dispute
The passage mentions a sub-discussion: if the buyer takes the slave (as a trust) without witnesses, does he become a possessor merely by contract? According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, yes — because a trust does not require witnessing. According to Abu Yusuf, no, if the buyer took it only to return to the owner.
This dispute illustrates a difference in defining 'possession' (qabd) for trusts vs. sales.
3
Trace the effect of return before annulment
The core issue: If the slave returns to the buyer before the contract is annulled, does the sale become valid? The passage gives the relied-upon view (Sahibayn) that it does not, because the contract was invalid from the start. Abu Hanifa holds that it becomes valid again.
This is a qiyas jali: compare to the case where the slave absconds after delivery and later returns — that sale is valid because it was valid initially.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The key principle is that validity of a sale requires deliverability (qudra 'ala al-taslim) at the time of contract. If the item is not deliverable, the contract is invalid. Exceptions exist only when the buyer already has possession.
This principle also governs other cases like selling a lost animal.
5
Note the ruling on manumission
The passage ends with a separate point: if the buyer manumits the absconding slave, the manumission is valid even without delivery, and may count for expiation if the buyer knows the slave is alive.
Manumission is a unilateral act that does not require delivery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن يبيعه ممن يزعم أنه عنده ولبن امرأة وشعر الخنزير وينتفع به للخرز أي البائع الآبق العبد الآبق وشعر الإنسان أي ولم يجز بيع شعر إلخ غير الخن بشعر صاحبه لأنه أمانة عنده وقبض الأمانة لا ينوب عن قبض المبيع لأن قبض الأمانة غير مضمون على المشتري ولو لم يشهد عند الأخذ يصير قابضا بمجرد العقد خلافا لأبي يوسف فيما إذا لم يأخذه لنفسه بل ليرده على صاحبه بناء على أن الإشهاد ليس بشرط عنده لكونه أمانة وعندهما شرط ولو باعه ممن قال هو عند فلان لم يجز لأنه أبق عندهما ولو باعه ثم عاد قبل الفسخ لم يعد صحيحا لوقوعه باطلا لكونه مقدور التسليم بخلاف ما إذا باعه ثم أبق قبل التسليم ثم عاد وسلم حيث يجوز بيعه لأن البيع وقع صحيحا ثم فسد بعارض الإباق وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يعود صحيحا ولو أعتقه نفذ عتقه لعدم اشتراط القبض فيه ولو علم حياته في وقت العتق أجزأه عن الكفارة.
Except that he sells it to someone who claims it is with him, and [also] the milk of a woman, and pig hair, and he benefits from it for sewing, meaning the seller of the absconding slave, and human hair, meaning it is not permissible to sell hair, etc. Other than that, [the buyer] is not [liable] for his companion's hair because it is a trust with him, and the taking of a trust does not substitute for the taking of the sold item because the taking of a trust is not guaranteed by the buyer. And if he did not witness upon taking, he becomes a taker merely by the contract, contrary to Abu Yusuf in the case when he did not take it for himself but rather to return it to its owner, based on the fact that witnessing is not a condition according to him because it is a trust, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is a condition. And if he sold it to someone who said 'It is with so-and-so', it is not permissible because it is absconding according to the two. And if he sold it and then it returned before annulment, it does not become valid because it occurred invalid, as it is not [currently] deliverable, unlike the case where he sold it and then it absconded before delivery and then returned and he delivered, then the sale is valid because the sale was valid, then it became corrupt due to the occurrence of absconding. And from Abu Hanifa that it returns to validity. And if he manumits it, his manumission is effective because delivery is not required in it. And if he knew of its life at the time of manumission, it suffices for the expiation.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on selling an absconding slave. The base text (matn) states an exception: if the buyer claims the slave is with him, the sale is permissible. The commentator explains that the buyer is not liable for the seller's property because it is a trust; taking a trust does not count as taking the sold item because a trust is not guaranteed. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the buyer does not take possession with witnesses, he becomes a possessor by the contract alone, but Abu Yusuf requires witnessing when the buyer takes it for the purpose of returning to the owner. If the slave is sold to someone who says 'he is with so-and-so', the sale is invalid because the slave is considered absconding in relation to the buyer. If the slave returns before the contract is annulled, the sale does not become valid because it was invalid from the start. However, if the slave was delivered and then absconded before delivery, but later returned, the sale is valid because the initial contract was sound. Abu Hanifa holds that if the slave returns before annulment, the sale becomes valid again. Manumission of the slave by the buyer is valid regardless of delivery, and if he knows the slave is alive, it suffices for expiation.

Key Terms
آبق (ābiq): A slave who has run away from his master and is not present for delivery.
إشهاد (ishhād): The act of having witnesses present when taking possession of a trust or sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, if a person sells an absconding slave and the slave returns before the contract is annulled, what is the ruling?
A) The sale becomes valid again.
B) The sale remains invalid.
C) The sale becomes valid only if the slave returns within three days.
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer takes possession with witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A void sale (batil) cannot be revived by subsequent events; delivery must be possible at the time of contract.

Selling Human Breast Milk: The Hanafi Prohibition
Ruling on the prohibition of selling human breast milk with proofs and inter-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human breast milk, explain the qiyas proof, and distinguish the Hanafi position from that of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Human breast milk is not property—its sale is prohibited because it degrades an honored being.

This passage addresses the permissibility of selling human breast milk, a topic where the Hanafi school takes a strong prohibitive stance based on a unique qiyas. Unlike animal milk, which is property and can be sold, human milk is classified as a benefit (manfa'ah) akin to services. The text presents the Hanafi proof, along with objections from Imam al-Shafi'i and a disagreement within the school from Abu Yusuf. We will systematically unpack the arguments and understand the basis for the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its Hanafi ruling
What is the act in question? Selling human breast milk. What is the Hanafi ruling? It is not permissible (haram), whether the milk is from a free or enslaved woman, even if in a container.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) with the validity of hiring a wet nurse. If human milk were property (mal), hiring a wet nurse would be invalid (like hiring a cow for its milk). Since hiring a wet nurse is valid by consensus, human milk is not property, so its sale is invalid.
This is a qiyas al-aks (inverse analogy).
3
Analyse the opposing views
Al-Shafi'i permits sale absolutely because milk is pure. Abu Yusuf permits sale of milk from an enslaved woman because she herself can be sold.
4
Examine the Hanafi responses
To Shafi'i: human milk is part of an honored human, and selling it degrades. To Abu Yusuf: slavery attaches to the living being, but milk has no life, so the analogy fails.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is prohibition for both free and enslaved women, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبن امرأة أي لم يجز أيضا بيع لبن إلخ إذا كان في وعاء مطلقا من حرة أو أمة واستدلوا على عدم جوازه ولو في قدح بصحة إجارة الظئر لأنه لما صحت الإجارة ثبت أن سبيله سبيل المنافع وليس سبيله سبيل الأموال إذ لو كان مالا لم تجز الإجارة ألا ترى أن إجارة البقرة لشرب لبنها لا يجوز لكون لبن البقرة مالا حتى صح بيعه فلما جازت إجارة الظئر ثبت أن لبنها ليس بمال ولهذا لا يجوز بيعه سواء كان لبن حرة أو أمة وقال الشافعي يجوز مطلقا لأنه مشروب طاهر قلنا إنه جزء آدمي مكرم وفي البيع إهانة فلا يجوز وقال أبو يوسف إن كان لبن أمة يجوز لأنه يجوز إيراد العقد على نفسها فكذا على جزئها قلنا نفسها محل للرق لاختصاصه بمحل القوة وهو الحي ولا حياة في اللبن وصبه في عين الرمدة لا يجوز عند بعض أصحابنا لقوله عليه السلام لم يجعل الله شفاء أمتي فيما حرم عليها وقيل يجوز إذا علم أنه يزول به الرمد ولم يجد دواء غيره ومعنى الحديث بالنسبة التي لا شفاء فيها أما التي فيها الشفاء فلا بأس به كشرب الخمر للعطشان.
And the milk of a woman, meaning it is also not permissible to sell milk, etc., if it is in a container, absolutely, whether from a free woman or an enslaved woman. And they argued for its impermissibility, even if in a cup, by the validity of hiring a wet nurse. Because when the hire is valid, it proves that its path is the path of benefits, not the path of wealth, because if it were wealth, the hire would not be valid. Do you not see that hiring a cow for drinking its milk is not permissible because cow's milk is wealth, such that its sale is valid. So when the hire of a wet nurse is valid, it proves that her milk is not wealth, and therefore its sale is not permissible, whether it is milk of a free woman or an enslaved woman. And al-Shafi'i said: it is permissible absolutely because it is a pure drink. We said: it is part of an honored human being, and in its sale is degradation, so it is not permissible. And Abu Yusuf said: if it is the milk of an enslaved woman, it is permissible because it is permissible to enter into a contract upon herself, so likewise upon her part. We said: she herself is subject to slavery due to its connection to the place of strength, which is the living being, and there is no life in the milk. And its use as eye medicine [by dropping] into the eye is not permissible according to some of our companions due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah has not placed the cure of my ummah in what He has forbidden upon them.' And it is said: it is permissible if it is known that it will remove the eye inflammation and he finds no other medicine. And the meaning of the hadith is regarding the type that has no cure; as for that which has a cure, there is no harm, like drinking alcohol for the thirsty.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling human breast milk. The Hanafi position is that it is not permissible to sell human milk, whether from a free or enslaved woman. The proof is based on a comparison (qiyas) with the validity of hiring a wet nurse: if milk were wealth, hiring a wet nurse would be like hiring a cow for milk, which is not valid because cow's milk is wealth and can be sold. Since hiring a wet nurse is valid, milk is not wealth, thus its sale is invalid. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that milk is pure and thus saleable. The Hanafi response is that human milk is part of an honored human being, and selling it involves degradation, so it is not permissible. Abu Yusuf makes an exception for the milk of an enslaved woman, analogizing that since she herself can be sold, so can her milk. The Hanafi rebuttal is that slavery attaches to the living being, but milk has no life. The passage then discusses using human milk as eye medicine: some Hanafis prohibit it based on a hadith, while others allow it if it is effective and no alternative exists, likening it to drinking alcohol for thirst.

Key Terms
لبن امرأة (laban imra'ah): Human breast milk, considered not to be property (mal) by Hanafis.
إجارة الظئر (ijārat al-ẓa'r): Hiring a wet nurse to breastfeed a child, which is valid by consensus and serves as a proof for the non-property nature of human milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi proof (dalil) for prohibiting the sale of human breast milk?
A) It is impure (najis).
B) Hiring a wet nurse is valid, showing milk is not property.
C) It causes harm to the child.
D) No explicit text prohibits it.

Key Takeaway
Human breast milk is not considered property in the Hanafi school; its sale is prohibited to preserve the honor of the human being, unlike animal milk which is property and saleable.

Selling Pig Hair and Its Use: Prohibition and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on prohibition of sale and dispute on use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling pig hair and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its use for sewing and its effect on water.

Core Idea
Pig hair is najis al-ayn: its sale is haram, but its use for sewing is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage addresses the legal status of pig hair in the Hanafi school. While its sale is unequivocally prohibited because it is inherently impure, there is a disagreement among the founding imams about whether it may be used for sewing leather goods. Understanding this dispute helps clarify how necessity (darurah) can create exceptions to general prohibitions, and how later scholars weigh differing proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling pig hair (shuʿr al-khinzīr). The ruling is that it is not permissible (lā yajūz) because pig hair is najis al-ayn (inherently impure).
Najis al-ayn items cannot be sold as they are not considered property (mal).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the principle that najis al-ayn items are not lawful to sell. The underlying reason is that impurity invalidates the basis of ownership and exchange.
The dalil is not a specific text but a general rule derived from the Quran and Sunnah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The obligation to avoid impurity and the prohibition of trading in ritually impure goods (e.g., dead animals, blood) establishes that najis al-ayn items like pig hair cannot be subject to a valid contract of sale.
This is a qiyas from clear impure substances.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad permits using pig hair for sewing (kharz) sandals and socks due to necessity at the Prophet's time (no iron needles). Imam Abu Yusuf considers it disliked (makruh) because alternatives exist. Also, regarding water impurity: Abu Yusuf says pig hair spoils a small quantity of water; Muhammad says it does not.
The ikhtilaf concerns the permissibility of use, not the sale.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator declares the correct view (al-sahih) is that of Abu Yusuf. Thus the mufta bihi position is that using pig hair is disliked and it spoils a small amount of water.
This tarjih is based on the availability of alternatives and the general prohibition of impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الخنزير أي ولم يجز أيضا بيع شعر إلخ لأنه نجس العين فلا يجوز بيعه وينتفع به للخرز للأساكفة لأن خرز النعال والأخفاف لا يتأتى إلا به فكان فيه ضرورة إذ لم يكن الخرز بالحديد في زمنه وهذا عند محمد وعند أبي يوسف أنه يكره ذلك لأن الخرز يتأتى بغيره وإذا وقع في الماء القليل يفسده عند أبي يوسف خلافا لمحمد والصحيح قول أبي يوسف.
And pig hair, meaning it is also not permissible to sell hair, etc., because it is impure by itself (najis al-ayn), so its sale is not permissible. And it is used for sewing for cobblers because sewing sandals and socks is only possible with it, so there is necessity, as sewing with iron was not available in his time. This is according to Muhammad. And according to Abu Yusuf, he dislikes that because sewing can be done with other means. And if it falls into a small amount of water, it spoils it according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And the correct view is that of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage discusses pig hair. The ruling is that selling pig hair is not permissible because it is najis al-ayn (inherently impure). However, using it for sewing (specifically for cobblers to stitch sandals and socks) is permitted due to necessity, as in the time of the Prophet iron needles were not available. This is the view of Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that it is disliked to use pig hair because sewing can be done with other materials. Furthermore, if pig hair falls into a small quantity of water, according to Abu Yusuf it renders the water impure, whereas Muhammad holds it does not. The commentator states that the correct position (al-sahih) is that of Abu Yusuf, meaning the use is disliked and the water becomes impure.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Something that is inherently impure, such as pig hair, which cannot be purified and cannot be sold.
خرز (kharz): The act of sewing leather, specifically for sandals and socks, using pig hair needles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school regarding pig hair, what is the ruling on it falling into a small quantity of water?
A) It does not spoil the water because pig hair is not impure.
B) It spoils the water, rendering it impure.
C) It spoils the water only if the water is used for drinking.
D) It spoils the water only if the hair is used for sewing.

Key Takeaway
The sale of pig hair is haram; its use is disliked in the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf, and it spoils a small quantity of water.

Selling Human Hair: The Prohibition Based on Honor and Degradation
Ruling on prohibition of selling human hair with philosophical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human hair and explain the reasoning based on the Shari'ah's valuation of honor and degradation.

Core Idea
Selling human hair is invalid because it degrades what the Shari'ah has honored.

The passage addresses a seemingly paradoxical ruling: why is selling pig hair prohibited as an 'honor' to something degraded, while selling human hair is prohibited as a 'degradation' of something honored? The answer lies in the Shari'ah's valuation of objects. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on selling human hair, its underlying logic, and the difference of opinion regarding its use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling and its classification
The passage states that selling human hair is invalid (batil) and not permissible. Determine the hukm level and note that the contract is void.
The phrase 'fi al-ashah' indicates the most correct view.
2
Examine the objection and the answer
The author raises an objection: how can selling pig hair be 'honoring' and selling human hair be 'degrading' when sale is one act? The answer distinguishes based on the Shari'ah's valuation of the object.
The ruling is not about the act of sale itself but about the object's legal description.
3
Trace the distinction between honoring a degraded object and degrading an honored object
If the Shari'ah has degraded something (e.g., pig), selling it would honor it, which is prohibited. If the Shari'ah has honored something (e.g., human), selling it degrades it, also prohibited.
This reasoning conserves the Shari'ah's valuation.
4
Explore the intra-madhhab dispute on benefiting from human hair
Most Hanafis prohibit using human hair because it is part of an honored human. Imam Muhammad permits use based on the hadith of the Prophet distributing his hair.
The majority respond that the Prophet's hair is unique; his action does not generalize.
5
Understand the epistemological weight of the relied-upon position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that selling and using human hair is invalid, as per the majority. The hadith of distribution is considered specific to the Prophet.
The example of Abu Taybah shows that even pure bodily substances are not to be used for blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الإنسان أي ولم يجز بيع شعر الإنسان فإنه باطل كرجيعه في الأصح إلا إذا غلب عليه التراب أو السرقين فإن قيل جعل المصنف بيع شعر الخنزير إعزازا فيما تقدم وجعل بيع شعر الإنسان إهانة والبيع حقيقة واحدة فكيف يجوز أن يكون موجبا للأمرين المتنافيين أجيب بأن البيع مبادلة فلابد فيه من المبيع فإن كان مما حقره الشرع فبيعه ومبادلته بما لم يحقره إعزاز له فلا يجوز لإفضائه إلى إعزاز ما حقره الشرع وإن كان مما كرمه وعظمه فبيعه ومبادلته بما ليس كذلك إهانة فلا يجوز لإفضائه إلى تحقير ما عظمه الشرع فليس ذلك من البيع في شيء وإنما هو من وصف المحل شرعا والانتفاع به أي ولا يجوز الانتفاع بشعر الإنسان لأنه جزء آدمي مكرم وعن محمد أنه جوز الانتفاع به لما ورد من أنه عليه السلام قسم شعره بين أصحابه فكانوا يتبركون به ولو كان نجسا لما فعل لأنه لا يتبرك بالنجس ألا ترى أن أبا طيبة حين شرب بوله لقصد التبرك نهاه أن يعود إلى مثل هذا في المستقبل.
And human hair, meaning it is not permissible to sell human hair, for it is invalid like his stool in the most correct view, unless soil or dung has overcome it. For if it is said that the author made selling pig hair an honor in the previous [section] and made selling human hair a degradation, while sale is one and the same, how can it be the cause of two contradictory things? The answer is that sale is an exchange, so the sold item is necessary. If it is something that the Shari'ah has degraded, then selling it and exchanging it for something not degraded is an honor for it, so it is not permissible because it leads to honoring what the Shari'ah degraded. And if it is something that the Shari'ah has honored and magnified, then selling it and exchanging it for something not so honored is a degradation, so it is not permissible because it leads to degrading what the Shari'ah magnified. So that is not essentially sale but rather a description of the object according to the Shari'ah. And benefiting from it, i.e., it is not permissible to benefit from human hair because it is a part of an honored human being. And from Muhammad that he permitted benefiting from it because of what is reported that he (peace be upon him) divided his hair among his companions, and they would seek blessings from it. If it were impure, he would not have done so, because one does not seek blessings from impurities. Do you not see that when Abu Taybah drank his urine with the intention of seeking blessings, he prohibited him from returning to such in the future?
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling and benefiting from human hair. The Hanafi position is that selling human hair is invalid (batil), likened to selling human stool in the most correct view, unless it is mixed with soil or dung. The commentator addresses a potential objection: how can selling pig hair be considered 'honoring' something degraded, and selling human hair be 'degrading' something honored, when sale is one act? The answer is that the ruling depends on the legal description of the object. If the Shari'ah has degraded something (like pig hair), selling it would be an honor, so it is prohibited. If the Shari'ah has honored something (like human hair), selling it would degrade it, so it is also prohibited. Thus, the prohibition is based on conserving the Shari'ah's valuation. As for benefiting from human hair (e.g., for medical use), the majority of Hanafis prohibit it because it is part of an honored human. However, Imam Muhammad permits it, citing the hadith that the Prophet distributed his hair among the companions for blessings. If hair were impure, he would not have done so. The commentator adds the example of Abu Taybah, who was prohibited from drinking the Prophet's urine for blessing, indicating that such practices are not allowed.

Key Terms
شعر الإنسان (sha'r al-insān): Human hair, considered part of an honored human being; its sale is prohibited to prevent degradation.
إعزاز (iʿzāz): Giving honor; used here to explain the ruling on selling degraded items like pig hair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling human hair according to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school?
A) It is permissible because hair is not impure.
B) It is invalid (batil) because it degrades an honored human being.
C) It is permissible if the hair is from a dead body.
D) It is disliked but not invalid.

Key Takeaway
Selling human hair is invalid because it degrades what the Shari'ah has honored, regardless of purity.

Selling Hide of a Dead Animal Before Tanning
Ruling on prohibition of selling hide of a dead animal before tanning with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the hide of a dead animal before tanning, explain the prohibition based on the hadith and the concept of original impurity, and distinguish it from accidentally impure items.

Core Idea
A dead animal's hide is originally impure like its meat; sale before tanning is haram, unlike accidentally impure items which are sellable.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the sale of the hide of a dead animal (carrion) before tanning. The passage explains that the hide, like the meat, carries original impurity (najis asli) and therefore cannot be sold. This ruling is based on a prophetic prohibition and a key distinction between original and accidental impurity. Understanding this distinction helps clarify similar rulings in the Hanafi school regarding the sale of impure items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? What is the hukm (haram, permissible, etc.)?
The act is selling the hide of a dead animal before tanning; the ruling is haram (not permissible).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, or Qiyas?
The author cites a hadith that the Prophet forbade benefiting from the hide, bone, and sinew of carrion.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does this dalil prove the ruling? What is the legal link?
The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates prohibition of sale and use. The impurity is original, making it like the meat.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? What is their proof?
The passage contrasts with accidentally impure items (like a garment or oil) where sale is permissible. This is not a madhhab ikhtilaf but a distinction within the Hanafi school.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon and why?
The Hanafi position is clear based on the hadith and the principle of original impurity; no tarjih needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجلد الميتة أي ولم يجز أيضا بيع جلد الميتة قبل إصلاحها بالشمس أو بطريق آخر للنهي الوارد أنه عليه السلام نهى عن الانتفاع بجلدها وعظمها وعصبها ولأن نجاسته أصلية فصار كلحم الميتة بخلاف الثوب النجس والدهن حيث يجوز بيعه لأن نجاسته عارضة فلا يتغير حكم الثوب بها وجاز بيع المتنجس والانتفاع به في غير الأكل بخلاف الودك.
And the hide of a dead animal, meaning it is also not permissible to sell the hide of a dead animal before its treatment by the sun or another method, due to the prohibition that has come that he (peace be upon him) forbade benefiting from its hide, its bone, and its sinew, and because its impurity is original, so it is like the meat of the dead animal, contrary to a impure garment or oil where its sale is permissible because their impurity is accidental, so the ruling of the garment does not change by it, and the sale of an item that has become impure (mutanajjis) is permitted and benefiting from it in other than eating, unlike [raw] animal fat.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling the hide of a dead animal (carrion) before it has been tanned or treated. The Hanafi position is that it is not permissible to sell it because the hide is impure with an original impurity (najis asli), similar to the meat of the dead animal. This prohibition is based on the hadith that the Prophet forbade benefiting from the hide, bone, and sinew of carrion. The commentator distinguishes this from a garment that becomes impure (mutanajjis) or oil that becomes impure; in those cases, the impurity is accidental (najis 'aridi), so the item itself is still considered property and can be sold. The sale of such mutanajjis items is permissible, and they can be used for non-eating purposes. However, the fat (wadak) of a dead animal is also prohibited. So before tanning, the hide is treated like the meat, and its sale is invalid.

Key Terms
نجاسة أصلية (najāsah aṣliyyah): The impurity inherent to the item itself, like the hide of a dead animal, which does not become pure by washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is harmful to health.
C) Because it has no beneficial use.
D) Because the Prophet explicitly allowed its sale only after tanning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits selling the hide of a dead animal before tanning because it is originally impure, like the meat, and such a sale is invalid.

Selling and Using Tanned Hide: Hanafi vs. Maliki Views
Ruling on sale and use of tanned hide with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale and use of tanned hide of dead animals, identify the exceptions (human, pig, snake), and explain the practical difference with the Maliki position.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies the hide, making its sale and unrestricted use permissible in the Hanafi school.

Dead animals' hides are normally impure, but tanning transforms them into pure, valuable property. The Hanafi school holds that after tanning, the hide can be sold and used for any purpose, with a few exceptions. This view is contrasted with the Maliki and Hanbali positions, which restrict sale or use. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: after tanning, the hide of a dead animal becomes pure and can be sold (except human, pig, snake). The source says 'it is permissible to sell' because it becomes valuable property.
Tanning is the key transformative act.
2
Identify the exceptions
Note that human, pig, and snake skins are excluded even after tanning. Their impurity cannot be removed by tanning.
This is a specific limitation within the Hanafi school.
3
State the opposing view (Malik and Ahmad)
The text says 'contrary to Malik and Ahmad'—they do not permit sale even after tanning. Additionally, Malik permits use only for solids (e.g., dry goods) but not liquids.
Malik’s restriction on liquids is a key difference.
4
Analyze the difference in use
Compare: Hanafis allow use for both solids and liquids without restriction. Malikis allow only solids (e.g., as a container for wheat) but not liquids (e.g., water).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
5
Conclude with the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'The tanning of the skin of a dead animal is its purification.' This establishes that tanning removes impurity, making the hide pure and thus permissible to sell and use.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعده يباع أي يجوز بيع جلد الميتة بعد الدبغ لأنه طهر بالدبغ فصار مالا متقوما وهو محل البيع إلا جلد إنسان وخنزير وحية خلافا لمالك وأحمد فإن بيعها عندهما لا يجوز بعد الدبغ أيضا وينتفع به أي يجوز الانتفاع بجلد الميتة بعد الدبغ عندنا خلافا لمالك وعنده يجوز استعماله في الجامد أي لو وضع فيه الشيء الجامد مثل البر جاز دون المائع أي لو استعمل في المائع كالماء مثلا لا يجوز وعندنا المائع والجامد سواء في الاستعمال ويجوز مطلقا.
And after it, it is sold, meaning it is permissible to sell the hide of a dead animal after tanning because it is purified by tanning, so it becomes valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and it is the subject of sale, except for the skin of a human, pig, and snake, contrary to Malik and Ahmad, for their sale is not permissible according to them even after tanning. And benefiting from it, meaning it is permissible to benefit from the hide of a dead animal after tanning according to us, contrary to Malik. According to him, its use is permissible for solids, i.e., if one places a solid thing like wheat in it, it is permissible, but not for liquids, i.e., if used for liquids like water, it is not permissible. Whereas according to us, liquid and solid are the same in use and it is permissible absolutely.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling after tanning. According to the Hanafi school, tanning purifies the hide of a dead animal, making it pure (tahir) and valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), so its sale is permissible. Exceptions are the skins of humans, pigs, and snakes, because these cannot be purified by tanning. This is contrary to Malik and Ahmad, who hold that the sale of such hides is not permissible even after tanning. Regarding use, Hanafis permit using tanned hide for both solids and liquids without restriction, while Malik restricts use to solids only, not for liquids. The Hanafi view is that tanning removes all impurity, so the hide becomes fully pure.

Key Terms
دبغ (dabgh): Tanning, a process that purifies the hide of a dead animal, making it pure and permissible to sell and use in the Hanafi school.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property that can be legally sold and used; after tanning, the hide becomes such.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible
B) Permissible only for solids, not liquids
C) Not permissible at all
D) Permissible only if the cow was slaughtered Islamically

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies the hide of most dead animals, making its sale and unrestricted use permissible in the Hanafi school, with the exception of human, pig, and snake skins.

Ruling on Selling Bones and Parts of Dead Animals: Hanafi vs. Other Schools
Ruling on permissibility with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling bones of dead animals, explain the proof, and distinguish the Hanafi position from Shafi'i, Maliki, and intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Bones of dead animals are pure and permissible to sell; death does not make bones impure.

The Hanafi school permits the sale and use of bones from dead animals, as well as tanned dog hide and elephant ivory, while Shafi'is and others consider them impure. This lesson explores the key disputes and the evidentiary basis for the Hanafi view, including a notable intra-Hanafi difference on ivory.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on selling bones of dead animals: permissible (halal), and note that the Hanafi school treats bones as pure, unlike flesh.
This is the central ruling.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their grounds
State that Shafi'i and Ahmad consider all parts of dead animals impure, while Malik considers bones impure but hair pure.
Understanding the disagreement is key.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the hadith that the Prophet purchased ivory bracelets for Fatima, and the lack of disapproval among companions, which indicates permissibility.
This is a dalil naqli for the Hanafi view.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on elephant bone (ivory)
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit sale of ivory, while Muhammad forbids it. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) follows the two shaykhs.
This shows how later scholars weighed evidence.
5
Apply the same principle to other parts
Extend the ruling to tanned dog hide (permissible), teeth of animals (depends on tadhkiyah and fat content), and pig's tooth (impure according to Muhammad, pure according to the two).
The principle: impurity is established only for flesh, not bones/hair/tusks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كعظم الميتة أي كما يجوز بيع عظم الميتة والانتفاع به لأن الحياة والممات لا يحلها وعند الشافعي لا يجوز بيعها ولا الانتفاع بها لأنها نجسة لأنها من أجزاء الميتة وبه قال أحمد في رواية وعند مالك عظم الميتة نجس دون شعرها وكذلك يجوز عندنا بيع جلد الكلب إذا كان مدبوغا خلافا للشافعي والحسن ويجوز أيضا بيع عظم الفيل عند الشيخين خلافا لمحمد ولنا ما روي أنه عليه السلام اشترى لفاطمة رضي الله عنها سوارين من عاج فظهر استعمال الناس له من غير نكير حتى حكى بعضهم الإجماع على جواز بيعه وهو قول الكرخي في مختصره وقال البخاري في جامعه قال ابن سيرين وابن إبراهيم لا بأس بتجارة العاج وفي العيون وروى إبراهيم بن رستم عن محمد في امرأة صلت وفي عنقها قلادة فيها سن كلب أو أسد أو ثعلب فصلاتها تامة لأنه يقع عليها الذكاة أي الذكاة طهارة له بناء على أن السن طرف عصب وأما على القول بأنه عظم فلا لأنه من الميتة إذا خلا عن الدسومة طهر بخلاف الخنزير إذا ذكي فإن سنه لا يطهر خلافا لمحمد فإنه نجس العين عنده كالخنزير وعندهما بمنزلة السباع لأنه ينتفع به حقيقة فكان ويجوز بيع الفيل منتفعا به شرعا كسائر السباع وبيع القرد جائز وكذا جميع الحيوانات سوى الخنزير.
Like the bone of a dead animal, meaning just as it is permissible to sell the bone of a dead animal and benefit from it because life and death do not apply to it. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible to sell it nor benefit from it because it is impure, as it is part of the dead animal. And Ahmad said similar in one narration. And according to Malik, the bone of a dead animal is impure, but not its hair. And likewise, according to us, it is permissible to sell the hide of a dog if it is tanned, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan. And the sale of elephant bone (ivory) is permissible according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. And for us, there is the report that he (peace be upon him) bought for Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her) two bracelets made of ivory (aj), and then the people's use of it appeared without disapproval, until some reported consensus on the permissibility of its sale, and it is the saying of al-Karkhi in his Mukhtasar. And al-Bukhari said in his Jami': Ibn Sirin and Ibn Ibrahim said: there is no harm in trading ivory. And in al-'Uyun, Ibrahim ibn Rustam narrated from Muhammad about a woman who prayed with a necklace around her neck containing a tooth of a dog, lion, or fox, that her prayer is complete because slaughter (tadhkiyah) falls upon it, meaning slaughter purifies it based on the view that the tooth is a nerve ending. And according to the view that it is a bone, then it is not purified because it is from the dead animal, but if it is free of fat, it becomes pure. Unlike a pig if it is slaughtered, its tooth is not purified, contrary to Muhammad, because according to him it is impure by itself like the pig, and according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is like beasts of prey because one can benefit from it in reality. So the elephant is benefited from legally like other beasts of prey, and the sale of a monkey is permissible, and likewise all animals except the pig.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on parts of dead animals. First, the bone of a dead animal (like camel) is permissible to sell and use because death does not make bones impure; they are separate from the flesh. This is the Hanafi view, contrary to Shafi'i who considers all parts of dead animals impure. Malik considers bone impure but not hair. Next, the hide of a dog is permissible to sell if tanned, according to the Hanafi school, contrary to Shafi'i and Hasan. Then, the sale of elephant bone (ivory) is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), but Muhammad disagrees. The proof is that the Prophet bought ivory bracelets for Fatima, indicating its permissibility. Bukhari reports that Ibn Sirin and Ibn Ibrahim saw no harm in trading ivory. The passage also discusses a woman praying with a necklace containing a tooth from a dog, lion, or fox: according to Muhammad, if the animal was slaughtered, the tooth is pure because slaughter purifies it (as the tooth is a nerve ending). But if the tooth is considered a bone, then it is not pure unless free of fat. However, if the animal is a pig, slaughter does not purify its tooth because the pig is inherently impure (najis al-ayn). This is Muhammad's view; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the pig's tooth like that of a beast of prey, not impure itself. Finally, the sale of elephants, monkeys, and all animals except the pig is permissible.

Key Terms
عظم الميتة (ʿaẓm al-maytah): Bone of a dead animal; according to Hanafis, it is not impure and can be sold and used.
عات (ʿāj): Ivory, elephant tusk; its sale is permissible according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling elephant ivory (عاج)?
A) It is not permissible because it comes from a dead animal.
B) It is permissible, supported by the practice of the Prophet and the companions.
C) It is permissible only for making utensils, not for jewelry.
D) It is disliked but not forbidden.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, bones and hair of dead animals are pure, making their sale permissible, unlike the flesh; the pig is an exception.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale and Use of Dead Animal Skin: Tanning and Purification
Ruling on dead animal skin before and after tanning with inter-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling dead animal skin before and after tanning, explain the effect of tanning, and identify the exceptions (human, pig, snake).

Core Idea
Tanning purifies dead animal skin, making it saleable — except human, pig, and snake.

This lesson dives into a classic fiqh dispute: can you sell or use the skin of a dead animal? The Hanafi school holds that tanning transforms the skin into a pure substance, making it permissible to trade. However, other schools like Malik and Ahmad disagree. We'll unpack the evidence and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its pre-tanning ruling
Determine the hukm on selling/using dead animal skin before tanning. The text explicitly states it is not permissible due to inherent impurity and a prophetic prohibition.
The prohibition includes skin, sinews, bones, etc.
2
Understand the effect of tanning
After tanning, the skin becomes pure (tahir) and thus becomes valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that can be sold. This is based on the principle that tanning purifies.
Tanning is a process that removes impurities.
3
Note the exceptions
Human, pig, and snake skins are excluded even after tanning because they are inherently impure or honored by Shariah.
Human skin is respected, pig is najis al-ayn, snake is harmful.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute with Malik
Malik and Ahmad do not permit sale even after tanning. Malik distinguishes between solid and liquid use. Evaluate the proofs: Hanafis rely on hadith implying permission; Malikis argue impurity remains.
The thamarah: Hanafis allow full use; Malikis only solid contact.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi due to strength of proof and widespread practice. Tanning purifies all skins except those explicitly excluded.
This is the relied-upon ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد
والانتفاع به وجلد الميتة قبل الدبغ، وبعده يباع وينتفع به، كعظم الميتة الدبغ أي ولا يجوز الانتفاع وعصبها وصوفها وقرنها إذا لم يكن عليها دسومة أي صوف الشاة = أي الإصلاح أي بجلد الميتة بعد الدبغ كان مما كرمه وعظمه فبيعه ومبادلته بما ليس كذلك إهانة فلا يجوز؛ لإفضائه إلى تحقير ما عظمه الشرع، فليس ذلك من البيع في شيء وإنما هو من وصف المحل شرعا. والانتفاع به أي ولا يجوز الانتفاع بشعر الإنسان؛ لأنه جزء آدمي مكرم، وعن محمد: أنه جوز الانتفاع به؛ لما ورد من أنه عليه السلام قسم شعره بين أصحابه، فكانوا يتبركون به، ولو كان نجساً لما فعل؛ لأنه لا يتبرك بالنجس، ألا ترى أن أبا طيبة حين شرب بوله لقصد التبرك نهاه أن يعود إلى مثل هذا في المستقبل. (عناية، فتح، كافي) وجلد الميتة: أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع جلد الميتة قبل إصلاحها بالشمس أو بطريق آخر؛ للنهي الوارد أنه عليه السلام نهي عن الانتفاع بجلدها وعظمها وعصبها رمز الحقائق: ۳۷/۲] ولأن نجاسته أصلية، فصار كلحم الميتة، بخلاف الثوب النجس والدهن حيث يجوز بيعه؛ لأن نجاسته عارضة، فلا يتغير حكم الثوب بها، وجاز بيع المتنجس، والانتفاع به في غير الأكل، بخلاف الودك. (فتح) وبعده يباع أي يجوز بيع جلد الميتة بعد الدبغ؛ لأنه طهر بالدبغ فصار مالا متقوما وهو محل البيع إلا جلد إنسان وخنزير وحية خلافاً لمالك وأحمد، فإن بيعها عندهما لا يجوز بعد الدبغ أيضاً. وينتفع به أي يجوز الانتفاع بجلد الميتة بعد الدبغ عندنا خلافاً لمالك، وعنده: يجوز استعماله في الجامد، أي لو وضع فيه الشيء الجامد مثل البر جاز، دون المائع أي لو استعمل في المائع كالماء مثلا لا يجوز، وعندنا: المائع والجامد سواء في الاستعمال ويجوز مطلقا رمز الحقائق: ٣٧/٢] (محشي)
كعظم الميتة : أي كما يجوز بيع عظم الميتة والانتفاع به؛ لأن الحياة والممات لا يحلها، وعند الشافعي: لا يجوز بيعها، ولا الانتفاع بها؛ لأنها نجسة؛ لأنها من أجزاء الميتة، وبه قال أحمد في رواية وعند مالك: عظم الميتة نجس دون شعرها، وكذلك يجوز عندنا بيع جلد الكلب إذا كان مدبوغاً خلافاً للشافعي والحسن، ويجوز أيضاً بيع عظم الفيل عند الشيخين، خلافاً لمحمد، ولنا ما روي أنه عليه السلام اشترى لفاطمة رضي الله عنها سوارين من عاج، فظهر استعمال الناس له من غير نكير، حتى حكى بعضهم الإجماع على جواز بيعه وهو قول الكرخي في "مختصره"، وقال البخاري في "جامعه": قال ابن سيرين وابن إبراهيم: لا بأس بتجارة العاج.
وفي "العيون" وروى إبراهيم بن رستم عن محمد في امرأة صلّت وفي عنقها قلادة فيها سن كلب أو أسد أو ثعلب، فصلاتها تامة؛ لأنه يقع عليها الذكاة رمز الحقائق: ۳۷/۲] أي الذكاة طهارة له بناء على أن السن طرف عصب، وأما على القول بأنه عظم فلا، لأنه من الميتة إذا خلا عن الدسومة طهر، بخلاف الخنزير إذا ذكي فإن سنه لا يطهر، خلافاً لمحمد فإنه نجس العين عنده كالخنزير وعندهما بمنزلة السباع؛ لأنه ينتفع به حقيقة، فكان ويجوز بيع الفيل منتفعا به شرعا كسائر السباع وبيع القرد جائز وكذا جميع الحيوانات سوى الخنزير. (فتح)
Chapter of invalid sales. And utilizing it and the skin of a dead animal before tanning, and after it is sold and utilized, like the bone of a dead animal after tanning. Meaning: it is not permissible to utilize its sinews, wool, and horn if there is no grease on them, meaning the sheep's wool. = meaning purification. Meaning: after tanning the skin of a dead animal, if it was something respected and honored by the Law, selling it and exchanging it for something not so is an insult, so it is not permissible; because it leads to contempt of what the Law honored. That is not selling at all but is a description of the subject according to the Law. And utilizing it: meaning it is not permissible to utilize human hair; because it is a part of a respected human. From Muhammad: he permitted utilizing it; due to the report that he (peace be upon him) distributed his hair among his companions, and they sought blessings from it. If it were impure, he would not have done so, because one does not seek blessings from impure things. Do you not see that when Abu Tayyiba drank his urine seeking blessing, he forbade him from returning to such in the future. (Inayah, Fath, Kafi) And the skin of a dead animal: meaning it is also not permissible to sell the skin of a dead animal before purifying it by the sun or another method; due to the prohibition reported that he (peace be upon him) forbade utilizing its skin, bone, and sinews. And because its impurity is inherent, so it becomes like the flesh of a dead animal, unlike a impure garment or oil where selling is permitted; because its impurity is accidental, so the ruling of the garment does not change due to it, and selling of a ritually impure item is permitted, and utilizing it for other than eating, unlike fat. (Fath) And after it is sold: meaning it is permissible to sell the skin of a dead animal after tanning; because it is purified by tanning, so it becomes valuable property which is the subject of sale, except for the skin of a human, pig, and snake, contrary to Malik and Ahmad, for in their view selling them is not permissible even after tanning. And it is utilized: meaning it is permissible to utilize the skin of a dead animal after tanning in our view, contrary to Malik. In his view: it is permissible to use it for solid things, meaning if a solid thing like wheat is placed in it, it is permissible, but not for liquid, meaning if it is used for liquid like water, for example, it is not permissible. In our view: liquid and solid are the same in utilization and it is permissible absolutely. (Hashiyah) Like the bone of a dead animal: meaning just as it is permissible to sell and utilize the bone of a dead animal; because life and death do not inhabit it. In the view of al-Shafi'i: it is not permissible to sell it or utilize it; because it is impure; because it is part of a dead animal. Ahmad also said this in one narration. In the view of Malik: the bone of a dead animal is impure but not its hair. Similarly, in our view, it is permissible to sell the skin of a dog if it is tanned, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan. And it is also permissible to sell the bone of an elephant according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. Our evidence: it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) bought two ivory bracelets for Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her), and the people used it without disapproval, to the extent that some reported consensus on the permissibility of its sale. This is also the saying of al-Karkhi in his 'Mukhtasar'. Al-Bukhari said in his 'Jami': Ibn Sirin and Ibn Ibrahim said: there is no harm in trading ivory. And in 'al-Uyun': Ibrahim ibn Rustam narrated from Muhammad concerning a woman who prayed with a necklace around her neck containing a tooth of a dog, lion, or fox: her prayer is complete; because ritual slaughter has been performed on it. Meaning: ritual slaughter purifies it based on the view that the tooth is a tip of a sinew. As for the view that it is bone, then no, because it is from a dead animal; but if it is free of grease, it is purified. Contrary to a pig if slaughtered, its tooth is not purified, contrary to Muhammad, for in his view it is impure in itself like the pig. In the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is like predatory beasts; because it is actually utilized, so the elephant may be sold as it is utilized legally like other predatory beasts. And the sale of a monkey is permissible, and likewise all animals except the pig. (Fath)
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on invalid sales discusses the rulings on selling and benefiting from the skin and bones of dead animals. The author, building on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains that the skin of a dead animal is impure and cannot be sold or used before tanning. After tanning, it becomes pure and can be sold and used, except for the skin of humans, pigs, and snakes. This is the Hanafi position, contra Malik and Ahmad who do not permit sale even after tanning. Malik further distinguishes between solid and liquid use. The text also discusses bones: Hanafis permit sale and use because impurity does not inhabit bones. Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad hold them impure. Malik distinguishes bone from hair. The author also mentions the permissibility of selling tanned dog skin and elephant bone (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad disagrees), citing the Prophet's purchase of ivory bracelets for Fatima. The discussion on a necklace containing a predator's tooth in prayer is also included: if the animal was slaughtered, the tooth is pure because it is considered sinew, not bone. This shows that Hanafis differentiate between sinew and bone. The passage presents multiple proofs: hadith, consensus, practicality, and qiyas. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) for the Hanafi school is that tanning purifies the skin of anything except human, pig, and snake, and that bones and sinews are pure and saleable.

Key Terms
الدبغ (al-dibgh): Tanning: purification of animal skin by chemical or natural process.
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal not slaughtered according to Islamic law; its flesh is impure and unlawful to eat.
الجامد والمائع (al-jāmid wa al-mā'i'): Solid and liquid: distinguishing between use of tanned skin for solids vs. liquids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following skins becomes permissible to sell after tanning?
A) Skin of a pig
B) Skin of a dead cow that died naturally
C) Skin of a human
D) Skin of a snake

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies dead animal skin, making it saleable and usable, except for human, pig, and snake skins.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ruling on Bones and Other Parts of Dead Animals
Rulings on bones, sinews, wool, horn, human hair, dog skin, elephant bone, and tooth case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling dead animal bones, sinews, wool, horn, human hair, and elephant bone, and identify the relevant inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Bones of dead animals are pure and saleable; even elephant bone is permissible per the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Beyond the skin, the classical texts also address other parts of a dead animal: bones, sinews, wool, horn, and even human hair. These rulings involve inter-madhhab disagreements with Shafi‘i and intra-Hanafi differences among the three imams. This lesson will help you sort through these rulings and understand the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on bones
According to the Hanafi school, bones of a dead animal are pure (tahir) and can be sold and used, because impurity does not inhabit bones. This contradicts Shafi‘i and Ahmad who deem them impure.
Bones are distinct from flesh; life and death do not affect them.
2
Examine other parts: sinews, wool, horn
These parts are also permissible to sell and use unless they have grease (dusuma) on them. If greasy, they are impure and cannot be sold before cleaning.
The condition is that they be free of grease.
3
Analyze the dispute on human hair
Majority of Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) forbid using human hair because it is part of a respected human. Muhammad permits it based on the Prophet distributing his hair.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the ruling on dog skin and elephant bone
Dog skin, if tanned, is permissible to sell in the Hanafi school (contra Shafi‘i). Elephant bone (ivory) is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad disagrees. Evidence includes the Prophet buying ivory bracelets and established practice.
Muhammad’s view is marjuh (weaker) here.
5
Apply the principle to the tooth case
A tooth of a slaughtered predator is considered sinew (not bone) and thus pure if the animal was slaughtered. If considered bone, it is pure only if free of grease.
This shows the practical application in purity for prayer.

(Fath) And after it is sold: meaning it is permissible to sell the skin of a dead animal after tanning; because it is purified by tanning, so it becomes valuable property which is the subject of sale, except for the skin of a human, pig, and snake, contrary to Malik and Ahmad, for in their view selling them is not permissible even after tanning. And it is utilized: meaning it is permissible to utilize the skin of a dead animal after tanning in our view, contrary to Malik. In his view: it is permissible to use it for solid things, meaning if a solid thing like wheat is placed in it, it is permissible, but not for liquid, meaning if it is used for liquid like water, for example, it is not permissible. In our view: liquid and solid are the same in utilization and it is permissible absolutely. (Hashiyah) Like the bone of a dead animal: meaning just as it is permissible to sell and utilize the bone of a dead animal; because life and death do not inhabit it. In the view of al-Shafi'i: it is not permissible to sell it or utilize it; because it is impure; because it is part of a dead animal. Ahmad also said this in one narration. In the view of Malik: the bone of a dead animal is impure but not its hair. Similarly, in our view, it is permissible to sell the skin of a dog if it is tanned, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan. And it is also permissible to sell the bone of an elephant according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. Our evidence: it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) bought two ivory bracelets for Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her), and the people used it without disapproval, to the extent that some reported consensus on the permissibility of its sale. This is also the saying of al-Karkhi in his 'Mukhtasar'. Al-Bukhari said in his 'Jami': Ibn Sirin and Ibn Ibrahim said: there is no harm in trading ivory. And in 'al-Uyun': Ibrahim ibn Rustam narrated from Muhammad concerning a woman who prayed with a necklace around her neck containing a tooth of a dog, lion, or fox: her prayer is complete; because ritual slaughter has been performed on it. Meaning: ritual slaughter purifies it based on the view that the tooth is a tip of a sinew. As for the view that it is bone, then no, because it is from a dead animal; but if it is free of grease, it is purified. Contrary to a pig if slaughtered, its tooth is not purified, contrary to Muhammad, for in his view it is impure in itself like the pig. In the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is like predatory beasts; because it is actually utilized, so the elephant may be sold as it is utilized legally like other predatory beasts. And the sale of a monkey is permissible, and likewise all animals except the pig. (Fath)
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on invalid sales discusses the rulings on selling and benefiting from the skin and bones of dead animals. The author, building on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains that the skin of a dead animal is impure and cannot be sold or used before tanning. After tanning, it becomes pure and can be sold and used, except for the skin of humans, pigs, and snakes. This is the Hanafi position, contra Malik and Ahmad who do not permit sale even after tanning. Malik further distinguishes between solid and liquid use. The text also discusses bones: Hanafis permit sale and use because impurity does not inhabit bones. Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad hold them impure. Malik distinguishes bone from hair. The author also mentions the permissibility of selling tanned dog skin and elephant bone (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad disagrees), citing the Prophet's purchase of ivory bracelets for Fatima. The discussion on a necklace containing a predator's tooth in prayer is also included: if the animal was slaughtered, the tooth is pure because it is considered sinew, not bone. This shows that Hanafis differentiate between sinew and bone. The passage presents multiple proofs: hadith, consensus, practicality, and qiyas. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) for the Hanafi school is that tanning purifies the skin of anything except human, pig, and snake, and that bones and sinews are pure and saleable.

Key Terms
العصب (al-'asab): Sinews/tendons of an animal.
الصوف (al-suf): Wool.
القرن (al-qarn): Horn.
العاج (al-'aj): Ivory (elephant tusk).
السن (al-sinn): Tooth, either considered bone or sinew.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT permissible to sell according to the Hanafi school, based on the passage?
A) Bone of a dead cow
B) Tanned dog skin
C) Elephant tusk (ivory)
D) Greasy wool from a dead sheep

Key Takeaway
Bones and non-greasy parts of dead animals are pure and saleable; elephant bone is permissible per the majority Hanafi position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Selling Air Rights and Water Channels
Ruling on sale of intangible rights and water channels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which intangible rights are considered property (mal) in Hanafi law and determine the validity of selling air rights, water channels, water rights, and rights of passage.

Core Idea
The right to build above another's property is not saleable because it is not property; only tangible assets or shares of water qualify.

In Hanafi sales law, the subject matter must be property (mal). This lesson examines three borderline cases: air rights (al-uluww) after building collapse, water channels (al-masil), and rights of passage (haqq al-murur). We will see how the Hanafi school distinguishes between rights that are mere licenses (not property) and shares of tangible commodities like water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
List the three sale scenarios discussed: (a) selling the empty airspace after a building collapse, (b) selling a water channel, (c) selling a road or right of passage.
2
Determine property status
For each case, assess if the subject qualifies as mal. Air rights (haqq al-ta'liya) are not mal because they are intangible. A water channel (masil) is unknown in quantity (jahala), hence not a valid subject. A road (tariq) is tangible and can be possessed. A right of passage is a right, not a tangible asset.
3
Apply the mal requirement
The general rule: sale requires a subject that is mal. Therefore, selling air rights alone is batil (invalid), selling a water channel is batil, but selling a road is valid. The right of passage is valid only when sold together with the land (as part of the property).
4
Contrast with water rights (shirb)
The right to a share of water (shirb) is considered property because it is a tangible share of water. Hence, it can be sold with the land by consensus, and even independently according to some Hanafi scholars (choice of Balkh).
This shows that not all rights are equal: water share is quantifiable and hence mal.
5
Note intra-Hanafi differences
On selling the right of passage alone: Ibn Sam'ah narrates it is valid, but al-Ziyadat (relied-upon) says it is invalid. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that rights independently are not saleable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووبرها: أي الإبل ويجوز الانتفاع بهذه الأشياء أيضاً وكذا بشعرها. (ط، عيني) وعلو سقط : [ أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع علو؛ لأن حق التعلي ليس بمال ومحل البيع مال (عيني)] علو الدار بالضم والكسر ضد السفل، أي إذا كان علو لأحد وسفل لآخر فسقطا أو سقط العلو وبقي السفل فباع صاحب العلو موضع العلو لم يجز، فإن سقط العلو بعد البيع قبل القبض بطل البيع؛ لأن له حق التعلي لا غير وهو ليس بمال ومحل البيع المال، وهو ما يمكن إحرازه، وإنما يصح بيعه قبل الانهدام باعتبار البناء القائم ولم يبق، بخلاف الشرب الذي من الماء حيث يصح بيعه تبعاً للأرض باتفاق الروايات، ومقصوداً في رواية، وهو اختيار مشايخ بلخ؛ لأنه نصيب من الماء وهو مال، ولهذا يضمن بالإتلاف، حتى لو سقى به رجل أرضه يضمن قيمته رمز الحقائق: ۳۷/۲] (فتح، شرح الوقاية) والمسيل: أي لا يجوز بيع مسيل الماء ولا هبته؛ لأن المسيل مجهول؛ إذ لا يعلم قدر ما يشغله الماء، ويجوز بيع الطريق وهبته سواء حد أو لا، وإذا لم يبين يقدر بعرض باب الدار العظمى، وصح بيع حق المرور تبعاً للأرض بالإجماع، ووحده في رواية ابن سماعة، وفي رواية "الزيادات": لا يجوز؛ لأنه حق من الحقوق، وبيع الحقوق بالانفراد لا يجوز. (عيني، فتح)
And its wool: meaning camels, and it is permissible to utilize these things as well, and likewise their hair. (T, Ayni) And the upper part of a collapsed building: meaning it is also not permissible to sell the upper part; because the right to build above is not property and the subject of sale is property (Ayni). The upper part of a house, with damm or kasr, is the opposite of the lower part. Meaning if the upper part belongs to one and the lower to another, and they collapse, or the upper collapses and the lower remains, and the owner of the upper sells the place of the upper, it is not permissible. If the upper collapses after the sale but before delivery, the sale becomes void; because he has only the right to build above, which is not property, and the subject of sale is property, which is something that can be possessed. Its sale before collapse was valid only by virtue of the existing building that is no longer present. This is unlike the right to water (shirb), which can be sold as part of the land by agreement of all narrations, and even independently in one narration, which is the choice of the scholars of Balkh; because it is a share of water which is property, and for this reason one is liable for destroying it. So if a man irrigates his land with it, he must pay its value. (Fath, Sharh al-Wiqayah) And the water channel: meaning it is not permissible to sell a water channel or gift it; because the channel is unknown; since the amount of water it carries is not known. It is permissible to sell a road and gift it whether it is defined or not, and if not specified, it is estimated by the width of the largest door of the house. The sale of the right of passage (haqq al-murur) is valid as part of the land by consensus, but alone it is not valid according to one narration from Ibn Sam'ah, and in the narration of 'al-Ziyadat': it is not permissible; because it is a right from among rights, and selling rights independently is not permissible. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of air rights (al-ʿuluww) and water channels (al-masīl). The author explains that if the upper story of a house is separated from the lower, and the building collapses, the owner of the upper part cannot sell the empty airspace because it is a mere right to build (haqq al-taʿliya), not tangible property. The sale of the building before collapse was valid because the structure existed, but after collapse the right alone is not saleable. This is contrasted with the right to water (shirb), which is considered property (māl) because it is a share of water, and thus can be sold independently according to some Hanafi scholars of Balkh. Regarding the water channel (masīl), sale is invalid due to uncertainty (jahāla) about the quantity of water. However, selling a road (ṭarīq) is permissible, and the right of passage (haqq al-murūr) can be sold with the land. But selling such rights alone is not permitted according to the relied-upon narration from al-Ziyādāt, as rights (ḥuqūq) are not considered property for independent sale.

Key Terms
حق التعلي (haqq al-taʿliya): Right to build or place a structure above another's property.
الشرب (al-shirb): Right to a share of water, typically for irrigation.
المسيل (al-masīl): Water channel or course for water flow.
حق المرور (haqq al-murūr): Right of passage over another's land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of the upper part of a collapsed building (al-ʿuluww) invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the building no longer exists
B) Because the right to build above is not considered property
C) Because the seller does not own it
D) Because the buyer does not accept

Key Takeaway
Intangible rights that are not considered property cannot be sold independently; only tangible assets or quantified shares of commodities like water are saleable.
